Spending Without Court Order: The Voluntary Spender
Ruling on multiple categories of spenders without authorization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a person spending on another's property becomes a mutatawwi' and state the legal consequence.

Core Idea
Spending on another's property without court order or owner's permission makes you a volunteer — no right to reimbursement.

This lesson addresses a common scenario where a partner, depositary, finder, or co-owner spends on shared or entrusted property without a qadi's order. The Hanafi rule is clear: such a spender is a mutatawwi' (volunteer) and cannot claim back what was spent, even if the property later goes to someone else.

Guided Path
1
Identify the spender and the property
First, determine who is spending and on what type of property: partner on a slave, depositary on a deposit, finder on a found object, co-owner on a shared house.
The categories are listed in al-Khulasa.
2
Check for authorization
Ask: Did the spender have permission from the owner or a court order? If not, he becomes a mutatawwi'.
Absence of both permission and court order triggers the rule.
3
Apply the consequence
The spender cannot recover the expenditure. He is considered to have done it voluntarily.
No claim for reimbursement, unlike if he had court authorization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخلاصة : الشريك إذا أنفق على العبد في غيبة شريكه بغير إذن القاضي وبغير إذن صاحبه، وكذا النخل والزرع، وكذا المودع والملتقط إذا أنفق على الوديعة واللقطة، وكذا في الدار المشتركة إذا اشتريت فأنفق أحدهما بغير إذن صاحبه وبغير إذن أمر القاضي فهو متطوع وفي القنية : ونفقة المبيع على البائع ما دام في يده هو الصحيح ثم رقم برقم آخر أنه يرفع البائع الأمر إلى الحاكم فيأذن له في بيعه أو إجارته، ثم رقم بأن نفقة العبد المبيع بشرط الخيار على من له الملك في العبد وقت الوجوب، وقيل على البائع ، وقيل يستدان فيرجع على من يصير له الملك كصدقة الفطر اهـ . وفي وجوب نفقة البيع على البائع قبل تسليمه إشكال لأنه لا ملك له لا رقبة ولا منفعة فينبغي أن تكون على المشتري وتكون تابعة للملك كالمرهون كما بحثه بعضهم . كما في القنية أيضاً. وشمل كلام المصنف أيضاً المملوك ظاهراً فلو شهدا عليه بحرية أمته فوضعها القاضي على يد عدل لأجل المسألة على الشهود فالنفقة على من هي في يده، سواء ادعت الأمة الحرية أو جحدت لوجوب نفقة المملوك على مولاه وإن كان ممنوعاً منه، ولا رجوع للمولى بما أنفقه، سواء زكيت الشهود أو لا إلا إذا أجبره القاضي على الإنفاق أو أكلت في بيته بغير إذنه فيرجع بما أنفقه لأنه تبين أن لا ملك له. وإن كان عبداً أمره أن يكتسب وينفق على نفسه إن كان قادراً عليه وإلا فعلى المدعى عليه، وتمامه في الذخيرة.
In al-Khulasa: The partner if he spends on the slave in the absence of his partner without the qadi's permission and without the permission of his owner, similarly for date-palms and crops, similarly the depositary and the finder if they spend on the deposit and found object, similarly in the shared house if bought and one of them spends without the permission of his partner and without the order of the qadi, he is a volunteer. And in al-Qunyah: The maintenance of the sold item is on the seller as long as it remains in his hand – this is the correct view. Then with another annotation that the seller raises the matter to the judge so he permits him to sell it or lease it. Then annotated that the maintenance of a slave sold with a condition of option is on the one who has ownership at the time the maintenance becomes due; and it is said on the seller; and it is said that he borrows and then recovers from whomever the ownership goes to, like sadaqat al-fitr. End. And there is a difficulty in the obligation of the maintenance of the sale being on the seller before delivery because he has no ownership, neither of the corpus nor of the benefit, so it should be on the buyer and be attached to ownership like a mortgage, as some have researched. As in al-Qunyah as well. And the word of the author also includes the apparent slave: if two witnesses testify to the freedom of his female slave, and the qadi places her in the hand of a trustworthy person for the sake of the claim against the witnesses, then the maintenance is on the one in whose hand she is, whether the slave claims freedom or denies it, because the maintenance of a slave is obligatory on the master even if he is prevented from it. And the master cannot recover what he spent, whether the witnesses are proven upright or not, unless the qadi forced him to spend or she ate in his house without his permission, then he recovers what he spent because it became clear that he has no ownership. And if it is a male slave, he orders him to earn and spend on himself if he is able, otherwise upon the defendant. And its completion is in al-Dhakhira.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This section continues the expansion of the matn, drawing from al-Khulasa and al-Qunyah. It first addresses cases where a partner, depositary, finder, or co-owner spends without court order: they are volunteers (mutatawwi') and cannot claim reimbursement. Then it discusses the sold slave who is still in the seller's hands: the correct view is that maintenance is on the seller. For a slave sold with a condition of option, there are three views: on the one who owned at the time maintenance became due, on the seller, or that he borrows and recovers from the eventual owner. The author notes a difficulty: before delivery, the seller has no ownership, so logically maintenance should be on the buyer, like a mortgage. Finally, it covers a case where witnesses testify that a slave is free: the slave is placed in a trustee's hands; maintenance is on the one in whose hand she is (the trustee), not the master, because the master is prevented from using her. If the master spends under court order or she eats in his house without permission, he can recover expenses because it becomes clear he no longer owns her. If the slave is male, he is ordered to earn his own upkeep.

Key Terms
متطوع (mutatawwi'): One who performs an act without legal obligation and cannot claim compensation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Khulasa, which of the following spenders is NOT a mutatawwi'?
A) Partner spending without partner's permission and without court order
B) Depositary spending with court order
C) Finder spending without court order or owner's permission
D) Co-owner spending without co-owner's permission and without court order

Key Takeaway
Without court order or owner's permission, any spending on another's property is voluntary and gives no right to reimbursement.

Maintenance of a Slave Sold with Option
Expansion on maintenance during a conditional sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi opinions on who bears maintenance of a slave sold with a condition of option and identify the preferred view.

Core Idea
For a slave sold with option, maintenance depends on ownership at the time it becomes due, with three scholarly opinions.

This lesson examines the maintenance of a slave during the option period in a conditional sale. The Hanafi school has three views, reflecting different ways to link maintenance to ownership. The last view, analogizing to sadaqat al-fitr, appears to be the most practical.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sale type
The slave is sold with a condition of option (khiyar). Ownership may be suspended or transferred subject to option.
The text mentions 'bay' bi shart al-khiyar'.
2
List the three opinions
From al-Qunyah: 1) On the one who owns the slave when maintenance becomes due. 2) On the seller. 3) The spender borrows and recovers from the eventual owner.
The third is compared to sadaqat al-fitr.
3
Evaluate the strength
The first view is the 'correct' one? The text says 'huwa al-sahih' for the seller before delivery, but for option, it presents multiple views. Later, it mentions a difficulty: seller has no ownership before delivery, so maintenance should be on the buyer like a mortgage.
The final opinion (borrow and recover) is presented as a researched view (kama bahathahu ba'duhum).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخلاصة : الشريك إذا أنفق على العبد في غيبة شريكه بغير إذن القاضي وبغير إذن صاحبه، وكذا النخل والزرع، وكذا المودع والملتقط إذا أنفق على الوديعة واللقطة، وكذا في الدار المشتركة إذا اشتريت فأنفق أحدهما بغير إذن صاحبه وبغير إذن أمر القاضي فهو متطوع وفي القنية : ونفقة المبيع على البائع ما دام في يده هو الصحيح ثم رقم برقم آخر أنه يرفع البائع الأمر إلى الحاكم فيأذن له في بيعه أو إجارته، ثم رقم بأن نفقة العبد المبيع بشرط الخيار على من له الملك في العبد وقت الوجوب، وقيل على البائع ، وقيل يستدان فيرجع على من يصير له الملك كصدقة الفطر اهـ . وفي وجوب نفقة البيع على البائع قبل تسليمه إشكال لأنه لا ملك له لا رقبة ولا منفعة فينبغي أن تكون على المشتري وتكون تابعة للملك كالمرهون كما بحثه بعضهم . كما في القنية أيضاً. وشمل كلام المصنف أيضاً المملوك ظاهراً فلو شهدا عليه بحرية أمته فوضعها القاضي على يد عدل لأجل المسألة على الشهود فالنفقة على من هي في يده، سواء ادعت الأمة الحرية أو جحدت لوجوب نفقة المملوك على مولاه وإن كان ممنوعاً منه، ولا رجوع للمولى بما أنفقه، سواء زكيت الشهود أو لا إلا إذا أجبره القاضي على الإنفاق أو أكلت في بيته بغير إذنه فيرجع بما أنفقه لأنه تبين أن لا ملك له. وإن كان عبداً أمره أن يكتسب وينفق على نفسه إن كان قادراً عليه وإلا فعلى المدعى عليه، وتمامه في الذخيرة.
In al-Khulasa: The partner if he spends on the slave in the absence of his partner without the qadi's permission and without the permission of his owner, similarly for date-palms and crops, similarly the depositary and the finder if they spend on the deposit and found object, similarly in the shared house if bought and one of them spends without the permission of his partner and without the order of the qadi, he is a volunteer. And in al-Qunyah: The maintenance of the sold item is on the seller as long as it remains in his hand – this is the correct view. Then with another annotation that the seller raises the matter to the judge so he permits him to sell it or lease it. Then annotated that the maintenance of a slave sold with a condition of option is on the one who has ownership at the time the maintenance becomes due; and it is said on the seller; and it is said that he borrows and then recovers from whomever the ownership goes to, like sadaqat al-fitr. End. And there is a difficulty in the obligation of the maintenance of the sale being on the seller before delivery because he has no ownership, neither of the corpus nor of the benefit, so it should be on the buyer and be attached to ownership like a mortgage, as some have researched. As in al-Qunyah as well. And the word of the author also includes the apparent slave: if two witnesses testify to the freedom of his female slave, and the qadi places her in the hand of a trustworthy person for the sake of the claim against the witnesses, then the maintenance is on the one in whose hand she is, whether the slave claims freedom or denies it, because the maintenance of a slave is obligatory on the master even if he is prevented from it. Commentary
This section continues the expansion of the matn, drawing from al-Khulasa and al-Qunyah. It first addresses cases where a partner, depositary, finder, or co-owner spends without court order: they are volunteers (mutatawwi') and cannot claim reimbursement. Then it discusses the sold slave who is still in the seller's hands: the correct view is that maintenance is on the seller. If the master spends under court order or she eats in his house without permission, he can recover expenses because it becomes clear he no longer owns her. If the slave is male, he is ordered to earn his own upkeep.

Key Terms
بيع بشرط الخيار (bay' bi shart al-khiyar): A sale with a condition of option (option to rescind)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in al-Qunyah, what should the person who spends on the slave's maintenance during the option period do?
A) He cannot recover the money
B) He should borrow and then recover from the eventual owner
C) He should ask the qadi for permission
D) He must pay half and the seller pays half

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school offers multiple solutions for maintenance during a conditional sale; the most practical is to borrow and recover from the ultimate owner.

Maintenance of a Slave Under Freedom Dispute
Ruling on maintenance during a freedom dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine who bears maintenance of a slave when witnesses testify to freedom and the slave is placed in a trustee's hands.

Core Idea
When a slave's freedom is claimed, the master is still liable for maintenance if the slave is in his custody, but if the slave is placed in a trustee's hands, the trustee bears maintenance.

This lesson addresses a scenario where witnesses testify that a female slave is free, leading the qadi to place her in the custody of a trustee. The key question is: who pays for her maintenance during the dispute? The Hanafi ruling draws a distinction based on possession and control.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial status
The slave is 'apparently' owned (mamluk zahiran). Witnesses then testify she is free.
The qadi places her in the hand of an adil (trustee).
2
Determine the duty of maintenance
Maintenance is on the one in whose hand she is placed (the trustee), whether she claims freedom or denies it.
This is because she is still a slave in appearance until proven otherwise, but the master is prevented from using her.
3
Master's right to reimbursement
The master cannot recover what he spent, even if witnesses are later proven upright, unless the qadi forced him to spend or she ate without permission.
If forced, he can recover because it becomes clear he was not the owner.
4
Case of a male slave
If the slave is male, he is ordered to earn and spend on himself if able; otherwise, on the defendant (the person who claimed he is a slave).
The defendant is the one who asserted ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخلاصة : الشريك إذا أنفق على العبد في غيبة شريكه بغير إذن القاضي وبغير إذن صاحبه، وكذا النخل والزرع، وكذا المودع والملتقط إذا أنفق على الوديعة واللقطة، وكذا في الدار المشتركة إذا اشتريت فأنفق أحدهما بغير إذن صاحبه وبغير إذن أمر القاضي فهو متطوع وفي القنية : ونفقة المبيع على البائع ما دام في يده هو الصحيح ثم رقم برقم آخر أنه يرفع البائع الأمر إلى الحاكم فيأذن له في بيعه أو إجارته، ثم رقم بأن نفقة العبد المبيع بشرط الخيار على من له الملك في العبد وقت الوجوب، وقيل على البائع ، وقيل يستدان فيرجع على من يصير له الملك كصدقة الفطر اهـ . وفي وجوب نفقة البيع على البائع قبل تسليمه إشكال لأنه لا ملك له لا رقبة ولا منفعة فينبغي أن تكون على المشتري وتكون تابعة للملك كالمرهون كما بحثه بعضهم . كما في القنية أيضاً. وشمل كلام المصنف أيضاً المملوك ظاهراً فلو شهدا عليه بحرية أمته فوضعها القاضي على يد عدل لأجل المسألة على الشهود فالنفقة على من هي في يده، سواء ادعت الأمة الحرية أو جحدت لوجوب نفقة المملوك على مولاه وإن كان ممنوعاً منه، ولا رجوع للمولى بما أنفقه، سواء زكيت الشهود أو لا إلا إذا أجبره القاضي على الإنفاق أو أكلت في بيته بغير إذنه فيرجع بما أنفقه لأنه تبين أن لا ملك له. وإن كان عبداً أمره أن يكتسب وينفق على نفسه إن كان قادراً عليه وإلا فعلى المدعى عليه، وتمامه في الذخيرة.

Key Terms
عدل (adl): Trustworthy person appointed by the qadi to hold property or a slave during a dispute

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When can the master of a female slave recover maintenance spent during a freedom dispute?
A) When the witnesses are proven upright
B) When the qadi forced him to spend
C) When the slave claims freedom
D) Never

Key Takeaway
When a slave is in trusteeship pending a freedom claim, the trustee bears maintenance; the master cannot recover voluntary spending unless forced by the qadi.

The Divisibility of Tadbir: Imam vs. Sahibayn
Exposition of intra-Hanafi dispute on divisibility of tadbir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on divisibility of tadbir and identify the thamarah al-khilaf in a co-ownership scenario.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa holds tadbir divisible; the Sahibayn hold it indivisible, leading to different ownership and compensation outcomes.

When two co-owners of a slave perform different acts—one makes him mudabbar, the other emancipates—the validity of each act depends on a foundational dispute within the Hanafi school: Is tadbir divisible? This lesson unpacks the two positions and their practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Identify the basic case: two co-owners, one makes the slave mudabbar (A), the other emancipates (B). Under Abu Hanifa, both acts are valid on each owner's share. Under the Sahibayn, the first tadbir overrides later emancipation.
2
Trace the dispute's roots
Understand that the dispute hinges on whether tadbir is divisible. Abu Hanifa analogizes to 'itq (emancipation) which is divisible; the Sahibayn see tadbir as a single contingency that cannot be split.
The Sahibayn's view is based on the unity of the mudabbar's status.
3
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the emancipator's share becomes free immediately, and the mudabbar's share remains contingent. Under the Sahibayn, the entire slave remains mudabbar, and the emancipator must compensate the mudabbar.
The compensation amount and whether the slave can be 'used' differ.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The text defends Abu Hanifa's view by arguing that immediate emancipation is an executed act that cannot be overridden by a contingent tadbir. Also, Qadikhan's reasoning that partitioning acts on shares prevents harm.
The passage notes that the silent partner may seek istis'a' if the mudabbar is poor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على العبد، وكذا لو صدر الإعتاق والتدبير منهما معاً. وهذا كله عند الإمام. وعندهما: المعتق أولى في الكل فإن كان المعتق موسراً ضمن للمدبر وإلا سعى العبد له في نصيبه؛ كذا في المحيط. وذكر قاضيخان في شرح الجامع الصغير أن قولنا للشريك هذه الخيارات أنه يصح منه هذه التصرفات، أما لا يؤذن بالإعتاق والاستسعاء لأن فيه إفساد نصيب المدبر لأن المدبر كان متمكناً من استسعاء نصيبه على ملكه إلى وقت الموت وبعد الإعتاق والاستسعاء لا يتمكن اهـ. وفي الهداية وقيمة المدبر ثلثا قيمته قناً على ما قالوا فلو كانت قيمته قناً سبعة وعشرين ديناراً ضمن له ستة دنانير لأن ثلثيها وهو قيمة المدبر ثمانية عشر وثلثها وهو المضمون ستة، والمدبر يضمن للساكت تسعة. وإنما كان كذلك لأن الانتفاع بالوطء والسعاية والبدل وإنما زال الأخير فقط وإليه مال الصدر الشهيد وعليه الفتوى إلا أن الوجه المذكور يخص المدبرة دون المدبر . وقيل : يسئل أهل الخبرة أن العلماء لو جوزوا بيع هذا فاتت المنفعة المذكورة كم يبلغ فما ذكر فهو قيمته وهذا أحسن عندي ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وجوابه أن الاستخدام هو المنظور إليه الشامل للعبد والجارية والوطء من الاستخدام فالباقي في المدبر شيئان الاستخدام والسعاية والفائت البدل وهذا المعنى يشمل العبد والجارية فلذا كان المفتى به ما في الهداية. وأما قيمة أم الولد والمكاتب فسيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى. وقالا : العبد للذي دبره أول مرة ويضمن ثلثي قيمته لشريكه موسراً كان أو معسراً بناء على أن التدبير لا يتجزئ عندهما. ولم يذكر المصنف أن للساكت الاستسعاء لظهوره لأن له أن يستسعي في ثلث قيمته وللمدبر أن يستسعي العبد في ثلث قيمته مدبراً إذا اختار عدم تضمين المعتق كما في غاية البيان، ولم يذكر الولاء قال في الهداية والولاء بين المعتق والمدبر أثلاثاً؛ ثلثاه للمدبر والثلث للمعتق لأن العبد عتق على ملكهما على هذا المقدار اهـ. ومراده أنه المدبر والمعتق لأن العتق لا يثبت للمدبر إلا بعد موت مولاه كما في غاية البيان والنهاية . وفي فتح القدير : وهو غلط لأن العتق المنجز يوجب إخراجه إلى الحرية بتنجيز أحد الأمور التضمين مع اليسار والسعاية والعتق حتى منع استخدام المدبر إياه من حين وجوده كما لو أعتق أحد الشريكين ابتداء ودبره الآخر الساكت فإنه لا تتأخر حرية باقية إلى موته كما قدمناه أول الباب إلى آخره . من العبد بين عصبة وقيد المصنف باليسار لأن المدبر لو كان معسراً فللساكت الاستسعاء دون التضمين، وكذا المعتق لو كان معسراً فللمدبر الاستسعاء دون تضمين المعتق؛ كذا في غاية البيان. وبهذا علم أن تقييد المصنف بيسار الثلاثة ليس بقيد لأن الاعتبار ليسار المدبر والمعتق، وأما الساكت يقال للمدبر أن يضمن المعتق نصفه مدبراً وثلثه قناً. وقوله ولو كان حرره يشهد الخ» يشهد للتصويب . ما ضمن ولو قال لشريكه هي أم ولدك وأنكر تخدمه يوماً وتتوقف يوماً وما لأم ولد هو فلا اعتبار بحاله من اليسار والإعسار ولم يذكر المصنف رجوع المدبر بما ضمنه للساكت على العبد وقد نص الحاكم الشهيد في الكافي بأنه يرجع على العبد بثلث قيمته قناً كما ضمن . وقيد المصنف بكون الساكت اختار تضمين المدبر بعد تحرير الآخر لأنه لو اختار تضمين المدبر . قبل أن يعتقه الآخر ثم أعتقه كان للمدبر أن يضمن المعتق ثلثي قيمته لأن الإعتاق وجد بعد تملك المدبر نصيب الساكت فله أن يضمنه ثلث قيمته قناً ، ثلث قيمته مدبراً كما صفته . قال في فتح القدير : وأورد بعض الطلبة على هذا أنه ينبغي أن يضمنه قيمة ثلثيه مدبراً لأنه حين ملك ثلث الساكت بالضمان صار مديراً لا قناً، ولذا قلنا في وجه كون ثلثي الولاء له لأنه صار كأنه دبر ثلثيه ابتداء، والجواب لا يتم إلا بمنع كون الثلث الذي ملكه بالضمان للساكت صار مدبراً بل هو قن على ملكه إذ لا موجب لصيرورته مدبراً لأن ظهور الملك الآن لا يوجبه والتدبير يتجزئ وذكرهم إياه في وجه كون ثلثي الولاء له غير محتاج إليه إذ يكفي فيه أنه على ملكه حين أعتقه الآخر وأدى الضمان وإنما لم يكن ولاؤه له لما ذكرنا من أنه ضمان جناية لا تملك ا هـ. وبما قررناه أولاً علم أن الواو في قول المصنف «وحرره آخر» بمعنى ثم قيد به لأنه لو أعتقه أحدهم ودبره الآخر وكاتب الآخر ولا يعلم الأول فالتصرفات كلها جائزة ويسعى العبد للمدبر في سدس قيمته وضمن له المعتق أيضاً سدس قيمته مدبراً إن كان موسراً، ويسعى العبد في المكاتبة للثالث، فإن عجز فهو بالخيار، إن شاء استسعى العبد في ثلث قيمته والولاء أثلاثاً، وإن شاء ضمن المدبر المعتق ثلث قيمته نصفين إذا كانا موسرين والولاء بينهما نصفان لأنهما لما جهلا التاريخ يجعل كأن هذه التصرفات وقعن معاً، وأنها متجزئه عند أبي حنيفة فصحت . ثم لا شيء للمعتق على أحد، وإن أعتق واحد وكاتب الآخر ودبر الثالث معاً ليس لواحد الرجوع لأن تصرف كل واحد حصل في ملك نفسه، وإن دبر أحدهم أولاً ثم أعتق الثاني ثم كاتب الآخر ثبت للمدبر الرجوع على المعتق بقيمة نصيبه ولا رجوع للمكاتب على أحد، فإن دبر ثم كاتب ثم أعتق فحكم المدبر والمعتق ما ذكرنا وأما المكاتب إذا عجز العبد يرجع على المعتق بقيمة نصيبه لأنه عاد عبداً له والمعتق أتلفه، وإن كاتبه أولاً ثم دبر ثم أعتق، فإن لم يعجز العبد يعتق عليه ولا ضمان له على أحد، وإن عجز يرجع على المدبر بثلث قيمته لا على المعتق وتمام تفريعاته في المحيط .
Upon the slave, and likewise if emancipation and tadbir occur from both of them together. All this according to Imam Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): the emancipator has precedence in all; if the emancipator is wealthy, he guarantees to the mudabbar, otherwise the slave seeks work for his share. So in al-Muhit. Qadikhan mentioned in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir that our statement to the partner that he has these options means that these acts are valid from him, but he is not permitted to emancipate and seek work because that spoils the mudabbar's share, since the mudabbar was able to seek work from his share while it was his property until death, and after emancipation and seeking work he cannot. In al-Hidaya: the value of the mudabbar is two-thirds of his value as a slave, as they said. So if his value as a slave was 27 dinars, he guarantees 6 dinars because two-thirds of it, which is the value of the mudabbar, is 18, and one-third of it, which is guaranteed, is 6, and the mudabbar guarantees 9 to the silent one. That is because the benefit through intercourse, seeking work, and the substitute; only the last is lost, and al-Sadr al-Shahid inclined to this, and upon it is the fatwa, except that the mentioned face is specific to the female mudabbara, not the male mudabbar. It is said: the experts are asked that if the scholars permitted selling this, the mentioned benefit would be lost; how much would it amount? Whatever is mentioned is its value, and this is better in my view. So in Fath al-Qadir. Its answer is that utilization (istikhdam) is the considered factor that includes the slave and the slave-girl, and intercourse is part of utilization. So with the mudabbar remain two things: utilization and seeking work, and the substitute is lost. This meaning includes the slave and the slave-girl, so the mufta bihi is what is in al-Hidaya. As for the value of umm walad and mukatab, it will come, God willing. They (the two) said: The slave belongs to the one who made him mudabbar first, and he guarantees two-thirds of his value to his partner, whether wealthy or poor, based on that tadbir is indivisible according to them. The author did not mention that the silent one has the right to istis'a' because it is apparent; for he may seek work for one-third of his value, and the mudabbar may seek work from the slave for one-third of his value as a mudabbar if he chooses not to hold the emancipator liable, as in Ghayat al-Bayan. He did not mention wala'. He said in al-Hidaya: wala' is between the emancipator and the mudabbar in thirds; two-thirds for the mudabbar and one-third for the emancipator, because the slave was freed while in their joint ownership to this extent. His meaning is that he is the mudabbar and the emancipator, because freedom does not become established for the mudabbar except after the death of his master, as in Ghayat al-Bayan and al-Nihaya. In Fath al-Qadir: This is a mistake, because immediate emancipation necessitates bringing him into freedom by one of the matters (tadmin with wealth, istis'a', or 'itq), such that it prevents the mudabbar from using him from the moment it occurs. The restriction of the author to wealth is because if the mudabbar is poor, the silent one has istis'a' without tadmin; likewise if the emancipator is poor, the mudabbar has istis'a' without holding the emancipator liable. From this it is known that the author's restriction to wealth of the three is not a condition, because consideration is to the wealth of the mudabbar and the emancipator. As for the silent one, it is said to the mudabbar: he may hold the emancipator liable for half of him as a mudabbar and one-third as a slave. And his saying 'and if he freed him' ... bears witness to correction. What he guaranteed, and if he said to his partner 'she is your umm walad' and he denies, she serves one day and stays one day, and what is for umm walad is... So no consideration of his state of wealth or poverty. The author did not mention the mudabbar's recourse against the slave for what he guaranteed to the silent one. Al-Hakim al-Shahid stated in al-Kafi that he has recourse against the slave for one-third of his value as a slave, just as he guaranteed. The author's restriction to the silent one choosing to hold the mudabbar liable after the other's emancipation is because if he chose to hold the mudabbar liable before the other emancipated him, then the other emancipated him, then the mudabbar may hold the emancipator liable for two-thirds of his value, because the emancipation occurred after the mudabbar owned the silent one's share, so he may hold him liable for one-third of his value as a slave and one-third of his value as a mudabbar, as described. In Fath al-Qadir: Some students objected to this that he should instead hold him liable for the value of two-thirds of him as a mudabbar, because when he owned the silent one's third by guarantee, he became a mudabbar, not a slave. Therefore we said in the face of two-thirds of wala' being for him, because he became as if he made a tadbir of two-thirds from the beginning. The answer is not complete without denying that the third which he owned by guarantee from the silent one became mudabbar; rather it is a slave in his ownership, because there is no cause for it to become mudabbar, because the emergence of ownership now does not necessitate it, and tadbir is divisible. Their mention of it in the face of two-thirds of wala' being for him is unnecessary; it suffices that it was in his ownership when the other emancipated him and he paid the guarantee. And it was not that his wala' was for him because it is a guarantee of injury, not ownership. By what we established first, it is known that the waw in the author's statement 'and another freed him' means 'then', and he restricted it because if one of them emancipated him, another made him mudabbar, and another made him mukatab, and the first is unknown, then all these acts are valid. The slave seeks work for the mudabbar for one-sixth of his value, and the emancipator also guarantees to him one-sixth of his value as a mudabbar if he is wealthy. The slave seeks work in the kitaba for the third. If he becomes unable, he has the choice: if he wishes, he seeks work from the slave for one-third of his value, and wala' is in thirds; if he wishes, he holds the mudabbar and the emancipator liable for one-third of his value in halves if they are both wealthy, and wala' is halves between them. Because when they are ignorant of the time, it is made as if these acts occurred together, and they are divisible according to Abu Hanifa, so they are valid. Then the emancipator has nothing against anyone. If one emancipates, another makes mukatab, and the third makes mudabbar together, no one has recourse because each's act occurred in his own property. If one made mudabbar first, then the second emancipates, then the third makes mukatab, the mudabbar has recourse against the emancipator for the value of his share, and the mukatab has no recourse against anyone. If he made mudabbar, then mukatab, then emancipates, the ruling for mudabbar and emancipator is what we mentioned. As for the mukatab, if the slave becomes unable, he has recourse against the emancipator for the value of his share, because he returned to being a slave for him, and the emancipator destroyed him. If he made him mukatab first, then mudabbar, then emancipates, if the slave does not become unable, he is freed upon him and there is no guarantee against anyone. If he becomes unable, he has recourse against the mudabbar for one-third of his value, not against the emancipator. And all of its details are in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq examines the complex scenario of partial emancipation and tadbir by co-owners. It is an intra-Hanafi dispute where Imam Abu Hanifa's view is contrasted with the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The core issue is whether tadbir is divisible: Imam holds it is divisible, so each partner's act affects only his share; the Sahibayn hold it is indivisible, so the first tadbir overrides later emancipation regarding ownership of the entire slave. The passage then explores practical consequences: the amount of compensation, the role of wealth (yasar) in determining whether the mudabbar or silent partner may demand istis'a' (seeking work from the slave) or tadmin (guarantee of value), and the distribution of wala' (clientage). It also addresses a subtle objection from Fath al-Qadir about whether the silent partner's share, when acquired by guarantee, becomes mudabbar. The author refutes this, affirming tadbir's divisibility. Finally, it presents various sequences of tadbir, i'itq, and kitaba from multiple unknown partners, showing how the Hanafi school applies the divisibility principle consistently. The passage is a model of systematic furu' reasoning within the madhhab.

Key Terms
تدبير (tadbir): A contract by which a master declares that his slave will become free upon the master's death.
استسعاء (istis'a'): Seeking work: the slave works to earn compensation for a co-owner.
ضمان (daman): Guarantee/compensation paid for loss of share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if one co-owner makes a slave mudabbar and the other emancipates, what is the status of the emancipator's share?
A) It becomes mudabbar as well.
B) It becomes free immediately.
C) It remains a slave until the mudabbar's death.
D) It is invalidated by the tadbir.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on whether tadbir is divisible; the mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view that it is divisible, allowing each co-owner's act to affect only his share.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

10 min
Value Calculation and Multi-Partner Sequences in Co-Ownership Tadbir
Practical application of value calculation and sequence rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the compensation amounts in co-ownership tadbir scenarios and apply the ruling to sequences of multiple partners.

Core Idea
Compensation for co-owners depends on wealth, and when multiple acts occur in sequence, each partner's rights and obligations are determined by the divisibility principle.

Once the core dispute on tadbir divisibility is settled (following Abu Hanifa's view), the next step is to calculate precisely what each party owes. This lesson dives into the value formula from al-Hidayah, the role of wealth in determining whether istis'a' is used, and how multiple acts in a known or unknown order affect the outcome.

Guided Path
1
Calculate the mudabbar's value
The mudabbar's value is two-thirds of the slave's market value as a qinn (unfree). Use the formula: mudabbar value = 2/3 * qinn value. Compensation from emancipator to mudabbar is one-third of the mudabbar value.
Example: slave worth 27 dinars -> mudabbar value = 18 dinars; emancipator owes 6 dinars to mudabbar; mudabbar owes 9 dinars to silent partner.
2
Apply the wealth condition (yasar)
The silent partner (or mudabbar) can demand compensation (daman) only if the other party is wealthy. If the other is poor, they can only demand istis'a' (slave works to pay).
Wealth is considered at the time of the act.
3
Determine wala' distribution
Wala' (clientage) is divided according to the freed shares. In the basic case, wala' is two-thirds for the mudabbar and one-third for the emancipator.
This is because the mudabbar's share is larger (two-thirds) and the emancipator's share is one-third.
4
Handle sequences with multiple partners and unknown timing
When acts occur in a known order, later acts can affect earlier rights (e.g., if emancipation occurs after the mudabbar has acquired the silent partner's share). If the order is unknown, all acts are considered simultaneous and divisible.
The passage details several sequences: tadbir then 'itq then kitabah, etc. Each sequence yields specific recourse rights.

Source Text

على العبد، وكذا لو صدر الإعتاق والتدبير منهما معاً. وهذا كله عند الإمام. وعندهما: المعتق أولى في الكل فإن كان المعتق موسراً ضمن للمدبر وإلا سعى العبد له في نصيبه؛ كذا في المحيط. وذكر قاضيخان في شرح الجامع الصغير أن قولنا للشريك هذه الخيارات أنه يصح منه هذه التصرفات، أما لا يؤذن بالإعتاق والاستسعاء لأن فيه إفساد نصيب المدبر لأن المدبر كان متمكناً من استسعاء نصيبه على ملكه إلى وقت الموت وبعد الإعتاق والاستسعاء لا يتمكن اهـ. وفي الهداية وقيمة المدبر ثلثا قيمته قناً على ما قالوا فلو كانت قيمته قناً سبعة وعشرين ديناراً ضمن له ستة دنانير لأن ثلثيها وهو قيمة المدبر ثمانية عشر وثلثها وهو المضمون ستة، والمدبر يضمن للساكت تسعة. وإنما كان كذلك لأن الانتفاع بالوطء والسعاية والبدل وإنما زال الأخير فقط وإليه مال الصدر الشهيد وعليه الفتوى إلا أن الوجه المذكور يخص المدبرة دون المدبر . وقيل : يسئل أهل الخبرة أن العلماء لو جوزوا بيع هذا فاتت المنفعة المذكورة كم يبلغ فما ذكر فهو قيمته وهذا أحسن عندي ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وجوابه أن الاستخدام هو المنظور إليه الشامل للعبد والجارية والوطء من الاستخدام فالباقي في المدبر شيئان الاستخدام والسعاية والفائت البدل وهذا المعنى يشمل العبد والجارية فلذا كان المفتى به ما في الهداية. وأما قيمة أم الولد والمكاتب فسيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى. وقالا : العبد للذي دبره أول مرة ويضمن ثلثي قيمته لشريكه موسراً كان أو معسراً بناء على أن التدبير لا يتجزئ عندهما. ولم يذكر المصنف أن للساكت الاستسعاء لظهوره لأن له أن يستسعي في ثلث قيمته وللمدبر أن يستسعي العبد في ثلث قيمته مدبراً إذا اختار عدم تضمين المعتق كما في غاية البيان، ولم يذكر الولاء قال في الهداية والولاء بين المعتق والمدبر أثلاثاً؛ ثلثاه للمدبر والثلث للمعتق لأن العبد عتق على ملكهما على هذا المقدار اهـ. ومراده أنه المدبر والمعتق لأن العتق لا يثبت للمدبر إلا بعد موت مولاه كما في غاية البيان والنهاية . وفي فتح القدير : وهو غلط لأن العتق المنجز يوجب إخراجه إلى الحرية بتنجيز أحد الأمور التضمين مع اليسار والسعاية والعتق حتى منع استخدام المدبر إياه من حين وجوده كما لو أعتق أحد الشريكين ابتداء ودبره الآخر الساكت فإنه لا تتأخر حرية باقية إلى موته كما قدمناه أول الباب إلى آخره . من العبد بين عصبة وقيد المصنف باليسار لأن المدبر لو كان معسراً فللساكت الاستسعاء دون التضمين، وكذا المعتق لو كان معسراً فللمدبر الاستسعاء دون تضمين المعتق؛ كذا في غاية البيان. وبهذا علم أن تقييد المصنف بيسار الثلاثة ليس بقيد لأن الاعتبار ليسار المدبر والمعتق، وأما الساكت يقال للمدبر أن يضمن المعتق نصفه مدبراً وثلثه قناً. وقوله ولو كان حرره يشهد الخ» يشهد للتصويب . ما ضمن ولو قال لشريكه هي أم ولدك وأنكر تخدمه يوماً وتتوقف يوماً وما لأم ولد هو فلا اعتبار بحاله من اليسار والإعسار ولم يذكر المصنف رجوع المدبر بما ضمنه للساكت على العبد وقد نص الحاكم الشهيد في الكافي بأنه يرجع على العبد بثلث قيمته قناً كما ضمن . وقيد المصنف بكون الساكت اختار تضمين المدبر بعد تحرير الآخر لأنه لو اختار تضمين المدبر . قبل أن يعتقه الآخر ثم أعتقه كان للمدبر أن يضمن المعتق ثلثي قيمته لأن الإعتاق وجد بعد تملك المدبر نصيب الساكت فله أن يضمنه ثلث قيمته قناً ، ثلث قيمته مدبراً كما صفته . قال في فتح القدير : وأورد بعض الطلبة على هذا أنه ينبغي أن يضمنه قيمة ثلثيه مدبراً لأنه حين ملك ثلث الساكت بالضمان صار مديراً لا قناً، ولذا قلنا في وجه كون ثلثي الولاء له لأنه صار كأنه دبر ثلثيه ابتداء، والجواب لا يتم إلا بمنع كون الثلث الذي ملكه بالضمان للساكت صار مدبراً بل هو قن على ملكه إذ لا موجب لصيرورته مدبراً لأن ظهور الملك الآن لا يوجبه والتدبير يتجزئ وذكرهم إياه في وجه كون ثلثي الولاء له غير محتاج إليه إذ يكفي فيه أنه على ملكه حين أعتقه الآخر وأدى الضمان وإنما لم يكن ولاؤه له لما ذكرنا من أنه ضمان جناية لا تملك ا هـ. وبما قررناه أولاً علم أن الواو في قول المصنف «وحرره آخر» بمعنى ثم قيد به لأنه لو أعتقه أحدهم ودبره الآخر وكاتب الآخر ولا يعلم الأول فالتصرفات كلها جائزة ويسعى العبد للمدبر في سدس قيمته وضمن له المعتق أيضاً سدس قيمته مدبراً إن كان موسراً، ويسعى العبد في المكاتبة للثالث، فإن عجز فهو بالخيار، إن شاء استسعى العبد في ثلث قيمته والولاء أثلاثاً، وإن شاء ضمن المدبر المعتق ثلث قيمته نصفين إذا كانا موسرين والولاء بينهما نصفان لأنهما لما جهلا التاريخ يجعل كأن هذه التصرفات وقعن معاً، وأنها متجزئه عند أبي حنيفة فصحت . ثم لا شيء للمعتق على أحد، وإن أعتق واحد وكاتب الآخر ودبر الثالث معاً ليس لواحد الرجوع لأن تصرف كل واحد حصل في ملك نفسه، وإن دبر أحدهم أولاً ثم أعتق الثاني ثم كاتب الآخر ثبت للمدبر الرجوع على المعتق بقيمة نصيبه ولا رجوع للمكاتب على أحد، فإن دبر ثم كاتب ثم أعتق فحكم المدبر والمعتق ما ذكرنا وأما المكاتب إذا عجز العبد يرجع على المعتق بقيمة نصيبه لأنه عاد عبداً له والمعتق أتلفه، وإن كاتبه أولاً ثم دبر ثم أعتق، فإن لم يعجز العبد يعتق عليه ولا ضمان له على أحد، وإن عجز يرجع على المدبر بثلث قيمته لا على المعتق وتمام تفريعاته في المحيط .
Upon the slave, and likewise if emancipation and tadbir occur from both of them together. All this according to Imam Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): the emancipator has precedence in all; if the emancipator is wealthy, he guarantees to the mudabbar, otherwise the slave seeks work for his share. So in al-Muhit. Qadikhan mentioned in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir that our statement to the partner that he has these options means that these acts are valid from him, but he is not permitted to emancipate and seek work because that spoils the mudabbar's share, since the mudabbar was able to seek work from his share while it was his property until death, and after emancipation and seeking work he cannot. In al-Hidaya: the value of the mudabbar is two-thirds of his value as a slave, as they said. So if his value as a slave was 27 dinars, he guarantees 6 dinars because two-thirds of it, which is the value of the mudabbar, is 18, and one-third of it, which is guaranteed, is 6, and the mudabbar guarantees 9 to the silent one. That is because the benefit through intercourse, seeking work, and the substitute; only the last is lost, and al-Sadr al-Shahid inclined to this, and upon it is the fatwa, except that the mentioned face is specific to the female mudabbara, not the male mudabbar. It is said: the experts are asked that if the scholars permitted selling this, the mentioned benefit would be lost; how much would it amount? Whatever is mentioned is its value, and this is better in my view. So in Fath al-Qadir. Its answer is that utilization (istikhdam) is the considered factor that includes the slave and the slave-girl, and intercourse is part of utilization. So with the mudabbar remain two things: utilization and seeking work, and the substitute is lost. This meaning includes the slave and the slave-girl, so the mufta bihi is what is in al-Hidaya. As for the value of umm walad and mukatab, it will come, God willing. They (the two) said: The slave belongs to the one who made him mudabbar first, and he guarantees two-thirds of his value to his partner, whether wealthy or poor, based on that tadbir is indivisible according to them. The author did not mention that the silent one has the right to istis'a' because it is apparent; for he may seek work for one-third of his value, and the mudabbar may seek work from the slave for one-third of his value as a mudabbar if he chooses not to hold the emancipator liable, as in Ghayat al-Bayan. He did not mention wala'. He said in al-Hidaya: wala' is between the emancipator and the mudabbar in thirds; two-thirds for the mudabbar and one-third for the emancipator, because the slave was freed while in their joint ownership to this extent. His meaning is that he is the mudabbar and the emancipator, because freedom does not become established for the mudabbar except after the death of his master, as in Ghayat al-Bayan and al-Nihaya. In Fath al-Qadir: This is a mistake, because immediate emancipation necessitates bringing him into freedom by one of the matters (tadmin with wealth, istis'a', or 'itq), such that it prevents the mudabbar from using him from the moment it occurs. The restriction of the author to wealth is because if the mudabbar is poor, the silent one has istis'a' without tadmin; likewise if the emancipator is poor, the mudabbar has istis'a' without holding the emancipator liable. From this it is known that the author's restriction to wealth of the three is not a condition, because consideration is to the wealth of the mudabbar and the emancipator. As for the silent one, it is said to the mudabbar: he may hold the emancipator liable for half of him as a mudabbar and one-third as a slave. And his saying 'and if he freed him' ... bears witness to correction. What he guaranteed, and if he said to his partner 'she is your umm walad' and he denies, she serves one day and stays one day, and what is for umm walad is... So no consideration of his state of wealth or poverty. The author did not mention the mudabbar's recourse against the slave for what he guaranteed to the silent one. Al-Hakim al-Shahid stated in al-Kafi that he has recourse against the slave for one-third of his value as a slave, just as he guaranteed. The author's restriction to the silent one choosing to hold the mudabbar liable after the other's emancipation is because if he chose to hold the mudabbar liable before the other emancipated him, then the other emancipated him, then the mudabbar may hold the emancipator liable for two-thirds of his value, because the emancipation occurred after the mudabbar owned the silent one's share, so he may hold him liable for one-third of his value as a slave and one-third of his value as a mudabbar, as described. In Fath al-Qadir: Some students objected to this that he should instead hold him liable for the value of two-thirds of him as a mudabbar, because when he owned the silent one's third by guarantee, he became a mudabbar, not a slave. Therefore we said in the face of two-thirds of wala' being for him, because he became as if he made a tadbir of two-thirds from the beginning. The answer is not complete without denying that the third which he owned by guarantee from the silent one became mudabbar; rather it is a slave in his ownership, because there is no cause for it to become mudabbar, because the emergence of ownership now does not necessitate it, and tadbir is divisible. Their mention of it in the face of two-thirds of wala' being for him is unnecessary; it suffices that it was in his ownership when the other emancipated him and he paid the guarantee. And it was not that his wala' was for him because it is a guarantee of injury, not ownership. By what we established first, it is known that the waw in the author's statement 'and another freed him' means 'then', and he restricted it because if one of them emancipated him, another made him mudabbar, and another made him mukatab, and the first is unknown, then all these acts are valid. The slave seeks work for the mudabbar for one-sixth of his value, and the emancipator also guarantees to him one-sixth of his value as a mudabbar if he is wealthy. The slave seeks work in the kitaba for the third. If he becomes unable, he has the choice: if he wishes, he seeks work from the slave for one-third of his value, and wala' is in thirds; if he wishes, he holds the mudabbar and the emancipator liable for one-third of his value in halves if they are both wealthy, and wala' is halves between them. Because when they are ignorant of the time, it is made as if these acts occurred together, and they are divisible according to Abu Hanifa, so they are valid. Then the emancipator has nothing against anyone. If one emancipates, another makes mukatab, and the third makes mudabbar together, no one has recourse because each's act occurred in his own property. If one made mudabbar first, then the second emancipates, then the third makes mukatab, the mudabbar has recourse against the emancipator for the value of his share, and the mukatab has no recourse against anyone. If he made mudabbar, then mukatab, then emancipates, the ruling for mudabbar and emancipator is what we mentioned. As for the mukatab, if the slave becomes unable, he has recourse against the emancipator for the value of his share, because he returned to being a slave for him, and the emancipator destroyed him. If he made him mukatab first, then mudabbar, then emancipates, if the slave does not become unable, he is freed upon him and there is no guarantee against anyone. If he becomes unable, he has recourse against the mudabbar for one-third of his value, not against the emancipator. And all of its details are in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq examines the complex scenario of partial emancipation and tadbir by co-owners. It is an intra-Hanafi dispute where Imam Abu Hanifa's view is contrasted with the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The core issue is whether tadbir is divisible: Imam holds it is divisible, so each partner's act affects only his share; the Sahibayn hold it is indivisible, so the first tadbir overrides later emancipation regarding ownership of the entire slave. The passage then explores practical consequences: the amount of compensation, the role of wealth (yasar) in determining whether the mudabbar or silent partner may demand istis'a' (seeking work from the slave) or tadmin (guarantee of value), and the distribution of wala' (clientage). It also addresses a subtle objection from Fath al-Qadir about whether the silent partner's share, when acquired by guarantee, becomes mudabbar. The author refutes this, affirming tadbir's divisibility. Finally, it presents various sequences of tadbir, i'itq, and kitaba from multiple unknown partners, showing how the Hanafi school applies the divisibility principle consistently. The passage is a model of systematic furu' reasoning within the madhhab.

Key Terms
ولاء (wala'): Clientage: the bond between the freed slave and the one who freed him, which gives the latter inheritance rights under certain conditions.
كتابة (kitabah): A contract of manumission by which the slave purchases his freedom in installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the timing of acts is unknown, how are the acts treated?
A) Only the earliest act is valid.
B) All acts are considered simultaneous and valid on respective shares.
C) The last act is void.
D) The mudabbar's act overrides all.

Key Takeaway
When multiple co-owners perform different acts on a slave, the divisibility principle (mufta bihi) ensures each act is valid on its share, with compensation and wala' calculated proportionally.

Liability and Disputes in Emancipation for a Consideration
Sets out liability, disputes, and evidence rules in emancipation for a consideration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the Hanafi rules on the slave's liability when a specific consideration is reclaimed or destroyed, and IDENTIFY the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Core Idea
Emancipation for a consideration is valid upon acceptance; liability for reclaimed specific items sparks a key intra-Hanafi split.

The contract of emancipation for a consideration (al-‘itq ‘alā jual) is valid once the master accepts the offer. But what happens if the specific property offered by the slave is later reclaimed by a third party? Or if the item is destroyed? The Hanafi school has a well-known internal disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on one side and Muhammad al-Shaybani on the other. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks these rules and also covers disputes on evidence, defects, and even wine for dhimmis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its validity
The passage opens with the ruling that if the slave brings the value (qimah) or a different item and the master accepts, emancipation takes effect (sahih) and the slave is liable for the consideration or his own value.
Note the distinction: if the specified item is a type (e.g., 'a garment' without further specification), the slave owes the value of his own person (qimat nafsihi).
2
Trace the effect of third-party reclamation (istihqaq)
If the item given by the slave is later reclaimed by its true owner, the emancipation is not annulled. The slave's liability then depends on whether the item was specified or not.
Unspecified: slave pays mithl or qimah. Specified: intra-Hanafi dispute.
3
Map the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: the slave must pay his own market value (qimat nafsihi). Muhammad: the slave must pay the value of the reclaimed property (qimat al-mustahaqq).
This dispute recurs for destruction before delivery and return for defect.
4
Examine the rules on disputes and evidence
If the master and slave disagree on the type/amount of consideration, the slave's word with oath is taken. But if both produce evidence, the master's evidence prevails – unlike the case of suspended emancipation where the opposite applies.
This reflects the principle that the slave is the primary obligor and the master is the claimant.
5
Apply the rulings to special cases
For dhimmis, wine and pig are valid considerations. If one party converts before delivery, the two imams say the slave owes the value of wine at the time; Muhammad says he owes the value of the wine as specified. Also, if the addressee is unknown, no liability.
The final narration by Abu Ja'far distinguishes acts that admit deputyship (Hajj) from those that do not (fasting, prayer).

Source Text

كتاب العتق باب العتق على جعل. ولو أتاه بالقيمة أجبر المولى على القبول، ولو لم يجانب بأن قال على ثوب أو حيوان أو دابة فقبل عتق ولزمه قيمة نفسه كما لو أعتقه على يسم قيمة رقبته فقبل عتق كما في المحيط. وأشار المصنف إلى أنه يعتق بالقبول ولو كان المال ملكاً للغير، فلو أعتقه على عبد مثلاً فاستحق لا ينفسخ العتق فإن كان بغير عينه فعلى العبد مثله في المثلي والوسط في القيمي، وإن كان معيناً رجع على العبد بقيمة نفسه عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف، وقال محمد بقيمة المستحق. وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا هلك قبل التسليم، وكذا على هذا الاختلاف لو رده بعيب وليس للمولى الرد بالعيب اليسير عند أبي حنيفة، وإنما يرده بالعيب الفاحش كالعيب في المهر، وقالا باليسير أيضاً؛ كذا في البدائع. ولو اختلفا في المال جنسه أو مقداره فالقول للعبد مع يمينه كما لو أنكر أصل المال، وإن أقاما البينة فالبينة للمولى بخلاف ما إذا كان العتق معلقاً بالأداء وهي المسألة الآتية فإن القول فيها قول المولى والبينة بينة العبد؛ كذا في البدائع. وشمل إطلاق المال الخمر في حق الذمي فإنها مال عندهم، فلو أعتق الذمي عبده على خمر أو خنزير فإنه يعتق بالقبول ويلزمه قيمة المسمى، فإن أسلم أحدهما قبل قبض الخمر فعندهما على العبد قيمته وعند محمد عليه قيمة الخمر؛ كذا في المحيط. وقيد بكون المخاطب بالعتق معيناً لأنه لو كان مجهولاً كما إذا قال أحدكما بألف والآخر بغير شيء فقبلا عتقا بلا شيء لأن عتقهما متيقن ومن عليه المال مجهول فلا يجب كرجلين قالا لرجل لك على أحدنا ألف وتمام تفريماته في المحيط. وفي الذخيرة: على أن تحج عني فلم يحج فعليه قيمة حج وسط. سئل أبو جعفر عن رجل قال لعبده صم عني يوماً وأنت حر أو صل عني ركعتين وأنت حر قال: عتق وإن لم يصم وإن لا يعتق حتى يحج لأن الصوم والصلاة مما لا تجري فيهما النيابة والحج مما يجري فيه النيابة، ولأنه لا مؤنة في الصوم والصلاة فلا يدل على اشتراط بدل والحج فيه مؤنة فدل على أنه شرط ذلك بدلاً ا.هـ.
Book of Emancipation, Chapter: Emancipation for a Consideration. If the slave brings the value, the master is compelled to accept. If he brings something of a different type, e.g., the master said 'on a garment' or 'an animal' and the slave accepted, he becomes free and is liable for his own value, just as if the master emancipated him on naming the value of his neck and he accepted. The author indicates that emancipation occurs by acceptance even if the property belongs to another. If the master emancipates him on a slave and it is reclaimed, the emancipation is not annulled. If it was unspecified, the slave must give its equivalent if fungible, or average value if non-fungible; if specified, he returns to the slave for his own value according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, but Muhammad says for the value of the reclaimed property. Similarly if it perishes before delivery, and similarly if he returns it for a defect. The master cannot return for a minor defect according to Abu Hanifa, only for a serious defect like in dower; the two say for minor defects as well. If they dispute about the type or amount of property, the slave's word with his oath is taken, as if he denied the property entirely; if both produce evidence, the master's evidence is preferred, unlike the case of suspended emancipation (to follow) where the master's word is taken and the slave's evidence is preferred. The general term 'property' includes wine for a dhimmī, as it is property for them. If a dhimmī master emancipates his slave for wine or pig, the slave becomes free by acceptance and is liable for the value of the named item. If one of them becomes Muslim before taking possession of the wine, according to the two, the slave owes its value; according to Muhammad, he owes the value of the wine. It is restricted to the addressee being specific; if unknown, e.g., the master says 'one of you for a thousand and the other for nothing' and both accept, both become free for nothing because their emancipation is certain and the debtor is unknown. Similarly if two men say to a man 'you have a thousand owed by one of us.' In al-Dhakhira: on condition that he perform Hajj for me but he did not, he owes the value of a moderate Hajj. Abu Ja'far was asked about a man saying to his slave: 'Fast for me a day and you are free' or 'Pray for me two rak'ahs and you are free.' He said: 'He becomes free even if he does not fast; but he does not become free until he performs Hajj because fasting and prayer do not admit deputyship, while Hajj does; also there is no expense in fasting and prayer, so it does not indicate a condition of compensation, whereas Hajj involves expense, so it indicates he made that a condition for compensation.' End.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq's commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq covers multiple rulings regarding emancipation for a consideration (al-‘itq ‘alā jual). It opens by stating that if the slave brings the market value instead of the specified item, the master must accept. If the slave brings a different type (e.g., the master said 'a garment' and the slave brings an animal), and the master accepts, emancipation occurs and the slave owes his own market value, not the value of the unspecified item. The author then notes that acceptance alone, even if the property belonged to a third party, triggers emancipation. If the specified item is later reclaimed from the master, emancipation is not reversed. The liability depends on whether the item was specified or not: if unspecified, the slave pays an equivalent or average value; if specified, there is a dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (slave pays his own value) and Muhammad (slave pays the value of the reclaimed item). This dispute extends to cases of destruction before delivery and return for defect. On the issue of returning a defective item, Abu Hanifa limits returns to serious defects, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow even minor defects. When the master and slave disagree on the type or amount of the consideration, the slave's word with oath is taken—unlike the case of suspended emancipation (ta‘līq bi al-adā') where the master's word is taken. For a dhimmī master, wine and pig are valid considerations; if either party converts to Islam before delivery, the two imams say the slave owes the value of the wine at the time, while Muhammad says he owes the value of the wine as specified. If the address is to an unspecified group (e.g., 'one of you'), both become free without liability. The passage ends with a narration from Abu Ja'far distinguishing between acts that admit deputyship (Hajj) and those that do not (fasting, prayer) in conditional emancipation.

Key Terms
العتق على جعل (al-‘itq ‘alā jual): Emancipation of a slave in exchange for a consideration (usually monetary) given by the slave to the master.
المثلي (al-mithlī): Fungible goods that have equivalents in the market (e.g., grains, oils).
القيمي (al-qīmī): Non-fungible goods whose value is estimated (e.g., animals, real estate).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the dispute between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on the liability of the slave when a specified reclaimed item is taken, what is the key difference?
A) Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf say the slave pays his own value; Muhammad says the slave pays the value of the reclaimed item.
B) Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf say the slave pays the value of the reclaimed item; Muhammad says the slave pays his own value.
C) Both say the slave pays nothing.
D) Both say the slave pays the value of the reclaimed item.

Key Takeaway
In emancipation for a consideration, the contract is valid upon acceptance; the slave's liability for a reclaimed specific item varies within the Hanafi school based on whether the slave owes his own value or the value of the item.

The Dual Nature of Emancipation for Payment
Explaining the dual nature of itq 'ala mal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi legal nature of itq 'ala mal as ta'liq from the master's perspective and mu'awada from the slave's perspective, and explain the resulting rules.

Core Idea
Emancipation for payment is a suspension from the master's side and an exchange from the slave's side.

In Hanafi fiqh, the transaction of emancipating a slave in exchange for a sum of money (itq 'ala mal) has a unique dual nature. The master's statement is a suspended emancipation (ta'liq), while the slave's role is that of an exchange (mu'awada). This hybrid structure produces distinct legal rules for each party, which the commentator in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq elaborates by highlighting the applicable rules and exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two perspectives
Recognize that from the master's side the transaction is ta'liq (suspension), and from the slave's side it is mu'awada (exchange).
This dual nature determines which set of rules applies to each party.
2
List the master's ta'liq rules
From the master's side: offer cannot be revoked, cannot be annulled, slave's presence not required, offer not invalidated by leaving session, can be suspended on condition or time, but stipulating option (khiyār) is invalid.
The master loses control once he makes the offer.
3
List the slave's mu'awada rules
From the slave's side: he has the right to revoke before acceptance, his leaving invalidates the offer, he cannot suspend or add to time, and the offer does not bind an absent master.
The slave's rights mirror those in an exchange contract.
4
Note the exception on khiyār
Abu Hanifa allows the master to stipulate an option (khiyār) for himself, contrary to the general rule that it is invalid.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن الإعتاق على مال من جانب المولى تعليق وهو تعليق العتق بشرط قبول العوض فيراعى فيه من جانبه أحكام التعليق حتى لو ابتدأ المولى لم يصح رجوعه عنه قبل قبول العبد، ولا الفسخ ولا النهي عن القبول ولا يبطل بقيامه عن المجلس ولا يشترط حضر العبد، ويصح تعليقه بشرط وإضافته إلى وقت ولا يصح شرط الخيار له ومن جانب العبد معاوضة فتراعي أحكامها، فملك الرجوع لو ابتدأ أو بطل بقيامه قبل قبول المولى. وبقيام المولى، ولا يقف على الغائب عن المجلس ولا يصح تعليقه ولا إضافته كما إذا قال اشتريت نفسي مني بألف إذا جاء غد أو عند رأس الشهر بخلاف ما إذا قال إذا جاء غد فأعتقني على كذا جاز لأن هذا توكيل منه بالإعتاق حتى يملك العبد عزله قبل وجود الشرط وبعده قبل أن يعتقه، ولو لم يعزل حتى عتقه نفذ اعتاقه ويجوز شرط الخيار له عند أبي حنيفة. ولو قال المولى أعتقتك أمس بألف فلم تقبل فقال العبد قبلت فالقول قول المولى مع يمينه تعليق وهو منكر لوجود الشرط؛ كذا في البدائع. قوله : (ولو علق عتقه بادائه صار مأذوناً) أي بأداء المال كأن يقول إن أديت إلي ألفاً حر فيصح ويعتق عند الأداء من غير أن يصير مكاتباً لأنه صريح في تعليق العتق بالأداء وإن كان فيه معنى المعاوضة في الانتهاء وإنما صار مأذوناً لأنه رغبة في الاكتساب لطلبه الأداء منه ومراده التجارة دون التكدي فكان إذناً له دلالة وذكر في فتح القدير أنه يخالف المكاتب في إحدى عشرة مسألة : الأولى ما إذا مات العبد قبل الأداء وترك مالاً فهو للمولى ولا يؤدي منه عنه ويعتق بخلاف الكتابة الثانية لو مات المولى وفي يد العبد كسب كان لورثة المولى ويباع العبد بخلاف الكتابة الثالثة لو كانت أمة فولدت ثم أدت فعتقت لم يعتق ولدها لأنه ليس لها حكم الكتابة وقت الولادة بخلاف الكتابة. الرابعة لو قال العبد للمولى حط عني مائة فحط عنه المولى وأدى تسعمائة لا يعتق بخلاف الكتابة. زاد في البدائع أنه لو أدى مكان الدراهم دنانير لا يعتق وإن قبل لعدم الشرط. الخامسة لو أبرأ المولى العبد عن الألف لم يعتق ولو أبرأ المكاتب عتق؛ كذا ذكروها والظاهر أنه لا موقع لها إذا الفرق بعد تحقق الإبراء في الموضعين يكون والإبراء لا يتصور في هذه المسألة لأنه لا دين على العبد بخلاف الكتابة السادسة لو باع المولى العبد ثم اشتراه أو رد عليه بخيار عيب ففي وجوب قبول ما يأتي به خلاف عند أبي يوسف نعم، وعند محمد لا ولكن لو قبضه عتق بخلاف الكتابة في أنه لا خلاف في أنه يجب أن يقبله ويعد قابضاً. السابعة أنه يقتصر على المجلس فلا يعتق ما لم يؤد في ذلك المجلس فلو اختلف بأن أعرض أو أخذ في عمل آخر فأدى لا يعتق بخلاف الكتابة وهذا إذا كان المذكور من أدوات الشرط لفظة «إن» فإن كان لفظ «إذا» أو «متى» فلا يقتصر على المجلس الثامنة أنه يجوز للمولى بيع العبد بعد قوله ذلك قبل أن يؤدي بخلاف الكاتبة التاسعة أن للسيد أن يأخذ ما يظفر به مما اكتسبه قبل أن يأتيه بما يؤديه بخلاف المكاتب العاشرة أنه إذا أدى وعتق وفضل عنده مال مما اكتسبه كان للسيد فيأخذه بخلاف المكاتب الحادية عشرة لو اكتسب العبد مالاً قبل تعليق السيد فأداه بعده إليه عتق وإن كان السيد يرجع بمثله على ما سيذكر بخلاف الكتابة لا يعتق بأدائه لأنه ملك المولى إلا أن يكون كاتبه على نفسه وماله فإنه حينئذ يصير أحق به بعد الوفاة.
Then know that emancipation for property from the master's side is a suspension, i.e., suspending emancipation on the condition of acceptance of the compensation. Thus from his side the rules of suspension are observed: if the master initiates, he cannot revoke before the slave's acceptance, nor annul, nor prohibit acceptance; it is not invalidated by his leaving the session; the slave's presence is not required; it is valid to suspend it on a condition and to add it to a time; but stipulating an option for himself is not valid. From the slave's side it is an exchange, so its rules are observed: he has the right of revocation if he initiates; it is invalidated by his leaving before the master's acceptance, and by the master's leaving; it does not depend on an absent person; its suspension or addition to a time is not valid, as when he says 'I bought myself from you for a thousand if tomorrow comes' or 'at the beginning of the month', unlike if he says 'if tomorrow comes, then emancipate me for such' because this is a delegation from him for emancipation, so the slave has the right to dismiss him before the condition is met and after it before he emancipates; but if he does not dismiss until he emancipates, his emancipation is effective. Stipulating an option for himself is valid according to Abu Hanifa. If the master says 'I emancipated you yesterday for a thousand and you did not accept,' and the slave says 'I accept,' the master's word with his oath is taken because it is a suspension and he denies the existence of the condition. His words: 'And if he suspends his emancipation on payment, he becomes a ma'dhūn (permitted to trade)' meaning payment of property, e.g., says 'if you pay me a thousand, you are free.' It is valid and he becomes free upon payment without becoming a mukātab, because it is explicit in suspending emancipation on payment, though it contains the meaning of exchange in the outcome. He becomes a ma'dhūn because it indicates desire for earning, seeking payment from him, and his intent is trade, not begging, so it is a permission by implication. It is mentioned in Fath al-Qadīr that it differs from kitābah in eleven issues: First: if the slave dies before payment leaving property, it belongs to the master, nothing is paid from it for him, and he becomes free (posthumously), unlike kitābah. Second: if the master dies and the slave has earnings, it goes to the master's heirs and the slave is sold, unlike kitābah. Third: if a female slave gives birth then pays and becomes free, her child does not become free because she had no kitābah status at birth, unlike kitābah. Fourth: if the slave says 'reduce a hundred for me' and the master reduces and he pays nine hundred, he does not become free, unlike kitābah. Al-Bada'i' added: if he pays dinars instead of dirhams, he does not become free even if accepted, due to lack of condition. Fifth: if the master discharges the slave from the thousand, he does not become free; but if he discharges a mukātab, he becomes free. They mentioned it, but apparently there is no place for it because the difference after realizing discharge in both places would be that discharge is inconceivable in this case since there is no debt on the slave, unlike kitābah. Sixth: if the master sells the slave then buys him back or returns him by option of defect, then regarding the obligation to accept what he brings, there is dispute: according to Abu Yusuf yes, according to Muhammad no, but if he takes possession he becomes free, unlike kitābah where there is no dispute that he must accept and be considered a taker. Seventh: it is restricted to the session; he does not become free unless he pays in that session; if he delays by turning away or engaging in other work and then pays, he does not become free, unlike kitābah. This if the conditional particle is 'in'; if it is 'idhā' or 'matā', it is not restricted to the session. Eighth: the master may sell the slave after saying that before he pays, unlike kitābah. Ninth: the master may take whatever he finds of the slave's earnings before he brings what he pays, unlike the mukātab. Tenth: if he pays and becomes free and has surplus earnings, it belongs to the master, unlike the mukātab. Eleventh: if the slave earned property before the master's suspension and then pays it to him, he becomes free, though the master can claim its equivalent as will be mentioned, unlike kitābah where he does not become free by payment because it is the master's property, unless he had contracted kitābah on himself and his property, then he becomes more entitled to it after death.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This section from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq provides a legal analysis of the transaction known as 'itq 'ala māl (emancipation for a consideration). The commentator first distinguishes the nature of the transaction from each party's perspective. From the master's side, it is a ta'liq (suspension): the master suspends emancipation on the condition of the slave's acceptance of the compensation. Therefore, rules of suspension apply: once the master makes the offer, he cannot revoke it before acceptance, nor can he prohibit acceptance; the slave's presence is not required; the offer is not invalidated by the master leaving the session; it can be suspended on a condition or added to a future time; but the master cannot stipulate an option (khiyār) for himself. From the slave's side, it is a mu'awadah (exchange), so the slave has the right to revoke before acceptance, and his revocation or leaving the session invalidates the offer. The slave's offer does not bind the master if absent, cannot be suspended or added to a time, etc. The commentator then addresses a specific application: if the master suspends emancipation on payment of a sum (ta'liq al-‘itq bi al-adā'), the slave becomes a ma'dhūn (permitted to trade) because the arrangement encourages earning. This does not make the slave a mukātab (under contract of manumission). The passage then lists eleven differences between this arrangement (ta'liq al-‘itq bi al-adā') and kitābah, drawn from Fath al-Qadīr. These differences cover death of either party, childbirth, reduction of amount, change of currency, discharge, sale of slave, session restrictions, right to sell, taking earnings, surplus earnings, and prior earnings. The commentary also includes a critical note on the fifth difference, questioning its applicability. The eleventh difference is cut off at the end.

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta‘līq): Suspending a legal effect (here emancipation) on the occurrence of a future condition (here acceptance of compensation).
المعاوضة (al-mu‘āwadah): Exchange; a contract where each party gives something in return for something from the other.
المأذون (al-ma’dhūn): A slave who is permitted by his master to engage in trade; his earnings are his own but subject to master's claims.
الكتابة (al-kitābah): A contract of manumission where the slave pays installments and becomes free upon completion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
From the master's side, the transaction of itq 'ala mal is considered what in Hanafi fiqh?
A) A binding sale (bay')
B) A suspended emancipation (ta'liq)
C) A revocable gift (hibah)
D) A delegation (tawkīl)

Key Takeaway
Emancipation for payment (itq 'ala mal) is legally a hybrid: ta'liq from the master's side and mu'awada from the slave's side, each with distinct rules.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Eleven Differences Between Ta'liq al-Itq bi al-Ada' and Kitabah
Listing eleven differences between ta'liq al-‘itq bi al-adā' and kitābah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list at least five of the eleven differences between ta'liq al-‘itq bi al-adā' and kitābah, and explain the legal reasoning behind selected differences.

Core Idea
Ta'liq al-‘itq bi al-adā' and kitābah are distinct despite superficial similarity, differing in death, earnings, discharge, session, and more.

Although both arrangements involve a slave paying a sum to become free, ta'liq al-‘itq bi al-adā' (suspended emancipation on payment) and kitābah (formal contract of manumission) are legally distinct. The text from Fath al-Qadīr enumerates eleven issues where the rulings diverge, highlighting the unique nature of each. This lesson examines these differences and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the distinction
Understand that ta'liq al-‘itq bi al-adā' is not a contract like kitābah; it is a unilateral suspension from the master and an exchange from the slave.
The eleven differences flow from this fundamental distinction.
2
Group the differences by theme
Some differences relate to death of parties, some to payment issues, some to the master's rights over the slave.
Grouping helps memorization and application.
3
Analyze a specific difference: the sixth
In the sixth difference, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the master's obligation to accept payment after resale.
This shows that even within the school, details are contested.
4
Apply to new scenarios
Given a hypothetical case, determine whether it falls under ta'liq or kitābah based on the eleven differences.
This tests comprehension of the distinctions.

ثم اعلم أن الإعتاق على مال من جانب المولى تعليق وهو تعليق العتق بشرط قبول العوض فيراعى فيه من جانبه أحكام التعليق حتى لو ابتدأ المولى لم يصح رجوعه عنه قبل قبول العبد، ولا الفسخ ولا النهي عن القبول ولا لو قبضه عتق بخلاف الكتابة في أنه لا خلاف في أنه يجب أن يقبله ويعد قابضاً. السابعة أنه يقتصر على المجلس فلا يعتق ما لم يؤد في ذلك المجلس فلو اختلف بأن أعرض أو أخذ في عمل آخر فأدى لا يعتق بخلاف الكتابة وهذا إذا كان المذكور من أدوات الشرط لفظة «إن» فإن كان لفظ «إذا» أو «متى» فلا يقتصر على المجلس الثامنة أنه يجوز للمولى بيع العبد بعد قوله ذلك قبل أن يؤدي بخلاف الكاتبة التاسعة أن للسيد أن يأخذ ما يظفر به مما اكتسبه قبل أن يأتيه بما يؤديه بخلاف المكاتب العاشرة أنه إذا أدى وعتق وفضل عنده مال مما اكتسبه كان للسيد فيأخذه بخلاف المكاتب الحادية عشرة لو اكتسب العبد مالاً قبل تعليق السيد فأداه بعده إليه عتق وإن كان السيد يرجع بمثله على ما سيذكر بخلاف الكتابة لا يعتق بأدائه لأنه ملك المولى إلا أن يكون كاتبه على نفسه وماله فإنه حينئذ يصير أحق به بعد الوفاة.
Then know that emancipation for property from the master's side is a suspension, i.e., suspending emancipation on the condition of acceptance of the compensation. Thus from his side the rules of suspension are observed: if the master initiates, he cannot revoke before the slave's acceptance, nor annul, nor prohibit acceptance; it is not invalidated by his leaving the session; the slave's presence is not required; it is valid to suspend it on a condition and to add it to a time; but stipulating an option for himself is not valid. From the slave's side it is an exchange, so its rules are observed: he has the right of revocation if he initiates; it is invalidated by his leaving before the master's acceptance, and by the master's leaving; it does not depend on an absent person; its suspension or addition to a time is not valid, as when he says 'I bought myself from you for a thousand if tomorrow comes' or 'at the beginning of the month', unlike if he says 'if tomorrow comes, then emancipate me for such' because this is a delegation from him for emancipation, so the slave has the right to dismiss him before the condition is met and after it before he emancipates; but if he does not dismiss until he emancipates, his emancipation is effective. If the master says 'I emancipated you yesterday for a thousand and you did not accept,' and the slave says 'I accept,' the master's word with his oath is taken because it is a suspension and he denies the existence of the condition. His words: 'And if he suspends his emancipation on payment, he becomes a ma'dhūn (permitted to trade)' meaning payment of property, e.g., says 'if you pay me a thousand, you are free.' It is valid and he becomes free upon payment without becoming a mukātab, because it is explicit in suspending emancipation on payment, though it contains the meaning of exchange in the outcome. He becomes a ma'dhūn because it indicates desire for earning, seeking payment from him, and his intent is trade, not begging, so it is a permission by implication. It is mentioned in Fath al-Qadīr that it differs from kitābah in eleven issues: First: if the slave dies before payment leaving property, it belongs to the master, nothing is paid from it for him, and he becomes free (posthumously), unlike kitābah. Second: if the master dies and the slave has earnings, it goes to the master's heirs and the slave is sold, unlike kitābah. Third: if a female slave gives birth then pays and becomes free, her child does not become free because she had no kitābah status at birth, unlike kitābah. Fourth: if the slave says 'reduce a hundred for me' and the master reduces and he pays nine hundred, he does not become free, unlike kitābah. Al-Bada'i' added: if he pays dinars instead of dirhams, he does not become free even if accepted, due to lack of condition. Fifth: if the master discharges the slave from the thousand, he does not become free; but if he discharges a mukātab, he becomes free. They mentioned it, but apparently there is no place for it because the difference after realizing discharge in both places would be that discharge is inconceivable in this case since there is no debt on the slave, unlike kitābah. Sixth: if the master sells the slave then buys him back or returns him by option of defect, then regarding the obligation to accept what he brings, there is dispute: according to Abu Yusuf yes, according to Muhammad no, but if he takes possession he becomes free, unlike kitābah where there is no dispute that he must accept and be considered a taker. Seventh: it is restricted to the session; he does not become free unless he pays in that session; if he delays by turning away or engaging in other work and then pays, he does not become free, unlike kitābah. This if the conditional particle is 'in'; if it is 'idhā' or 'matā', it is not restricted to the session. Eighth: the master may sell the slave after saying that before he pays, unlike kitābah. Ninth: the master may take whatever he finds of the slave's earnings before he brings what he pays, unlike the mukātab. Tenth: if he pays and becomes free and has surplus earnings, it belongs to the master, unlike the mukātab.
