Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This section from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq provides a legal analysis of the transaction known as 'itq 'ala māl (emancipation for a consideration). The commentator first distinguishes the nature of the transaction from each party's perspective. From the master's side, it is a ta'liq (suspension): the master suspends emancipation on the condition of the slave's acceptance of the compensation. Therefore, rules of suspension apply: once the master makes the offer, he cannot revoke it before acceptance, nor can he prohibit acceptance; the slave's presence is not required; the offer is not invalidated by the master leaving the session; it can be suspended on a condition or added to a future time; but the master cannot stipulate an option (khiyār) for himself. From the slave's side, it is a mu'awadah (exchange), so the slave has the right to revoke before acceptance, and his revocation or leaving the session invalidates the offer. The slave's offer does not bind the master if absent, cannot be suspended or added to a time, etc. The commentator then addresses a specific application: if the master suspends emancipation on payment of a sum (ta'liq al-‘itq bi al-adā'), the slave becomes a ma'dhūn (permitted to trade) because the arrangement encourages earning. This does not make the slave a mukātab (under contract of manumission). The passage then lists eleven differences between this arrangement (ta'liq al-‘itq bi al-adā') and kitābah, drawn from Fath al-Qadīr. These differences cover death of either party, childbirth, reduction of amount, change of currency, discharge, sale of slave, session restrictions, right to sell, taking earnings, surplus earnings, and prior earnings. The commentary also includes a critical note on the fifth difference, questioning its applicability. The eleventh difference is cut off at the end.

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta‘līq): Suspending a legal effect (here emancipation) on the occurrence of a future condition (here acceptance of compensation).
المعاوضة (al-mu‘āwadah): Exchange; a contract where each party gives something in return for something from the other.
المأذون (al-ma’dhūn): A slave who is permitted by his master to engage in trade; his earnings are his own but subject to master's claims.
الكتابة (al-kitābah): A contract of manumission where the slave pays installments and becomes free upon completion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the eighth difference, what can the master do in ta'liq al-‘itq bi al-adā' that he cannot do in kitābah?
A) Sell the slave before payment
B) Cancel the agreement unilaterally
C) Increase the payment amount
D) Take the slave's earnings after freedom

Key Takeaway
The eleven differences show that ta'liq al-‘itq bi al-adā' gives the master more control and fewer obligations than kitābah, reflecting its nature as a conditional suspension rather than a contract.

Prohibited Transactions on Mudabbar and the Effect of a Qadi's Ruling
Expansion of rulings on mudabbar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which transactions are prohibited for a mudabbar and explain the effect of a qadi's ruling that permits such a transaction.

Core Idea
Any transaction that nullifies the cause of freedom is prohibited for a mudabbar.

This lesson expands upon the rulings concerning the mudabbar (a slave promised freedom upon the master's death). We explore which transactions the master cannot undertake with the mudabbar, such as sale, pawning, and making him a guardian. We then examine a nuanced scenario: what happens if a qadi rules in favor of a sale despite the prohibition? The passage clarifies that such a ruling revokes the tadbir, but the commentator raises a critical distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify prohibited transactions
From the passage, list the specific transactions that are prohibited for a mudabbar: sale, dower, pawning (rahn), and giving as guardianship (wasaya).
These are transactions that would not be permissible for a free person.
2
Understand the rationale
The prohibition is because the mudabbar has a cause of freedom attached; any transaction that nullifies this cause (e.g., transferring ownership) is prevented.
The master's ownership remains, but the cause of freedom limits his disposition.
3
Examine the effect of a qadi's ruling
If a qadi rules that the sale of a mudabbar is valid, according to the passage, his ruling is executed and the tadbir is revoked, so even if the slave returns to the master, he is not freed.
This is the stated view of Abu Hanifa as per al-Zahiriyya.
4
Analyze the commentator's critique
The commentator finds this problematic because the dispute is over the necessity (luzum) of the tadbir, not its validity. He suggests the qadi's ruling should only nullify the necessity, not the tadbir itself.
This highlights a nuanced intra-Hanafi discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الذخيرة وغيرها: كل تصرف لا يقع في الحر نحو البيع والإمهار فإنه يمنع في المدبر والمدبرة لأن المدبر باقي على حكم الملك المولى إلا أنه انعقد له سبب الحرية فكل تصرف يبطل هذا السبب يمنع المولى منه ا هـ. فلذا لا تجوز الوصاية به ولا رهنه لأن الرهن والارتهان من باب إيفاء الدين واستيفائه عندنا فكان من باب تمليك العين وتملكها كذا في البدائع ومن هنا يعلم أن شرط الواقفين في كتبهم أنها لا تخرج إلا برهن شرط باطل إذ الوقف أمانة في يد مستعيره فلا يتأتى الإيفاء والاستيفاء بالرهن سنوضحه إن شاء الله تعالى وفي الظهيرية فإن باعه وقضى القاضي رقبته عتق ذلك القدر ويسعى في الباقي عند أبي حنفية . ولو وهب له رقبته أو تصدق عليها بها عتق من ثلثه . ولو أوصى له بثلث ماله صح وعتق ثلثه فإن بقي من الثلث أكمل له، وإن كان في قيمته فضل على الثلث سعى للورثة ا.هـ. وقوله فإن بقي من الثلث أكمل له الخ معناه والله أعلم أنه يستحق ثلث المال ومنه ثلث رقبته وعليه ثلثا رقبته فإن كان ثلثاها أقل من ثلث باقي المال أكمل له تتمة الثلث وإن كان ثلثاها أكثر يسعى للورثة فيما زاد فيكمل له ثلث المال فقط قوله : (ولم يصرح بالخ) كيف تكون متفقاً عليه ولم بجواز بيعه نفذ قضاؤه ويكون ذلك فسخاً للتدبير حتى لو عاد إليه يوماً من الدهر بوجه من الوجوه ثم مات لا يعتق وهذا مشكل، لأنه يبطل بقضاء القاضي ما هو مختلف فيه وما هو مختلف فيه لزوم التدبير لا صحة التعليق فينبغي أن يبطل وصف اللزوم لا غير
And in al-Dhakhira and others: Every disposition that does not occur concerning a free person, such as sale and dower, is prevented concerning the mudabbar and the mudabbara, because the mudabbar remains under the rule of the master's ownership, except that a cause of freedom has been established for him. So every disposition that nullifies this cause is prevented from the master. Hence, giving him in guardianship (wasaya) is not permissible, nor pawning him, because pawn and taking pawn are from the category of fulfilling debt and collecting it according to us, so they are from the category of making the object owned and owning it. Thus in al-Bada'i. And from this it is known that the condition of the endowers (waqifs) in their books that something is not taken out except with a pawn is an invalid condition, since the endowment is a trust in the hand of the borrower, so fulfilling and collecting by pawn is not possible. We will clarify it if Allah wills. And in al-Zahiriyya: If he sold him and the judge ruled (concerning) his neck, that amount would be freed, and he would work for the remainder according to Abu Hanifa. And if he gave him his neck as a gift or gave charity of it, he would be freed from his third. And if he bequeathed one-third of his property to him, it is valid and one-third of him is freed; if there remains from the third, it is completed for him; and if the value exceeds the third, he works for the heirs. End. And his statement: 'if there remains from the third, it is completed for him' etc. means, Allah knows best, that he deserves one-third of the property, and from it one-third of his neck, and upon him is two-thirds of his neck. If the two-thirds is less than one-third of the remaining property, the completion of the third is completed for him; and if the two-thirds is more, he works for the heirs for the excess, so one-third of the property is completed for him only. His statement: (and he did not clarify...) how it is agreed upon? And if (the judge) executed his sale, his ruling is executed and that is a revocation of the tadbir, so if it returns to him at any time by any means and then dies, he is not freed. And this is problematic because the judge's ruling nullifies what is disputed over, and what is disputed over is the necessity (luzum) of tadbir, not the validity of the suspension; so it should nullify the attribute of necessity only, nothing else.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of rulings related to the mudabbar. It begins by stating a general principle from al-Dhakhira: any transaction that is not valid for a free person (like sale, dower) is also prohibited for the mudabbar, because the mudabbar, though remaining under ownership, has a cause of freedom attached. Transactions that would nullify this cause are prevented. Then specific examples: giving the mudabbar as a pledge (rahn) is not permitted, because rahn involves transfer of ownership rights which would conflict with the tadbir. From this, the commentator deduces that a condition set by waqifs that an endowed item can only be taken out with a pawn is invalid, because endowment property is a trust, not subject to pawn. Then he discusses a case from al-Zahiriyya: if someone sells a mudabbar and the qadi issues a ruling (presumably upholding the sale or acknowledging the sale), then the portion that was sold (the neck) becomes free, and the slave must work to pay the remaining value. Also, if someone gives the mudabbar's neck as a gift or charity, the mudabbar is freed from the master's one-third—meaning the freeing is valid from the third of the estate. And if the master bequeaths one-third of his property to the mudabbar, the mudabbar is partially freed. The commentator explains the math: the mudabbar deserves one-third of the estate, including one-third of his own value; if two-thirds of his value is less than one-third of the remaining estate, the third is completed from the estate; if two-thirds exceeds, he works for the heirs. Finally, there is a discussion about the effect of a qadi's ruling that permits the sale: if the qadi rules in favor of sale (despite the Hanafi prohibition), his ruling is effective and revokes the tadbir permanently. However, the commentator finds this problematic because the dispute is about the necessity (luzum) of the tadbir, not its validity. He suggests that the qadi's ruling should only nullify the attribute of necessity, not the tadbir itself.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): Pawning; a contract where a property is given as security for a debt.
قضاء القاضي (qada' al-qadi): The ruling of a judge; when a qadi rules on a disputed matter, his ruling is enforceable.
الوصاية (al-wasaya): Guardianship or making someone a guardian; not permitted for mudabbar as it involves transfer of authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The tadbir remains intact and the sale is void.
B) The tadbir is revoked and the mudabbar does not become free even if returned.
C) The mudabbar becomes free immediately.
D) The qadi's ruling is not enforceable.

Key Takeaway
The qadi's ruling on a disputed transaction involving a mudabbar may revoke the tadbir, but the Hanafi school emphasizes that the prohibition against such transactions remains.

Permissible Uses of the Mudabbar and Their Earnings
Ruling with proof: permissibility of using mudabbar and ownership of earnings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the permissible actions a master may perform with a mudabbar/mudabbara and apply the condition that such actions must not invalidate the cause of tadbir.

Core Idea
Ownership continues until death; the master's authority over the mudabbar remains except where it would defeat eventual freedom.

A mudabbar is a slave whose master has declared he will be free upon the master's death. This creates a unique status: the slave remains owned, but his eventual freedom is a right. The passage clarifies that the master may still employ, rent, cohabit with, and marry off the mudabbar, and any earnings belong to the master. The key legal principle is that any disposition valid for a full slave is valid for a mudabbar, provided it does not nullify the cause of tadbir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts ruled upon
List the specific actions the master may take: employ (istikhdam), rent (ijara), cohabit (wata'), and force marriage (nikah jabran) - both for mudabbar and mudabbara.
The phrase 'as previously mentioned in the chapter on marriage of slaves' links to general slave rules.
2
State the hukm and its basis
The ruling is mubah (permissible). The proof is 'because ownership is established over him, and through it authority over these actions is acquired.' Ownership is the legal cause (sabab) for these powers.
The hukm level is mubah, not wajib or haram, because ownership confers discretion.
3
Identify the limiting principle
The passage quotes al-Dhakhira: 'any action that occurs regarding a free person is not prevented regarding the mudabbar... because it does not invalidate the cause for which he was designated.' The cause is eventual freedom. So actions that would destroy or defeat that cause (e.g., permanently disabling, selling) are not allowed.
This is a condition (shart) for permissibility: no harm to the tadbir.
4
Apply to financial benefits
Earnings (kasb), injury compensation (arsh), and dower (mahr) of the mudabbara all belong to the master because the slave remains under the master's ownership rule.
This follows logically from continued ownership.
5
Contrast with opposing views
Some schools may restrict such uses if they impair the slave's eventual freedom. The Hanafi position unanimously holds that as long as the tadbir is not nullified, all slave-related dispositions are valid.
The passage does not detail opposing views, but the madhhab position is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ويستخدم ويؤجر وتوطأ وتنكح أي ويستخدم المدبر ويؤجر وكذا المدبرة وتوطأ المدبرة أي يجوز للمولى ذلك ويجوز أن يزوجها جبراً عليها وكذا المدبر كما تقدم في نكاح الرقيق. وإنما جازت هذه التصرفات لأن الملك ثابت فيه وبه تستفاد ولاية هذه التصرفات . وضابطها كما في الذخيرة أن كل تصرف يقع في الحر فإنه لا يمنع في المدبر والمدبرة لأنه لا يبطل ما انعقد له من السبب. وأفاد المصنف رحمه الله بجواز ذلك أن أكساب المدبر والمدبرة للمولى وكذا أرشهما وكذا مهرها للمولى لأنهما بقيا على حكم ملك المولى؛ كذا في الذخيرة.
His saying: (And he is employed, rented, she is cohabited, and she is married) i.e., the mudabbar is employed and rented, and likewise the mudabbara; the mudabbara is cohabited, meaning that it is permissible for the master to do that, and it is permissible for him to force her into marriage, and likewise the mudabbar, as previously mentioned in the chapter on marriage of slaves. These actions are permissible because ownership is established over him, and through it authority over these actions is acquired. The rule, as in al-Dhakhira, is that any action that occurs regarding a free person is not prevented regarding the mudabbar and mudabbara, because it does not invalidate the cause for which he was designated. The author, may Allah have mercy on him, indicated by the permissibility of that that the earnings of the mudabbar and mudabbara belong to the master, and likewise their compensation for injuries, and likewise her dower belongs to the master, because they remain under the rule of the master's ownership; thus in al-Dhakhira.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This is an expansion probe explaining a ruling from the matn. The passage states that a master may employ, rent out, have sexual intercourse with, and marry off his mudabbar (male slave whose freedom is deferred to the master's death) and mudabbara (female counterpart). The justification is that ownership remains intact, and ownership confers authority over such actions. The general principle from al-Dhakhira is that any disposition that is valid regarding a free person is also valid regarding a mudabbar or mudabbara, as long as it does not nullify the reason for which he was declared mudabbar (i.e., eventual freedom). The commentator notes that the earnings, compensation for injuries (arsh), and dower (for a female mudabbara) all belong to the master because the slave remains under the master's ownership. This is the standard Hanafi position as recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose master declares that he will be free upon the master's death.
أرش (arsh): Compensation paid for injury to a slave, belonging to the master.
مهر (mahr): Dower paid to the master of a female slave upon her marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The master retains full ownership of a mudabbar until death, so he may employ, rent, cohabit, and marry him/her off, and all earnings belong to him, provided the tadbir is not nullified.

The Mudabbar's Obligation to Pursue Payment (Sa'yah)
Ruling on mudabbar's obligation to seek payment when estate cannot accommodate tadbir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the amount a mudabbar must pursue (two-thirds or full value) based on the master's financial status and explain the legal reasoning.

Core Idea
A mudabbar must earn to pay his value if the master's estate cannot free him: debts come first, then wills.

This passage expands on the matn's ruling: when a master dies poor or in debt, the mudabbar must work to pay his value to the heirs. The commentator explains the rationale and then introduces a key difference among the early Hanafi masters regarding the mudabbar's legal status during this payment period.

Guided Path
1
Identify the master's financial condition
Determine if the master is poor (no other wealth) or in debt (debt covers entire estate).
Poor: no wealth except mudabbar; In debt: debt exceeds or equals estate value.
2
Determine the amount due
If poor, mudabbar must pursue two-thirds of his value; if in debt, he must pursue the full value.
Reasoning: tadbir is a will, limited to one-third; debt has priority.
3
Understand the legal basis
The tadbir (will of manumission) is treated as a wasiyya, which takes effect from one-third of the estate. Debt takes precedence over wasiyya.
Thus, if only one-third available, the mudabbar pays two-thirds to heirs for the remaining portion.
4
Recognize the status during sa'yah
During the period of pursuit, the mudabbar is not yet fully free. A dispute exists: Abu Hanifa equates him to a mukatib; the two consider him a free debtor.
This dispute will be explored in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ويسعى في ثلثيه لو فقيراً أو كله لو مديوناً) أي يسعى المدبر للورثة في ثلثي قيمته لو كان المولى فقيراً ليس له مال إلا هو، وفي جميع قيمته لو كان المولى مديوناً ديناً يستغرق ماله لما ذكرنا أنه وصية ومحل نفاذها الثلث والدين مقدم عليها. اعلم أن المدبر في زمن سعايته كالمكاتب عند الإمام وعندهما حر مديون فتتفرع الأحكام فلا تقبل شهادته ولا يزوج نفسه عنده لما في المجمع من الجنايات ولو ترك مدبراً فقتل خطأ. وهو يسعى للوارث فعليه قيمته لوليه وقالا ديته على عاقلته ا هـ . وهكذا في الكافي وعلله بما ذكرناه. وكذا المنجز عتقه في مرض الموت إذا لم يخرج من الثلث فإنه في زمن سعايته كالمكاتب عنده فلا تقبل شهادته كما في شهادات البزازية وحكم جنايته كجناية المكاتب كما في شرح المجمع للمصنف. وقولهم هنا يعتق المدبر بموت المولى من ثلث المال يدل عليه فإن لم يخرج من الثلث لم يعتق حتى يسعى ويؤديها. قيدنا بكون الدين مستغرقاً لأن الدين لو كان أقل من ثلثه ويسعى في ثلثيه لو فقيراً أو كله لو مديوناً.
His saying: (And he pursues for two-thirds if [the master] is poor, or all if [the master] is in debt) i.e., the mudabbar pursues payment for the heirs for two-thirds of his value if the master was poor and had no wealth except him; and for all of his value if the master was in debt with debt covering all his wealth, because, as we mentioned, it is a will and its enforcement is from the third, and debt takes precedence over it. Know that the mudabbar during the period of his pursuit is like a mukatib according to Imam [Abu Hanifa], while according to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] he is a free man in debt. Therefore, legal branches follow: his testimony is not accepted, and he cannot marry himself according to the Imam, as in al-Majma' from the criminal cases. If he leaves a mudabbar who kills accidentally while he is pursuing payment for the heir, then he owes his value to his guardian; according to the two, his blood money is on his 'aqlah. This is in al-Kafi, and he gave the reason as we mentioned. Likewise, the one whose manumission is executed during terminal illness if it does not come from the third, he is during his pursuit like a mukatib according to the Imam, so his testimony is not accepted, as in Shahadat al-Bazzaziyya, and the ruling of his crime is like that of a mukatib, as in Sharh al-Majma' by the author. And their statement here 'the mudabbar is freed upon the master's death from one-third of the estate' indicates this: if it does not come from the third, he is not freed until he pursues and pays it. We restricted it to the debt being covering all because if the debt is less than one-third, then he pursues for two-thirds if poor, or all if in debt.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This is an expansion probe on the matn regarding the mudabbar's sa'yah (pursuit of value). If the master dies poor (with no other wealth) or in debt exceeding the estate, the mudabbar must work and pay his value to the heirs (if poor, two-thirds; if in debt, full value) because the will (tadbir) only takes effect from the third, and debts take priority. The commentator then discusses the status of the mudabbar during the period of sa'yah. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, he is like a mukatib (a slave contracted to buy his freedom) — thus his testimony is not accepted, and he cannot marry himself. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, he is a free man in debt, so his testimony is accepted and he can marry. The difference affects criminal liability: if he kills accidentally while pursuing, according to Abu Hanifa he owes his value to the guardian; according to the two, the blood money is on his 'aqlah (tribal group). The same ruling applies to one manumitted in terminal illness if the bequest exceeds the third. The text clarifies that the mudabbar is not freed until he pays. The condition of debt being covering all is to distinguish when he must pay all vs. two-thirds.

Key Terms
سعي (sa'y): The mudabbar's effort to earn and pay his value to the heirs when the estate is insufficient.
مكاتب (mukatib): A slave who has contracted with his master to purchase his freedom by installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 100 dirhams
B) 50 dirhams
C) 66.67 dirhams
D) 33.33 dirhams

Key Takeaway
The mudabbar's obligation to pay is determined by the master's financial status: two-thirds if poor, full value if in debt.

The Legal Status of the Mudabbar During Sa'yah: Abu Hanifa vs. the Two
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mudabbar's status during sa'yah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the mudabbar's status during sa'yah and identify the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
During sa'yah, Abu Hanifa treats the mudabbar like a mukatib; the two treat him as a free debtor — affecting testimony, marriage, and liability.

This passage extends the previous lesson by delving into the intra-Hanafi disagreement on the mudabbar's legal capacity while he is pursuing payment. Imam Abu Hanifa equates him to a mukatib (a slave under contract), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider him a free man burdened by a debt. This difference leads to distinct rulings on testimony, marriage, and accidental killing.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's position
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the mudabbar during sa'yah is like a mukatib: he remains partially enslaved.
This affects his capacity to testify and marry.
2
State the Sahibayn's position
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the mudabbar is a free man who owes a debt.
Thus, he retains full legal capacity except for the obligation to pay.
3
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequences: testimony (accepted or not), marriage (permits himself or needs master's permission?), and accidental killing (pays value vs. 'aqlah).
These differences matter in actual cases.
4
Note the parallel for manumission in terminal illness
The same dispute applies to one who is manumitted during terminal illness if the bequest exceeds the third.
He is treated like a mukatib according to Abu Hanifa during his sa'yah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ويسعى في ثلثيه لو فقيراً أو كله لو مديوناً) أي يسعى المدبر للورثة في ثلثي قيمته لو كان المولى فقيراً ليس له مال إلا هو، وفي جميع قيمته لو كان المولى مديوناً ديناً يستغرق ماله لما ذكرنا أنه وصية ومحل نفاذها الثلث والدين مقدم عليها. اعلم أن المدبر في زمن سعايته كالمكاتب عند الإمام وعندهما حر مديون فتتفرع الأحكام فلا تقبل شهادته ولا يزوج نفسه عنده لما في المجمع من الجنايات ولو ترك مدبراً فقتل خطأ. وهو يسعى للوارث فعليه قيمته لوليه وقالا ديته على عاقلته ا هـ . وهكذا في الكافي وعلله بما ذكرناه. وكذا المنجز عتقه في مرض الموت إذا لم يخرج من الثلث فإنه في زمن سعايته كالمكاتب عنده فلا تقبل شهادته كما في شهادات البزازية وحكم جنايته كجناية المكاتب كما في شرح المجمع للمصنف. وقولهم هنا يعتق المدبر بموت المولى من ثلث المال يدل عليه فإن لم يخرج من الثلث لم يعتق حتى يسعى ويؤديها. قيدنا بكون الدين مستغرقاً لأن الدين لو كان أقل من ثلثه ويسعى في ثلثيه لو فقيراً أو كله لو مديوناً.
His saying: (And he pursues for two-thirds if [the master] is poor, or all if [the master] is in debt) i.e., the mudabbar pursues payment for the heirs for two-thirds of his value if the master was poor and had no wealth except him; and for all of his value if the master was in debt with debt covering all his wealth, because, as we mentioned, it is a will and its enforcement is from the third, and debt takes precedence over it. Know that the mudabbar during the period of his pursuit is like a mukatib according to Imam [Abu Hanifa], while according to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] he is a free man in debt. Therefore, legal branches follow: his testimony is not accepted, and he cannot marry himself according to the Imam, as in al-Majma' from the criminal cases. If he leaves a mudabbar who kills accidentally while he is pursuing payment for the heir, then he owes his value to his guardian; according to the two, his blood money is on his 'aqlah. This is in al-Kafi, and he gave the reason as we mentioned. Likewise, the one whose manumission is executed during terminal illness if it does not come from the third, he is during his pursuit like a mukatib according to the Imam, so his testimony is not accepted, as in Shahadat al-Bazzaziyya, and the ruling of his crime is like that of a mukatib, as in Sharh al-Majma' by the author. And their statement here 'the mudabbar is freed upon the master's death from one-third of the estate' indicates this: if it does not come from the third, he is not freed until he pursues and pays it. We restricted it to the debt being covering all because if the debt is less than one-third, then he pursues for two-thirds if poor, or all if in debt.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This is an expansion probe on the matn regarding the mudabbar's sa'yah (pursuit of value). If the master dies poor (with no other wealth) or in debt exceeding the estate, the mudabbar must work and pay his value to the heirs (if poor, two-thirds; if in debt, full value) because the will (tadbir) only takes effect from the third, and debts take priority. The commentator then discusses the status of the mudabbar during the period of sa'yah. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, he is like a mukatib (a slave contracted to buy his freedom) — thus his testimony is not accepted, and he cannot marry himself. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, he is a free man in debt, so his testimony is accepted and he can marry. The difference affects criminal liability: if he kills accidentally while pursuing, according to Abu Hanifa he owes his value to the guardian; according to the two, the blood money is on his 'aqlah (tribal group). The same ruling applies to one manumitted in terminal illness if the bequest exceeds the third. The text clarifies that the mudabbar is not freed until he pays. The condition of debt being covering all is to distinguish when he must pay all vs. two-thirds.

Key Terms
سعي (sa'y): The mudabbar's effort to earn and pay his value to the heirs when the estate is insufficient.
مكاتب (mukatib): A slave who has contracted with his master to purchase his freedom by installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why is the mudabbar's testimony not accepted during sa'yah?
A) Because he is a debtor and debtors cannot testify.
B) Because he is like a mukatib, and slaves' testimony is not accepted.
C) Because he is not a free man until he pays.
D) Because he is under suspicion of fraud.

Key Takeaway
The dispute on mudabbar's status during sa'yah highlights the interaction between slavery and debt, with practical effects on testimony, marriage, and liability.

The Prohibition on Selling Umm Walad and the Effect of a Judge's Ruling
Discussion of prohibitions and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the prohibition on selling an umm walad and explain why a judge's ruling permitting sale is not executed according to the relied-upon fatwa.

Core Idea
An umm walad cannot be sold; even a judge's ruling cannot overturn the Companions' consensus.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a critical fiqh issue: the absolute prohibition on selling an umm walad. The master retains ownership for other purposes, but any transaction that would transfer her ownership is void. A further dispute arises: if a judge rules the sale valid, is the ruling executed? The Hanafi school has an internal difference, and the fatwa adopts Imam Muhammad's view that the ruling is ineffective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited transactions
The text states that an umm walad cannot be sold, gifted, pawned, or removed from ownership in any way. These are actions that would transfer or encumber ownership.
The prohibition is absolute and based on the special status of the umm walad.
2
Determine permissible uses
The master can have intercourse, use her for service, hire her out, or marry her off. These do not cancel the right of the umm walad to become free at his death.
Ownership remains for all other purposes.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, if a judge permits the sale, the ruling is executed; according to Muhammad, it is not executed.
This is a difference in usul al-fiqh.
4
Analyze the underlying usul principle
The dispute hinges on whether a later consensus (ijma' muta'akhkhir) can abrogate an earlier consensus (ijma' mutaqaddim). Muhammad says it cannot, because that would imply error in the Sahaba's consensus.
Hanafi usul: earlier consensus is definitive.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The text states that the fatwa follows Muhammad's view: the judge's ruling is not executed, and the sale remains invalid.
This is the operative position for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأفاد بقوله «لم تملك» أنه لا يجوز بيعها ولا هبتها ولا إخراجها عن الملك بوجه وكذا لا يجوز رهنها وليس المراد أنها لم تملك لأحد لأنها باقية على ملك مولاها بدليل ما سيأتي من جواز وطئها. وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو قضی قاض بجواز بيعها لم ينفذ قضاؤه. قال في الخانية وهو أظهر الروايات. وفي الظهيرية : وإذا قضى القاضي بجواز بيع أم الولد نفذ قضاؤه في قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف، وفي قول محمد لا يجوز بناء على المسألة الأصولية أن الإجماع المتأخر هل يرفع المتقدم؟ عندهما لا يرفع لما فيه من تضليل بعض الصحابة، وعند محمد يرفع، والفتوى على قول محمد في هذه المسألة أنه لا ينفذ قضاؤه اهـ. (وتوطأ وتستخدم وتؤجر وتزوج لأن الملك قائم فيها فأشبهت المدبرة، فكل تصرف يبطل هذا الحق فإنه لا يجوز فيها وما لا يبطله فهو جائز. وأفاد بالوطء والاستخدام أن الكسب والغلة والعقر والمهر للمولى لأنها بدل المنفعة والمنافع على ملكه، وكذا ملك العين قائم.
And he [the author] indicated by his saying 'she is not owned' that it is not permissible to sell her, nor to give her as a gift, nor to remove her from ownership in any way; likewise it is not permissible to pawn her. And the meaning is not that she is not owned by anyone, because she remains in the ownership of her master, as evidenced by what will come about the permissibility of having intercourse with her. And the author indicated that if a judge ruled that her sale is permissible, his ruling would not be executed. He said in al-Khaniyya: 'And it is the most apparent of the narrations.' And in al-Zahiraiyya: 'When a judge rules that the sale of an umm walad is permissible, his ruling is executed according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf; but according to Imam Muhammad it is not permissible, based on the fundamental principle of whether a later consensus can abrogate an earlier one. According to them [Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf], it cannot abrogate, because that would imply accusing some Companions of error; according to Muhammad, it can abrogate. And the fatwa in this matter is on the saying of Imam Muhammad, that his ruling is not executed.' (She is penetrated, used for service, hired out, and married, because the right of ownership remains in her, so she resembles a mudabbara. Thus, every transaction that would cancel this right [of being umm walad] is not permissible with respect to her, and what does not cancel it is permissible. He indicated by 'penetration and service' that her earnings, produce, indemnity (for injury), and dower belong to the master, because these are compensations for benefit, and the benefits are within his ownership; likewise, the ownership of the object [the slave herself] remains.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal status of an umm walad once she has attained that status. The core rule: she cannot be sold, gifted, or removed from her master's ownership in any way (including pawning). This is because she is irrevocably tied to her master's ownership until his death, at which point she becomes free. However, the master retains full ownership for all other purposes: he can have intercourse with her (since she remains his concubine), use her for service, hire her out for labor, and even marry her off to another man. These actions are permissible because they do not contradict the essential right of the umm walad—her freedom upon his death. If a transaction would invalidate that right (like sale), it is prohibited. The passage then addresses an important legal dispute: if a judge (qadi) decides to permit the sale of an umm walad, is that ruling executed? According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, if a judge issues such a ruling, it is executed and takes effect, because the property rights of the master are not absolutely nullified—there is a legal mechanism (ijtihad) that could potentially allow it. However, Imam Muhammad holds that such a ruling is not executed. His reasoning is based on an usul al-fiqh principle: the Companions (sahaba) had consensus (ijma) that an umm walad cannot be sold. Later consensus cannot abrogate earlier consensus because that would accuse the earlier Companions of error. Therefore, any ruling contradicting that consensus is void. The fatwa is according to Imam Muhammad—that is, the judge's ruling is not executed, and the sale is invalid. The passage affirms that earnings from hiring, indemnity, and dower fall to the master, as he still owns the benefits and the slave.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master; she cannot be sold and becomes free upon her master's death.
قضاء القاضي (qada' al-qadi): A judge's ruling; in this context, whether it can overturn an established prohibition.
إجماع متأخر ومتقدم (ijma' muta'akhkhir wa mutaqaddim): Later consensus vs. earlier consensus; a usul al-fiqh issue about whether later consensus can abrogate earlier.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fatwa in the text, what happens if a judge rules that an umm walad can be sold?
A) The ruling is executed and the sale is valid.
B) The ruling is not executed and the sale remains invalid.
C) The ruling is executed only if the master agrees.
D) The ruling is void automatically.

Key Takeaway
An umm walad cannot be sold, and even a judge's ruling cannot validly permit it because the Companions' consensus on the prohibition is irrevocable.

Basic Ruling of 'Every Lawful Thing is Forbidden to Me' in Oaths
Explains the basic ruling and the shift from oath to divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the phrase 'kullu hillin 'alayya haram' according to Zahir al-Riwayah and the mufta bihi, and explain the role of intention.

Core Idea
The phrase can be an oath requiring expiation or divorce depending on intention and custom.

This phrase is a common verbal declaration whose legal effect depends heavily on the speaker's intention and prevalent custom. The Hanafi school has a nuanced position: originally it was treated as a verbal oath, but later fatwa shifted to automatic divorce when a wife exists due to custom. You will learn the difference between the classical transmitted ruling and the operative fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and its ambiguity
The phrase 'kullu hillin 'alayya haram' is a general declaration that can be interpreted as an oath, divorce, or nothing. The first step is to note that its legal effect is not fixed but context-dependent.
This is the starting point of the discussion.
2
State the Zahir al-Riwayah position
According to the core transmitted books, the phrase is treated as a verbal oath (yamin) that does not affect the wife unless specifically intended. Breaking the oath requires expiation (kaffarah). No divorce occurs without explicit intention of divorce.
This is the default ruling from Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
3
Explain the mufta bihi shift
Later Hanafi authorities, based on prevalent custom, ruled that if the speaker has a wife, the wife becomes divorced without intention. This is because the phrase has become widely used to mean divorce. This shift is reflected in al-Hidayah and other texts.
The mufta bihi overrides Zahir al-Riwayah due to custom ('urf).
4
Distinguish the conditions
If the speaker has no wife, the phrase remains a verbal oath. If he intends divorce, it is divorce. If he intends something else (e.g., lying), nothing is binding. The presence of a wife and the prevalence of custom are key determinants.
Intention is still relevant but custom can override lack of intention.
5
Apply to a scenario
Given a man with a wife who says the phrase without any specific intention, according to the mufta bihi, his wife is divorced by one revocable talaq. According to Zahir al-Riwayah, it would only be an oath.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان
زوجته لإسقاط اعتبار العموم، وإذا نواها كان إيلاء ولا تصرف اليمين عن المأكول والمشروب، وهذا كله جواب ظاهر الرواية ؛ كذا في الهداية مع أن عبارة الحاكم في الكافي : إذا قال الرجل كل حل علي حرام سئل عن نيته، فإن نوى يميناً فهو يمين يكفرها ولا تدخل امرأته في ذلك إلا أن ينوي فإن نواها دخلت، فإن أكل أو شرب أو قرب امرأته حنث وسقط عنه الإيلاء وإن لم يكن له نية فهو يمين يكفرها لا تدخل امرأته فيها، ولو نوى به الطلاق فالقول فيه كالقول في الحرام أي يصح ما نوى وإن نوى الكذب فهو كذب ا هـ . تقتضي أن الأمر موقوف على النية وأنه لو نوى الكذب لا يلزمه شيء وهو غير مستفاد من عبارة الهداية كما لا يخفى قوله : والفتوى على أنه تبين امرأته من غير نية لغلبة الاستعمال ؛ كذا في الهداية. وإن لم تكن له امرأة ذكر في النهاية معزياً إلى النوازل أنه يحنث وعليه الكفارة يعني إذا أكل أو شرب لانصرافه عند عدم الزوجة إلى الطعام والشراب لا كما يفهم من ظاهر العبارة. وقال البزدوي في مبسوطه : هكذا قال بعض مشايخ سمرقند ولم يتضح لي عرف الناس في هذا لأن . لا امرأة له يحلف به كما يحلف ذو الحليلة، ولو كان العرف مستفيضاً في ذلك لما استعمله إلا ذو الحليلة، فالصحيح أن يقيد الجواب في هذا فنقول : إن
اه.
من
نوى الطلاق يكون طلاقاً، فأما من غير دلالة فالاحتياط أن يقف الإنسان فيه ولا يخالف المتقدمين. واعلم أن مثل هذا اللفظ لم يتعارف في ديارنا بل المتعارف فيه «حرام علي كلامك» ونحوه كأكل كذا ولبسه دون الصيغة العامة، وتعارفوا أيضاً الحرام يلزمني ولا شك في أنهم يريدون الطلاق معلقاً فإنهم يزيدون بعده لا أفعل كذا ولأفعلن وهو مثل تعارفهم الطلاق يلزمني لا أفعل كذا فإنه يراد إن فعلت كذا فهي طالق ويجب إمضاؤه عليهم. والحاصل أن المعتبر في انصراف هذه الألفاظ عربية كانت أو فارسية إلى معنى بلا نية التعارف فيه، فإن لم
يخفى على المتأمل قوله : (يعني إذا أكل أو شرب الخ) مخالف لما سيأتي عن الظهيرية من أنه لو لم تكن له امرأة ثم تزوج امرأة ثم باشر الشرط الفتوى على أنه لا تبين لأنه يمينه جعل يميناً بالله تعالى الخ. ولكن ينبغي تقييد هذا بما إذا حلف على أمر من المستقبل وإلا فلا يلزمه شيء كما يأتي في عبارة الظهيرية أيضاً. وفي البزازية قال : وفي المواضع التي يقع الطلاق بلفظ الحرام إن لم تكن له امرأة إن حنث لزمته الكفارة والنسفي على أنه لا تلزمه ا هـ . قلت : والظاهر حمل كلام النسفي على ما إذا لم يكن حلفه على مستقبل فلا ينافي ما قبله والحاصل أنه إذا قال كل حل علي حرام وسكت أو قال إن كنت فعلت كذا الأمر فعله لا يلزمه شيء إذا لم تكن له امرأة وإن قال إن فعلت كذا في المستقبل لزمه كفارة بالحنث هذا كله بناء على تغير العرف من انصرافه إلى الطلاق بعد ما كان العرف قبله في انصرافه إلى الطعام والشراب، فما ذكره المؤلف في تأويل عبارة النهاية مخالفة لكلامهم والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم قوله : (وقال البزدوي في مبسوطه الخ) قال الرملي في حاشيته على المنح أقول: ما بحثه
........
يتعارف سئل عن نيته وفيما ينصرف بلا نية لو قال أردت غيره لا يصدقه القاضي وفيما بينه وبين الله تعالى وهو مصدق ؛ هكذا قال في فتح القدير والحاصل أنه على ظاهر الرواية يحنث بالأكل والشرب فقط ولا يقع عليه ،طلاق، وعلى المفتى به إن لم يكن له امرأة فكذلك، وإن كان له امرأة وقع الطلاق عليها ولا يحنث بالأكل والشرب. وفي الظهيرية: رجل قال كل حل علي حرام أو قال كل حلال علي حرام أو قال حلال الله أو قال حلال المسلمين وله امرأة ولم ينو شيئاً قال الشيخ الإمام أبو بكر محمد بن الفضل والفقيه أبو جعفر وأبو بكر الاسكاف وأبو بكر بن سعيد تبين امرأته بتطليقة وإن نوى ثلاثاً فثلاث، وإن قال لم أنو الطلاق لا يصدق قضاء لأنه صار طلاقاً عرفاً ولهذا لا يحلف به إلا الرجال فإن كان له امرأة واحدة تبين بتطليقة، وإن كن ثلاثاً أو أربعاً يقع على كل واحدة واحدة بائنة، وإن حلف بهذا اللفظ إن كان فعل كذا وقد كان فعل وله امرأة واحدة أو أكثرين جميعاً وإن لم يكن له امرأة لا يلزمه شيء لأنه جعل يميناً بالطلاق، ولو جعلناه يميناً بالله فهو غموس. وإن حلف بهذا على أمر في المستقبل ففعل ذلك وليس له امرأة كان عليه الكفارة لأن تحريم الحلال يمين، وإن كان له امرأة وقت اليمين فماتت قبل الشرط أو بانت لا إلى عدة ثم باشر الشرط لا تلزمه الكفارة لأن يمينه انصرف إلى الطلاق وقت وجودها وإن لم تكن له امرأة وقت اليمين ثم تزوج امرأة ثم باشر الشرط اختلفوا فيه قال الفقيه أبو جعفر : تبين المتزوجة وقال غيره : لا تبين وبه أخذ الفقيه أبو الليث وعليه الفتوى لأن يمينه جعل يميناً بالله تعالى وقت وجودها فلا يكون طلاقاً بعد ذلك اهـ. وقيد بصيغة العموم لأنه لو قال لزوجته أنت علي حرام فقد قدم في باب الإيلاء أنه ينصرف للزوجة فتطلق من غير نية.
جيد موافق لكلام المتقدمين ويحمل كلام صاحب الهداية وغيره على ما إذا لم يكن الاستعمال مشتركاً فيه وفي غيره، أما إذا كان مشتركاً تعين موافقة المتقدمين. وأقول : أكثر عوام بلادنا لا يقصدون بقولهم أنت محرمة علي أو حرام علي أو حرمتك علي إلا حرمة الوطء المقابل لحله ولذلك أكثرهم يضرب مدة لتحريمها ولا يريد قطعاً إلا تحريم الجماع إلى هذه المدة، ولا شك أنه يمين موجب للإيلاء تأمل. فقل من حقق هذه المسألة على وجهها وانظر إلى قولهم لا نقول لا تشترط النية لكن يجعل ناوياً عرفاً فهو صريح في اعتبار العرف فإن لم يكن العرف كذلك بل كان مشتركاً تعين اعتبار النية وتصديق الحالف كما هو مذهب المتقدمين قوله : (وإن كن ثلاثاً أو أربعاً يقع على كل واحدة واحدة بائنة) قال في النهر بعده لكن في الدراية : لو كان له امرأتان وقع الطلاق على واحدة وإليه البيان في الأظهر كقوله امرأتي كذا وله امرأتان أو أكثر ا.هـ. قال محشي مسكين: ظاهر قوله «أو أكثر أن وقوع الطلاق على واحدة وإليه البيان لا يخص الثنتين بل كذلك لو كن ثلاثاً أو أربعاً فهو قول مقابل لما في الظهيرية، وحيث كان وقوع الطلاق على واحدة وإليه البيان هو الأظهر مطلقاً، سواء كان له امرأتان أو أكثر، فما في الظهيرية من وقوعه على الكل خلاف الأظهر وإن كان في
Book of Oaths: his wife to drop consideration of generality, and if he intends it it becomes ila' and the oath does not direct to food and drink, and all this is the answer of Zahir al-Riwayah; thus in al-Hidayah even though the expression of al-Hakim in al-Kafi: if a man says 'every permissible thing is forbidden to me' he is asked about his intention, if he intends an oath it is an oath he expiates and his wife does not enter into that unless he intends her, then if he intends her she enters, if he eats or drinks or approaches his wife he breaks the oath and ila' falls from him, and if he has no intention it is an oath he expiates, his wife does not enter into it, and if he intends divorce by it the statement is as the statement in 'al-haram' i.e. what he intended is valid, and if he intends lying it is a lie etc. It necessitates that the matter depends on intention and that if he intends lying nothing is binding on him, and this is not derived from the expression of al-Hidayah as is not hidden. His statement: and the fatwa is that his wife becomes separated from him without intention due to prevalence of usage; thus in al-Hidayah. And if he has no wife, it is mentioned in al-Nihayah attributing to al-Nawazil that he breaks the oath and expiation is upon him, i.e. if he eats or drinks because when there is no wife it turns to food and drink, not as understood from the apparent expression. And al-Bazdawi said in his Mabsoot: thus said some shaykhs of Samarkand and it is not clear to me the custom of people in this because he who has no wife swears by it as does the one with a wife, and if custom were widespread in that, only the one with a wife would use it, so the correct view is to qualify the answer in this and we say: if he intends divorce it becomes divorce, but without indication the caution is to stop in it and not to oppose the predecessors. And know that such a phrase is not customary in our lands, rather the customary phrase is 'haram upon me your speech' and the like, like eating such and such and wearing it without the general formula, and they also customarily say 'al-haram is binding on me' and there is no doubt that they intend suspended divorce, for they add after it 'I will not do such and such' or 'I will certainly do' and it is like their custom of 'divorce is binding on me I will not do such and such' for it is intended: if I do such and such she is divorced, and it must be enforced upon them. The conclusion is that what is considered in the turning of these expressions, whether Arabic or Persian, to a meaning without intention is custom in it. If it is not hidden from the contemplator, his statement (i.e. if he eats or drinks etc.) contradicts what will come from al-Zahiriyyah that if he did not have a wife then married a woman then the condition occurred, the fatwa is that she does not become separated because his oath is made an oath by Allah etc. But this should be restricted to when he swore about a future matter, otherwise nothing is binding on him as also comes in the expression of al-Zahiriyyah. And in al-Bazzaziyyah it says: and in the places where divorce occurs by the word 'al-haram', if he has no wife, if he breaks the oath expiation is binding on him, and al-Nasafi is of the opinion that it is not binding on him etc. I say: the apparent view is to interpret al-Nasafi's words as referring to when he did not swear about the future, so it does not contradict what preceded. The conclusion is that if he says 'every permissible thing is forbidden to me' and remains silent or says 'if I did such and such thing' he did it, nothing is binding on him if he has no wife, and if he says 'if I do such and such in the future' then breaking the oath makes expiation binding. All this is based on the change of custom from its turning to divorce after the custom before was its turning to food and drink. So what the author mentioned in interpreting the expression of al-Nihayah contradicts their words, and Allah knows best. His statement: (and al-Bazdawi in his Mabsoot etc.) al-Ramli said in his Hashiyah on al-Manh: I say: what he researched... it is customary, he is asked about his intention, and in what turns without intention if he says 'I intended otherwise' the judge does not accept him, but between him and Allah Almighty he is believed; thus in Fath al-Qadir. The conclusion is that according to Zahir al-Riwayah he breaks the oath only by eating and drinking and divorce does not occur, and according to the mufta bihi if he has no wife it is the same, and if he has a wife, divorce falls upon her and he does not break the oath by eating and drinking. And in al-Zahiriyyah: a man said 'every permissible thing is forbidden to me' or said 'every lawful thing is forbidden to me' or said 'the lawful of Allah' or said 'the lawful of the Muslims' and he has a wife and did not intend anything, said Shaykh Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl and al-Faqih Abu Ja'far and Abu Bakr al-Iskaf and Abu Bakr ibn Sa'eed: his wife becomes separated by one revocable divorce, and if he intended three then three, and if he said 'I did not intend divorce' he is not believed in court because it has become divorce by custom, and therefore only men swear by it. If he has one wife she becomes separated by one revocable divorce, and if they are three or four, an irrevocable divorce occurs on each one. And if he swore by this expression 'if I did such and such' and he had done it and he has one wife or more, together, and if he has no wife nothing is binding on him because he made it an oath by divorce, and if we make it an oath by Allah it is a false oath (ghamoos). And if he swore by this about a future matter then did it and he has no wife, expiation is upon him because forbidding the lawful is an oath. And if he had a wife at the time of the oath, and she died before the condition or separated without a waiting period, then the condition occurred, expiation is not binding on him because his oath turned to divorce at the time of her existence. And if he had no wife at the time of the oath, then married a woman, then the condition occurred, they differed. Al-Faqih Abu Ja'far said: the married woman becomes separated. Others said: she does not become separated, and al-Faqih Abu al-Layth took this and the fatwa is upon it because his oath is made an oath by Allah at the time of its occurrence, so it is not divorce after that. End. And it is restricted to the general formula because if he said to his wife 'you are forbidden to me' it has preceded in the chapter of Ila' that it turns to the wife and she is divorced without intention. Good, agreeing with the words of the predecessors, and the words of the author of al-Hidayah and others are interpreted as when the usage is not common in it and in other than it, but if it is common, then agreeing with the predecessors is determined. And I say: most common people in our lands do not intend by their saying 'you are forbidden to me' or 'haram upon me' or 'I have forbidden you to me' except the prohibition of intercourse corresponding to its permissibility, and therefore most of them set a period for her prohibition and do not intend certainly except prohibition of intercourse until that period, and there is no doubt that it is an oath necessitating ila'. Ponder. So few have investigated this issue properly. And look at their saying 'We do not say that intention is not required, but he is considered as intending customarily' - it is explicit in considering custom. If custom is not like that but rather common, then considering intention and believing the swearer is determined as is the madhhab of the predecessors. His statement: (and if they are three or four, an irrevocable divorce occurs on each one) said in al-Nahr after it: but in al-Dirayah: if he has two wives, divorce falls on one and the clarification is given to him in the more apparent view, like his saying 'my wife is such' and he has two wives or more. End. The commentator Miskin said: the apparent meaning of his words 'or more' is that the occurrence of divorce on one and the clarification is given to him is not specific to two, but also if they are three or four, so it is an opposing statement to what is in al-Zahiriyyah, and since the occurrence of divorce on one and the clarification is given to him is the more apparent view absolutely, whether he has two wives or more, what is in al-Zahiriyyah of occurrence on all is contrary to the more apparent view, even if it is in
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discusses the legal ruling of the phrase 'کل حل علی حرام' (every permissible thing is forbidden to me) in the context of oaths (al-aiman). The passage type is an expansion probe, detailing the positions of Zahir al-Riwayah, the mufta bihi (operative fatwa), and intra-Hanafi disputes, with a focus on the role of intention and custom ('urf).

The author begins by stating that according to Zahir al-Riwayah, when a man says this phrase, he is asked about his intention. If he intends an oath, it is a verbal oath (yamin) requiring expiation (kaffarah), and it does not affect his wife unless he specifically intends her. If he intends his wife, it becomes ila' (a vow of abstinence). The oath does not turn to food and drink; only if he eats or drinks or approaches his wife does he break the oath (hanth), and ila' falls. If he has no intention, it is still an oath requiring expiation, and the wife is not included. If he intends divorce, it is valid as an expression of divorce (talaq), and if he intends lying, it is a lie but nothing is binding. This is from al-Hakim's al-Kafi, and the text notes that al-Hidayah implies the same, but the mufta bihi position is that the wife becomes separated (tabeen) without intention due to prevalent custom (ghalabat al-isti'mal), as in al-Hidayah.

The passage then discusses the case when the speaker has no wife. According to al-Nihayah citing al-Nawazil, if he eats or drinks, he breaks the oath and must give expiation, because without a wife the phrase turns to food and drink. However, al-Bazdawi questioned this: if custom is not widespread for those without wives, the correct approach is to say that if he intends divorce it is divorce, but without indication, one should be cautious and not oppose the predecessors. Later, the author notes that such phrases are not customary in their lands; the common phrases are 'haram upon me your speech' etc., which are used for suspended divorce. The conclusion is that the turning of these expressions without intention depends on custom. If custom is not established, then intention is considered.

The passage then reconciles apparent contradictions: according to al-Zahiriyyah, if someone had no wife at the time of the oath but later married and then fulfilled the condition, the fatwa is that the wife does not become separated because the oath is considered an oath by Allah. This is restricted to oaths about future matters; for past actions, nothing is binding if there is no wife. Al-Nasafi holds that no expiation is required at all in such cases, but this is understood as referring to past actions. Thus, the key distinction is between past and future: if he says 'كل حل علي حرام' and remains silent or refers to a past action, nothing is binding if he has no wife; if it is about a future action, breaking it requires expiation. This reversal is due to change in custom: earlier custom turned the phrase to food and drink, later to divorce.

The text then presents a substantial quote from al-Zahiriyyah summarizing the fatwa: if a man says the phrase and has a wife without intention, the wife is divorced by one revocable talaq, and if he intends three, three occur. In court, his denial of intention is not accepted because it has become divorce by custom. If he has one wife, she gets one talaq; if three or four, each gets a ba'in (irrevocable) talaq. If he swears about a past action and has a wife, divorce occurs; if no wife, nothing. For future actions without a wife, expiation is due. If he had a wife at the time of oath but she died or separated before the condition, no expiation; if he had no wife then married later, there is a dispute: Abu Ja'far says she is divorced, but the fatwa is with Abu al-Layth that she is not, because the oath is considered an oath by Allah at the time of the condition.

The passage ends with a discussion of the phrase 'أنت علي حرام' (you are forbidden to me) said to a specific wife. It was stated earlier in Bab al-Ila' that this turns to the wife and she is divorced without intention. The author then gives his own observation that common people in his land use such phrases only to prohibit intercourse temporarily, making it an oath of ila', not divorce. He emphasizes that custom must be clear; if the usage is common (mushtarak), then intention must be considered and the swearer believed, as per the earlier scholars.

Finally, a note from al-Nahr and al-Dirayah mentions a dispute about multiple wives: some say divorce falls on all, but the more apparent view (al-azhar) is that it falls on only one wife, and clarification is given to him, similar to saying 'my wife is such' when having multiple wives. This opposes the ruling in al-Zahiriyyah that divorce occurs on all.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The well-known transmitted positions from the six core books of Imam Muhammad.
الفتوى (al-fatwa): The operative ruling given to questioners, which may differ from Zahir al-Riwayah due to custom or need.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi, what happens when a married man says 'kullu hillin 'alayya haram' without any intention?
A) It is a verbal oath requiring expiation.
B) Nothing occurs because intention is required.
C) His wife is divorced by one talaq.
D) It becomes ila'.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on 'kullu hillin 'alayya haram' shifts from an oath (Zahir al-Riwayah) to automatic divorce (mufta bihi) when a wife is present, due to custom.

10 min
Ruling When the Speaker Has No Wife: Oath or Nothing?
Focuses on the no-wife scenario and the distinction between past and future

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between future and past conditions in the phrase 'kullu hillin 'alayya haram' when the speaker has no wife, and apply the correct ruling: expiation or nothing.

Core Idea
Without a wife, the phrase is a verbal oath only if it concerns a future action; otherwise nothing is binding.

When a man has no wife, the phrase cannot turn to divorce, so it defaults to an oath. But there is a further nuance: if he swears about a past action (e.g., 'if I did such and such'), it is a false oath (ghamoos) and nothing is required. If about a future action, breaking it requires expiation. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario: no wife
The first condition is that the speaker has no wife at the time of uttering the phrase. This prevents the phrase from being treated as divorce.
The mufta bihi only gives automatic divorce to married men.
2
Distinguish between past and future conditions
The phrase often occurs in a conditional form: 'if I did X' (past) or 'if I do X' (future). The ruling differs based on this temporal aspect.
This distinction is central to the discussion in the passage.
3
Apply the ruling for past conditions
If the speaker says the phrase about a past action that is true (or false), it is a false oath (yamīn ghamūs) if he intends an oath. Since it is a lie, no expiation is required; it is a sin. But if he intends divorce, it is ineffective because no wife exists.
The passage states that if he has no wife and swears about a past action, nothing is binding.
4
Apply the ruling for future conditions
If the speaker says the phrase about a future action, it is a valid oath. If he performs the action (hanth), he must give expiation (kaffarah).
This is the majority view; some scholars (like al-Nasafi) say nothing is binding, but the text reconciles that as referring to past.
5
Consider the scenario of later marriage
If he has no wife at the time of the oath, then marries, and later the condition occurs, there is a dispute: some say the new wife is divorced, but the fatwa (Abu al-Layth) says no divorce, only expiation.
This is covered in more detail in Lesson 3.
