كتاب الأيمان
زوجته لإسقاط اعتبار العموم، وإذا نواها كان إيلاء ولا تصرف اليمين عن المأكول والمشروب، وهذا كله جواب ظاهر الرواية ؛ كذا في الهداية مع أن عبارة الحاكم في الكافي : إذا قال الرجل كل حل علي حرام سئل عن نيته، فإن نوى يميناً فهو يمين يكفرها ولا تدخل امرأته في ذلك إلا أن ينوي فإن نواها دخلت، فإن أكل أو شرب أو قرب امرأته حنث وسقط عنه الإيلاء وإن لم يكن له نية فهو يمين يكفرها لا تدخل امرأته فيها، ولو نوى به الطلاق فالقول فيه كالقول في الحرام أي يصح ما نوى وإن نوى الكذب فهو كذب ا هـ . تقتضي أن الأمر موقوف على النية وأنه لو نوى الكذب لا يلزمه شيء وهو غير مستفاد من عبارة الهداية كما لا يخفى قوله : والفتوى على أنه تبين امرأته من غير نية لغلبة الاستعمال ؛ كذا في الهداية. وإن لم تكن له امرأة ذكر في النهاية معزياً إلى النوازل أنه يحنث وعليه الكفارة يعني إذا أكل أو شرب لانصرافه عند عدم الزوجة إلى الطعام والشراب لا كما يفهم من ظاهر العبارة. وقال البزدوي في مبسوطه : هكذا قال بعض مشايخ سمرقند ولم يتضح لي عرف الناس في هذا لأن . لا امرأة له يحلف به كما يحلف ذو الحليلة، ولو كان العرف مستفيضاً في ذلك لما استعمله إلا ذو الحليلة، فالصحيح أن يقيد الجواب في هذا فنقول : إن
اه.
من
نوى الطلاق يكون طلاقاً، فأما من غير دلالة فالاحتياط أن يقف الإنسان فيه ولا يخالف المتقدمين. واعلم أن مثل هذا اللفظ لم يتعارف في ديارنا بل المتعارف فيه «حرام علي كلامك» ونحوه كأكل كذا ولبسه دون الصيغة العامة، وتعارفوا أيضاً الحرام يلزمني ولا شك في أنهم يريدون الطلاق معلقاً فإنهم يزيدون بعده لا أفعل كذا ولأفعلن وهو مثل تعارفهم الطلاق يلزمني لا أفعل كذا فإنه يراد إن فعلت كذا فهي طالق ويجب إمضاؤه عليهم. والحاصل أن المعتبر في انصراف هذه الألفاظ عربية كانت أو فارسية إلى معنى بلا نية التعارف فيه، فإن لم
يخفى على المتأمل قوله : (يعني إذا أكل أو شرب الخ) مخالف لما سيأتي عن الظهيرية من أنه لو لم تكن له امرأة ثم تزوج امرأة ثم باشر الشرط الفتوى على أنه لا تبين لأنه يمينه جعل يميناً بالله تعالى الخ. ولكن ينبغي تقييد هذا بما إذا حلف على أمر من المستقبل وإلا فلا يلزمه شيء كما يأتي في عبارة الظهيرية أيضاً. وفي البزازية قال : وفي المواضع التي يقع الطلاق بلفظ الحرام إن لم تكن له امرأة إن حنث لزمته الكفارة والنسفي على أنه لا تلزمه ا هـ . قلت : والظاهر حمل كلام النسفي على ما إذا لم يكن حلفه على مستقبل فلا ينافي ما قبله والحاصل أنه إذا قال كل حل علي حرام وسكت أو قال إن كنت فعلت كذا الأمر فعله لا يلزمه شيء إذا لم تكن له امرأة وإن قال إن فعلت كذا في المستقبل لزمه كفارة بالحنث هذا كله بناء على تغير العرف من انصرافه إلى الطلاق بعد ما كان العرف قبله في انصرافه إلى الطعام والشراب، فما ذكره المؤلف في تأويل عبارة النهاية مخالفة لكلامهم والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم قوله : (وقال البزدوي في مبسوطه الخ) قال الرملي في حاشيته على المنح أقول: ما بحثه
........
يتعارف سئل عن نيته وفيما ينصرف بلا نية لو قال أردت غيره لا يصدقه القاضي وفيما بينه وبين الله تعالى وهو مصدق ؛ هكذا قال في فتح القدير والحاصل أنه على ظاهر الرواية يحنث بالأكل والشرب فقط ولا يقع عليه ،طلاق، وعلى المفتى به إن لم يكن له امرأة فكذلك، وإن كان له امرأة وقع الطلاق عليها ولا يحنث بالأكل والشرب. وفي الظهيرية: رجل قال كل حل علي حرام أو قال كل حلال علي حرام أو قال حلال الله أو قال حلال المسلمين وله امرأة ولم ينو شيئاً قال الشيخ الإمام أبو بكر محمد بن الفضل والفقيه أبو جعفر وأبو بكر الاسكاف وأبو بكر بن سعيد تبين امرأته بتطليقة وإن نوى ثلاثاً فثلاث، وإن قال لم أنو الطلاق لا يصدق قضاء لأنه صار طلاقاً عرفاً ولهذا لا يحلف به إلا الرجال فإن كان له امرأة واحدة تبين بتطليقة، وإن كن ثلاثاً أو أربعاً يقع على كل واحدة واحدة بائنة، وإن حلف بهذا اللفظ إن كان فعل كذا وقد كان فعل وله امرأة واحدة أو أكثرين جميعاً وإن لم يكن له امرأة لا يلزمه شيء لأنه جعل يميناً بالطلاق، ولو جعلناه يميناً بالله فهو غموس. وإن حلف بهذا على أمر في المستقبل ففعل ذلك وليس له امرأة كان عليه الكفارة لأن تحريم الحلال يمين، وإن كان له امرأة وقت اليمين فماتت قبل الشرط أو بانت لا إلى عدة ثم باشر الشرط لا تلزمه الكفارة لأن يمينه انصرف إلى الطلاق وقت وجودها وإن لم تكن له امرأة وقت اليمين ثم تزوج امرأة ثم باشر الشرط اختلفوا فيه قال الفقيه أبو جعفر : تبين المتزوجة وقال غيره : لا تبين وبه أخذ الفقيه أبو الليث وعليه الفتوى لأن يمينه جعل يميناً بالله تعالى وقت وجودها فلا يكون طلاقاً بعد ذلك اهـ. وقيد بصيغة العموم لأنه لو قال لزوجته أنت علي حرام فقد قدم في باب الإيلاء أنه ينصرف للزوجة فتطلق من غير نية.
جيد موافق لكلام المتقدمين ويحمل كلام صاحب الهداية وغيره على ما إذا لم يكن الاستعمال مشتركاً فيه وفي غيره، أما إذا كان مشتركاً تعين موافقة المتقدمين. وأقول : أكثر عوام بلادنا لا يقصدون بقولهم أنت محرمة علي أو حرام علي أو حرمتك علي إلا حرمة الوطء المقابل لحله ولذلك أكثرهم يضرب مدة لتحريمها ولا يريد قطعاً إلا تحريم الجماع إلى هذه المدة، ولا شك أنه يمين موجب للإيلاء تأمل. فقل من حقق هذه المسألة على وجهها وانظر إلى قولهم لا نقول لا تشترط النية لكن يجعل ناوياً عرفاً فهو صريح في اعتبار العرف فإن لم يكن العرف كذلك بل كان مشتركاً تعين اعتبار النية وتصديق الحالف كما هو مذهب المتقدمين قوله : (وإن كن ثلاثاً أو أربعاً يقع على كل واحدة واحدة بائنة) قال في النهر بعده لكن في الدراية : لو كان له امرأتان وقع الطلاق على واحدة وإليه البيان في الأظهر كقوله امرأتي كذا وله امرأتان أو أكثر ا.هـ. قال محشي مسكين: ظاهر قوله «أو أكثر أن وقوع الطلاق على واحدة وإليه البيان لا يخص الثنتين بل كذلك لو كن ثلاثاً أو أربعاً فهو قول مقابل لما في الظهيرية، وحيث كان وقوع الطلاق على واحدة وإليه البيان هو الأظهر مطلقاً، سواء كان له امرأتان أو أكثر، فما في الظهيرية من وقوعه على الكل خلاف الأظهر وإن كان في
Book of Oaths: his wife to drop consideration of generality, and if he intends it it becomes ila' and the oath does not direct to food and drink, and all this is the answer of Zahir al-Riwayah; thus in al-Hidayah even though the expression of al-Hakim in al-Kafi: if a man says 'every permissible thing is forbidden to me' he is asked about his intention, if he intends an oath it is an oath he expiates and his wife does not enter into that unless he intends her, then if he intends her she enters, if he eats or drinks or approaches his wife he breaks the oath and ila' falls from him, and if he has no intention it is an oath he expiates, his wife does not enter into it, and if he intends divorce by it the statement is as the statement in 'al-haram' i.e. what he intended is valid, and if he intends lying it is a lie etc. It necessitates that the matter depends on intention and that if he intends lying nothing is binding on him, and this is not derived from the expression of al-Hidayah as is not hidden. His statement: and the fatwa is that his wife becomes separated from him without intention due to prevalence of usage; thus in al-Hidayah. And if he has no wife, it is mentioned in al-Nihayah attributing to al-Nawazil that he breaks the oath and expiation is upon him, i.e. if he eats or drinks because when there is no wife it turns to food and drink, not as understood from the apparent expression. And al-Bazdawi said in his Mabsoot: thus said some shaykhs of Samarkand and it is not clear to me the custom of people in this because he who has no wife swears by it as does the one with a wife, and if custom were widespread in that, only the one with a wife would use it, so the correct view is to qualify the answer in this and we say: if he intends divorce it becomes divorce, but without indication the caution is to stop in it and not to oppose the predecessors. And know that such a phrase is not customary in our lands, rather the customary phrase is 'haram upon me your speech' and the like, like eating such and such and wearing it without the general formula, and they also customarily say 'al-haram is binding on me' and there is no doubt that they intend suspended divorce, for they add after it 'I will not do such and such' or 'I will certainly do' and it is like their custom of 'divorce is binding on me I will not do such and such' for it is intended: if I do such and such she is divorced, and it must be enforced upon them. The conclusion is that what is considered in the turning of these expressions, whether Arabic or Persian, to a meaning without intention is custom in it. If it is not hidden from the contemplator, his statement (i.e. if he eats or drinks etc.) contradicts what will come from al-Zahiriyyah that if he did not have a wife then married a woman then the condition occurred, the fatwa is that she does not become separated because his oath is made an oath by Allah etc. But this should be restricted to when he swore about a future matter, otherwise nothing is binding on him as also comes in the expression of al-Zahiriyyah. And in al-Bazzaziyyah it says: and in the places where divorce occurs by the word 'al-haram', if he has no wife, if he breaks the oath expiation is binding on him, and al-Nasafi is of the opinion that it is not binding on him etc. I say: the apparent view is to interpret al-Nasafi's words as referring to when he did not swear about the future, so it does not contradict what preceded. The conclusion is that if he says 'every permissible thing is forbidden to me' and remains silent or says 'if I did such and such thing' he did it, nothing is binding on him if he has no wife, and if he says 'if I do such and such in the future' then breaking the oath makes expiation binding. All this is based on the change of custom from its turning to divorce after the custom before was its turning to food and drink. So what the author mentioned in interpreting the expression of al-Nihayah contradicts their words, and Allah knows best. His statement: (and al-Bazdawi in his Mabsoot etc.) al-Ramli said in his Hashiyah on al-Manh: I say: what he researched... it is customary, he is asked about his intention, and in what turns without intention if he says 'I intended otherwise' the judge does not accept him, but between him and Allah Almighty he is believed; thus in Fath al-Qadir. The conclusion is that according to Zahir al-Riwayah he breaks the oath only by eating and drinking and divorce does not occur, and according to the mufta bihi if he has no wife it is the same, and if he has a wife, divorce falls upon her and he does not break the oath by eating and drinking. And in al-Zahiriyyah: a man said 'every permissible thing is forbidden to me' or said 'every lawful thing is forbidden to me' or said 'the lawful of Allah' or said 'the lawful of the Muslims' and he has a wife and did not intend anything, said Shaykh Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl and al-Faqih Abu Ja'far and Abu Bakr al-Iskaf and Abu Bakr ibn Sa'eed: his wife becomes separated by one revocable divorce, and if he intended three then three, and if he said 'I did not intend divorce' he is not believed in court because it has become divorce by custom, and therefore only men swear by it. If he has one wife she becomes separated by one revocable divorce, and if they are three or four, an irrevocable divorce occurs on each one. And if he swore by this expression 'if I did such and such' and he had done it and he has one wife or more, together, and if he has no wife nothing is binding on him because he made it an oath by divorce, and if we make it an oath by Allah it is a false oath (ghamoos). And if he swore by this about a future matter then did it and he has no wife, expiation is upon him because forbidding the lawful is an oath. And if he had a wife at the time of the oath, and she died before the condition or separated without a waiting period, then the condition occurred, expiation is not binding on him because his oath turned to divorce at the time of her existence. And if he had no wife at the time of the oath, then married a woman, then the condition occurred, they differed. Al-Faqih Abu Ja'far said: the married woman becomes separated. Others said: she does not become separated, and al-Faqih Abu al-Layth took this and the fatwa is upon it because his oath is made an oath by Allah at the time of its occurrence, so it is not divorce after that. End. And it is restricted to the general formula because if he said to his wife 'you are forbidden to me' it has preceded in the chapter of Ila' that it turns to the wife and she is divorced without intention. Good, agreeing with the words of the predecessors, and the words of the author of al-Hidayah and others are interpreted as when the usage is not common in it and in other than it, but if it is common, then agreeing with the predecessors is determined. And I say: most common people in our lands do not intend by their saying 'you are forbidden to me' or 'haram upon me' or 'I have forbidden you to me' except the prohibition of intercourse corresponding to its permissibility, and therefore most of them set a period for her prohibition and do not intend certainly except prohibition of intercourse until that period, and there is no doubt that it is an oath necessitating ila'. Ponder. So few have investigated this issue properly. And look at their saying 'We do not say that intention is not required, but he is considered as intending customarily' - it is explicit in considering custom. If custom is not like that but rather common, then considering intention and believing the swearer is determined as is the madhhab of the predecessors. His statement: (and if they are three or four, an irrevocable divorce occurs on each one) said in al-Nahr after it: but in al-Dirayah: if he has two wives, divorce falls on one and the clarification is given to him in the more apparent view, like his saying 'my wife is such' and he has two wives or more. End. The commentator Miskin said: the apparent meaning of his words 'or more' is that the occurrence of divorce on one and the clarification is given to him is not specific to two, but also if they are three or four, so it is an opposing statement to what is in al-Zahiriyyah, and since the occurrence of divorce on one and the clarification is given to him is the more apparent view absolutely, whether he has two wives or more, what is in al-Zahiriyyah of occurrence on all is contrary to the more apparent view, even if it is in
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discusses the legal ruling of the phrase 'کل حل علی حرام' (every permissible thing is forbidden to me) in the context of oaths (al-aiman). The passage type is an expansion probe, detailing the positions of Zahir al-Riwayah, the mufta bihi (operative fatwa), and intra-Hanafi disputes, with a focus on the role of intention and custom ('urf).

The author begins by stating that according to Zahir al-Riwayah, when a man says this phrase, he is asked about his intention. If he intends an oath, it is a verbal oath (yamin) requiring expiation (kaffarah), and it does not affect his wife unless he specifically intends her. If he intends his wife, it becomes ila' (a vow of abstinence). The oath does not turn to food and drink; only if he eats or drinks or approaches his wife does he break the oath (hanth), and ila' falls. If he has no intention, it is still an oath requiring expiation, and the wife is not included. If he intends divorce, it is valid as an expression of divorce (talaq), and if he intends lying, it is a lie but nothing is binding. This is from al-Hakim's al-Kafi, and the text notes that al-Hidayah implies the same, but the mufta bihi position is that the wife becomes separated (tabeen) without intention due to prevalent custom (ghalabat al-isti'mal), as in al-Hidayah.

The passage then discusses the case when the speaker has no wife. According to al-Nihayah citing al-Nawazil, if he eats or drinks, he breaks the oath and must give expiation, because without a wife the phrase turns to food and drink. However, al-Bazdawi questioned this: if custom is not widespread for those without wives, the correct approach is to say that if he intends divorce it is divorce, but without indication, one should be cautious and not oppose the predecessors. Later, the author notes that such phrases are not customary in their lands; the common phrases are 'haram upon me your speech' etc., which are used for suspended divorce. The conclusion is that the turning of these expressions without intention depends on custom. If custom is not established, then intention is considered.

The passage then reconciles apparent contradictions: according to al-Zahiriyyah, if someone had no wife at the time of the oath but later married and then fulfilled the condition, the fatwa is that the wife does not become separated because the oath is considered an oath by Allah. This is restricted to oaths about future matters; for past actions, nothing is binding if there is no wife. Al-Nasafi holds that no expiation is required at all in such cases, but this is understood as referring to past actions. Thus, the key distinction is between past and future: if he says 'كل حل علي حرام' and remains silent or refers to a past action, nothing is binding if he has no wife; if it is about a future action, breaking it requires expiation. This reversal is due to change in custom: earlier custom turned the phrase to food and drink, later to divorce.

The text then presents a substantial quote from al-Zahiriyyah summarizing the fatwa: if a man says the phrase and has a wife without intention, the wife is divorced by one revocable talaq, and if he intends three, three occur. In court, his denial of intention is not accepted because it has become divorce by custom. If he has one wife, she gets one talaq; if three or four, each gets a ba'in (irrevocable) talaq. If he swears about a past action and has a wife, divorce occurs; if no wife, nothing. For future actions without a wife, expiation is due. If he had a wife at the time of oath but she died or separated before the condition, no expiation; if he had no wife then married later, there is a dispute: Abu Ja'far says she is divorced, but the fatwa is with Abu al-Layth that she is not, because the oath is considered an oath by Allah at the time of the condition.

The passage ends with a discussion of the phrase 'أنت علي حرام' (you are forbidden to me) said to a specific wife. It was stated earlier in Bab al-Ila' that this turns to the wife and she is divorced without intention. The author then gives his own observation that common people in his land use such phrases only to prohibit intercourse temporarily, making it an oath of ila', not divorce. He emphasizes that custom must be clear; if the usage is common (mushtarak), then intention must be considered and the swearer believed, as per the earlier scholars.

Finally, a note from al-Nahr and al-Dirayah mentions a dispute about multiple wives: some say divorce falls on all, but the more apparent view (al-azhar) is that it falls on only one wife, and clarification is given to him, similar to saying 'my wife is such' when having multiple wives. This opposes the ruling in al-Zahiriyyah that divorce occurs on all.

Key Terms
يمين غموس (yamin ghamoos): A false oath about something that has already happened, which is a major sin but does not require expiation.
حنث (hanth): The breaking of an oath by doing the prohibited or not doing the promised action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man with no wife says 'kullu hillin 'alayya haram if I slept early tonight' and then sleeps early. What is the ruling?
A) Nothing, because he has no wife.
B) He must give expiation for breaking an oath.
C) He is divorced from a non-existent wife.
D) It is a false oath and nothing is required.

Key Takeaway
For a man with no wife, the phrase 'kullu hillin 'alayya haram' results in an oath requiring expiation only if it concerns a future action; if about a past action, it is a false oath with no expiation.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Multiple Wives and Later Marriage
Discusses disputes on multiple wives and later marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding the phrase 'kullu hillin 'alayya haram' when the speaker has multiple wives or later marries after the oath, and state the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
When multiple wives are involved, the more apparent view is that divorce falls on one wife only; the fatwa in later marriage is no divorce.

Two additional disputes arise from this phrase: (1) If the speaker has multiple wives, does divorce fall on all or just one? (2) If he had no wife at the time of the oath but later marries and then the condition occurs, does the new wife get divorced? The passage presents both issues and resolves them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario: multiple wives
If the speaker has more than one wife and says the phrase without specifying, the text presents a disagreement: some say divorce falls on all, others say on only one.
This is a sub-issue within the broader discussion.
2
State the two views
Al-Zahiriyyah states that if there are three or four wives, an irrevocable divorce (talaq ba'in) occurs on each one. However, al-Dirayah reports a more apparent view (al-azhar) that divorce falls on only one wife, and the husband is given the chance to clarify which one he meant.
This echoes the rule for ambiguous statements like 'my wife is divorced' when he has multiple wives.
3
Evaluate the tarjih
The commentator notes that the more apparent view is that of al-Dirayah: the divorce is on one wife only, with clarification. The ruling in al-Zahiriyyah (divorce on all) is considered less preferred.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf resolved by later preference.
4
Address the later marriage scenario
If the speaker had no wife at the time of the oath, then later marries, and then the condition occurs, there is a dispute between Abu Ja'far (divorce occurs) and Abu al-Layth (no divorce, only expiation).
This was touched on in Lesson 2; here we examine the dispute.
5
Apply the mufta bihi
The fatwa is with Abu al-Layth: no divorce, only expiation. The oath is considered an oath by Allah at the time of the condition, not a suspended divorce.
This is the relied-upon position for later marriage.

كتاب الأيمان
زوجته لإسقاط اعتبار العموم، وإذا نواها كان إيلاء ولا تصرف اليمين عن المأكول والمشروب، وهذا كله جواب ظاهر الرواية ؛ كذا في الهداية مع أن عبارة الحاكم في الكافي : إذا قال الرجل كل حل علي حرام سئل عن نيته، فإن نوى يميناً فهو يمين يكفرها ولا تدخل امرأته في ذلك إلا أن ينوي فإن نواها دخلت، فإن أكل أو شرب أو قرب امرأته حنث وسقط عنه الإيلاء وإن لم يكن له نية فهو يمين يكفرها لا تدخل امرأته فيها، ولو نوى به الطلاق فالقول فيه كالقول في الحرام أي يصح ما نوى وإن نوى الكذب فهو كذب ا هـ . تقتضي أن الأمر موقوف على النية وأنه لو نوى الكذب لا يلزمه شيء وهو غير مستفاد من عبارة الهداية كما لا يخفى قوله : والفتوى على أنه تبين امرأته من غير نية لغلبة الاستعمال ؛ كذا في الهداية. وإن لم تكن له امرأة ذكر في النهاية معزياً إلى النوازل أنه يحنث وعليه الكفارة يعني إذا أكل أو شرب لانصرافه عند عدم الزوجة إلى الطعام والشراب لا كما يفهم من ظاهر العبارة. وقال البزدوي في مبسوطه : هكذا قال بعض مشايخ سمرقند ولم يتضح لي عرف الناس في هذا لأن . لا امرأة له يحلف به كما يحلف ذو الحليلة، ولو كان العرف مستفيضاً في ذلك لما استعمله إلا ذو الحليلة، فالصحيح أن يقيد الجواب في هذا فنقول : إن
اه.
من
نوى الطلاق يكون طلاقاً، فأما من غير دلالة فالاحتياط أن يقف الإنسان فيه ولا يخالف المتقدمين. واعلم أن مثل هذا اللفظ لم يتعارف في ديارنا بل المتعارف فيه «حرام علي كلامك» ونحوه كأكل كذا ولبسه دون الصيغة العامة، وتعارفوا أيضاً الحرام يلزمني ولا شك في أنهم يريدون الطلاق معلقاً فإنهم يزيدون بعده لا أفعل كذا ولأفعلن وهو مثل تعارفهم الطلاق يلزمني لا أفعل كذا فإنه يراد إن فعلت كذا فهي طالق ويجب إمضاؤه عليهم. والحاصل أن المعتبر في انصراف هذه الألفاظ عربية كانت أو فارسية إلى معنى بلا نية التعارف فيه، فإن لم
يخفى على المتأمل قوله : (يعني إذا أكل أو شرب الخ) مخالف لما سيأتي عن الظهيرية من أنه لو لم تكن له امرأة ثم تزوج امرأة ثم باشر الشرط الفتوى على أنه لا تبين لأنه يمينه جعل يميناً بالله تعالى الخ. ولكن ينبغي تقييد هذا بما إذا حلف على أمر من المستقبل وإلا فلا يلزمه شيء كما يأتي في عبارة الظهيرية أيضاً. وفي البزازية قال : وفي المواضع التي يقع الطلاق بلفظ الحرام إن لم تكن له امرأة إن حنث لزمته الكفارة والنسفي على أنه لا تلزمه ا هـ . قلت : والظاهر حمل كلام النسفي على ما إذا لم يكن حلفه على مستقبل فلا ينافي ما قبله والحاصل أنه إذا قال كل حل علي حرام وسكت أو قال إن كنت فعلت كذا الأمر فعله لا يلزمه شيء إذا لم تكن له امرأة وإن قال إن فعلت كذا في المستقبل لزمه كفارة بالحنث هذا كله بناء على تغير العرف من انصرافه إلى الطلاق بعد ما كان العرف قبله في انصرافه إلى الطعام والشراب، فما ذكره المؤلف في تأويل عبارة النهاية مخالفة لكلامهم والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم قوله : (وقال البزدوي في مبسوطه الخ) قال الرملي في حاشيته على المنح أقول: ما بحثه
........
يتعارف سئل عن نيته وفيما ينصرف بلا نية لو قال أردت غيره لا يصدقه القاضي وفيما بينه وبين الله تعالى وهو مصدق ؛ هكذا قال في فتح القدير والحاصل أنه على ظاهر الرواية يحنث بالأكل والشرب فقط ولا يقع عليه ،طلاق، وعلى المفتى به إن لم يكن له امرأة فكذلك، وإن كان له امرأة وقع الطلاق عليها ولا يحنث بالأكل والشرب. وفي الظهيرية: رجل قال كل حل علي حرام أو قال كل حلال علي حرام أو قال حلال الله أو قال حلال المسلمين وله امرأة ولم ينو شيئاً قال الشيخ الإمام أبو بكر محمد بن الفضل والفقيه أبو جعفر وأبو بكر الاسكاف وأبو بكر بن سعيد تبين امرأته بتطليقة وإن نوى ثلاثاً فثلاث، وإن قال لم أنو الطلاق لا يصدق قضاء لأنه صار طلاقاً عرفاً ولهذا لا يحلف به إلا الرجال فإن كان له امرأة واحدة تبين بتطليقة، وإن كن ثلاثاً أو أربعاً يقع على كل واحدة واحدة بائنة، وإن حلف بهذا اللفظ إن كان فعل كذا وقد كان فعل وله امرأة واحدة أو أكثرين جميعاً وإن لم يكن له امرأة لا يلزمه شيء لأنه جعل يميناً بالطلاق، ولو جعلناه يميناً بالله فهو غموس. وإن حلف بهذا على أمر في المستقبل ففعل ذلك وليس له امرأة كان عليه الكفارة لأن تحريم الحلال يمين، وإن كان له امرأة وقت اليمين فماتت قبل الشرط أو بانت لا إلى عدة ثم باشر الشرط لا تلزمه الكفارة لأن يمينه انصرف إلى الطلاق وقت وجودها وإن لم تكن له امرأة وقت اليمين ثم تزوج امرأة ثم باشر الشرط اختلفوا فيه قال الفقيه أبو جعفر : تبين المتزوجة وقال غيره : لا تبين وبه أخذ الفقيه أبو الليث وعليه الفتوى لأن يمينه جعل يميناً بالله تعالى وقت وجودها فلا يكون طلاقاً بعد ذلك اهـ. وقيد بصيغة العموم لأنه لو قال لزوجته أنت علي حرام فقد قدم في باب الإيلاء أنه ينصرف للزوجة فتطلق من غير نية.
جيد موافق لكلام المتقدمين ويحمل كلام صاحب الهداية وغيره على ما إذا لم يكن الاستعمال مشتركاً فيه وفي غيره، أما إذا كان مشتركاً تعين موافقة المتقدمين. وأقول : أكثر عوام بلادنا لا يقصدون بقولهم أنت محرمة علي أو حرام علي أو حرمتك علي إلا حرمة الوطء المقابل لحله ولذلك أكثرهم يضرب مدة لتحريمها ولا يريد قطعاً إلا تحريم الجماع إلى هذه المدة، ولا شك أنه يمين موجب للإيلاء تأمل. فقل من حقق هذه المسألة على وجهها وانظر إلى قولهم لا نقول لا تشترط النية لكن يجعل ناوياً عرفاً فهو صريح في اعتبار العرف فإن لم يكن العرف كذلك بل كان مشتركاً تعين اعتبار النية وتصديق الحالف كما هو مذهب المتقدمين قوله : (وإن كن ثلاثاً أو أربعاً يقع على كل واحدة واحدة بائنة) قال في النهر بعده لكن في الدراية : لو كان له امرأتان وقع الطلاق على واحدة وإليه البيان في الأظهر كقوله امرأتي كذا وله امرأتان أو أكثر ا.هـ. قال محشي مسكين: ظاهر قوله «أو أكثر أن وقوع الطلاق على واحدة وإليه البيان لا يخص الثنتين بل كذلك لو كن ثلاثاً أو أربعاً فهو قول مقابل لما في الظهيرية، وحيث كان وقوع الطلاق على واحدة وإليه البيان هو الأظهر مطلقاً، سواء كان له امرأتان أو أكثر، فما في الظهيرية من وقوعه على الكل خلاف الأظهر وإن كان في
Book of Oaths: his wife to drop consideration of generality, and if he intends it it becomes ila' and the oath does not direct to food and drink, and all this is the answer of Zahir al-Riwayah; thus in al-Hidayah even though the expression of al-Hakim in al-Kafi: if a man says 'every permissible thing is forbidden to me' he is asked about his intention, if he intends an oath it is an oath he expiates and his wife does not enter into that unless he intends her, then if he intends her she enters, if he eats or drinks or approaches his wife he breaks the oath and ila' falls from him, and if he has no intention it is an oath he expiates, his wife does not enter into it, and if he intends divorce by it the statement is as the statement in 'al-haram' i.e. what he intended is valid, and if he intends lying it is a lie etc. It necessitates that the matter depends on intention and that if he intends lying nothing is binding on him, and this is not derived from the expression of al-Hidayah as is not hidden. His statement: and the fatwa is that his wife becomes separated from him without intention due to prevalence of usage; thus in al-Hidayah. And if he has no wife, it is mentioned in al-Nihayah attributing to al-Nawazil that he breaks the oath and expiation is upon him, i.e. if he eats or drinks because when there is no wife it turns to food and drink, not as understood from the apparent expression. And al-Bazdawi said in his Mabsoot: thus said some shaykhs of Samarkand and it is not clear to me the custom of people in this because he who has no wife swears by it as does the one with a wife, and if custom were widespread in that, only the one with a wife would use it, so the correct view is to qualify the answer in this and we say: if he intends divorce it becomes divorce, but without indication the caution is to stop in it and not to oppose the predecessors. And know that such a phrase is not customary in our lands, rather the customary phrase is 'haram upon me your speech' and the like, like eating such and such and wearing it without the general formula, and they also customarily say 'al-haram is binding on me' and there is no doubt that they intend suspended divorce, for they add after it 'I will not do such and such' or 'I will certainly do' and it is like their custom of 'divorce is binding on me I will not do such and such' for it is intended: if I do such and such she is divorced, and it must be enforced upon them. The conclusion is that what is considered in the turning of these expressions, whether Arabic or Persian, to a meaning without intention is custom in it. If it is not hidden from the contemplator, his statement (i.e. if he eats or drinks etc.) contradicts what will come from al-Zahiriyyah that if he did not have a wife then married a woman then the condition occurred, the fatwa is that she does not become separated because his oath is made an oath by Allah etc. But this should be restricted to when he swore about a future matter, otherwise nothing is binding on him as also comes in the expression of al-Zahiriyyah. And in al-Bazzaziyyah it says: and in the places where divorce occurs by the word 'al-haram', if he has no wife, if he breaks the oath expiation is binding on him, and al-Nasafi is of the opinion that it is not binding on him etc. I say: the apparent view is to interpret al-Nasafi's words as referring to when he did not swear about the future, so it does not contradict what preceded. The conclusion is that if he says 'every permissible thing is forbidden to me' and remains silent or says 'if I did such and such thing' he did it, nothing is binding on him if he has no wife, and if he says 'if I do such and such in the future' then breaking the oath makes expiation binding. All this is based on the change of custom from its turning to divorce after the custom before was its turning to food and drink. So what the author mentioned in interpreting the expression of al-Nihayah contradicts their words, and Allah knows best. His statement: (and al-Bazdawi in his Mabsoot etc.) al-Ramli said in his Hashiyah on al-Manh: I say: what he researched... it is customary, he is asked about his intention, and in what turns without intention if he says 'I intended otherwise' the judge does not accept him, but between him and Allah Almighty he is believed; thus in Fath al-Qadir. The conclusion is that according to Zahir al-Riwayah he breaks the oath only by eating and drinking and divorce does not occur, and according to the mufta bihi if he has no wife it is the same, and if he has a wife, divorce falls upon her and he does not break the oath by eating and drinking. And in al-Zahiriyyah: a man said 'every permissible thing is forbidden to me' or said 'every lawful thing is forbidden to me' or said 'the lawful of Allah' or said 'the lawful of the Muslims' and he has a wife and did not intend anything, said Shaykh Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl and al-Faqih Abu Ja'far and Abu Bakr al-Iskaf and Abu Bakr ibn Sa'eed: his wife becomes separated by one revocable divorce, and if he intended three then three, and if he said 'I did not intend divorce' he is not believed in court because it has become divorce by custom, and therefore only men swear by it. If he has one wife she becomes separated by one revocable divorce, and if they are three or four, an irrevocable divorce occurs on each one. And if he swore by this expression 'if I did such and such' and he had done it and he has one wife or more, together, and if he has no wife nothing is binding on him because he made it an oath by divorce, and if we make it an oath by Allah it is a false oath (ghamoos). And if he swore by this about a future matter then did it and he has no wife, expiation is upon him because forbidding the lawful is an oath. And if he had a wife at the time of the oath, and she died before the condition or separated without a waiting period, then the condition occurred, expiation is not binding on him because his oath turned to divorce at the time of her existence. And if he had no wife at the time of the oath, then married a woman, then the condition occurred, they differed. Al-Faqih Abu Ja'far said: the married woman becomes separated. Others said: she does not become separated, and al-Faqih Abu al-Layth took this and the fatwa is upon it because his oath is made an oath by Allah at the time of its occurrence, so it is not divorce after that. End. And it is restricted to the general formula because if he said to his wife 'you are forbidden to me' it has preceded in the chapter of Ila' that it turns to the wife and she is divorced without intention. Good, agreeing with the words of the predecessors, and the words of the author of al-Hidayah and others are interpreted as when the usage is not common in it and in other than it, but if it is common, then agreeing with the predecessors is determined. And I say: most common people in our lands do not intend by their saying 'you are forbidden to me' or 'haram upon me' or 'I have forbidden you to me' except the prohibition of intercourse corresponding to its permissibility, and therefore most of them set a period for her prohibition and do not intend certainly except prohibition of intercourse until that period, and there is no doubt that it is an oath necessitating ila'. Ponder. So few have investigated this issue properly. And look at their saying 'We do not say that intention is not required, but he is considered as intending customarily' - it is explicit in considering custom. If custom is not like that but rather common, then considering intention and believing the swearer is determined as is the madhhab of the predecessors. His statement: (and if they are three or four, an irrevocable divorce occurs on each one) said in al-Nahr after it: but in al-Dirayah: if he has two wives, divorce falls on one and the clarification is given to him in the more apparent view, like his saying 'my wife is such' and he has two wives or more. End. The commentator Miskin said: the apparent meaning of his words 'or more' is that the occurrence of divorce on one and the clarification is given to him is not specific to two, but also if they are three or four, so it is an opposing statement to what is in al-Zahiriyyah, and since the occurrence of divorce on one and the clarification is given to him is the more apparent view absolutely, whether he has two wives or more, what is in al-Zahiriyyah of occurrence on all is contrary to the more apparent view, even if it is in
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discusses the legal ruling of the phrase 'کل حل علی حرام' (every permissible thing is forbidden to me) in the context of oaths (al-aiman). The passage type is an expansion probe, detailing the positions of Zahir al-Riwayah, the mufta bihi (operative fatwa), and intra-Hanafi disputes, with a focus on the role of intention and custom ('urf).

The author begins by stating that according to Zahir al-Riwayah, when a man says this phrase, he is asked about his intention. If he intends an oath, it is a verbal oath (yamin) requiring expiation (kaffarah), and it does not affect his wife unless he specifically intends her. If he intends his wife, it becomes ila' (a vow of abstinence). The oath does not turn to food and drink; only if he eats or drinks or approaches his wife does he break the oath (hanth), and ila' falls. If he has no intention, it is still an oath requiring expiation, and the wife is not included. If he intends divorce, it is valid as an expression of divorce (talaq), and if he intends lying, it is a lie but nothing is binding. This is from al-Hakim's al-Kafi, and the text notes that al-Hidayah implies the same, but the mufta bihi position is that the wife becomes separated (tabeen) without intention due to prevalent custom (ghalabat al-isti'mal), as in al-Hidayah.

The passage then discusses the case when the speaker has no wife. According to al-Nihayah citing al-Nawazil, if he eats or drinks, he breaks the oath and must give expiation, because without a wife the phrase turns to food and drink. However, al-Bazdawi questioned this: if custom is not widespread for those without wives, the correct approach is to say that if he intends divorce it is divorce, but without indication, one should be cautious and not oppose the predecessors. Later, the author notes that such phrases are not customary in their lands; the common phrases are 'haram upon me your speech' etc., which are used for suspended divorce. The conclusion is that the turning of these expressions without intention depends on custom. If custom is not established, then intention is considered.

The passage then reconciles apparent contradictions: according to al-Zahiriyyah, if someone had no wife at the time of the oath but later married and then fulfilled the condition, the fatwa is that the wife does not become separated because the oath is considered an oath by Allah. This is restricted to oaths about future matters; for past actions, nothing is binding if there is no wife. Al-Nasafi holds that no expiation is required at all in such cases, but this is understood as referring to past actions. Thus, the key distinction is between past and future: if he says 'كل حل علي حرام' and remains silent or refers to a past action, nothing is binding if he has no wife; if it is about a future action, breaking it requires expiation. This reversal is due to change in custom: earlier custom turned the phrase to food and drink, later to divorce.

The text then presents a substantial quote from al-Zahiriyyah summarizing the fatwa: if a man says the phrase and has a wife without intention, the wife is divorced by one revocable talaq, and if he intends three, three occur. In court, his denial of intention is not accepted because it has become divorce by custom. If he has one wife, she gets one talaq; if three or four, each gets a ba'in (irrevocable) talaq. If he swears about a past action and has a wife, divorce occurs; if no wife, nothing. For future actions without a wife, expiation is due. If he had a wife at the time of oath but she died or separated before the condition, no expiation; if he had no wife then married later, there is a dispute: Abu Ja'far says she is divorced, but the fatwa is with Abu al-Layth that she is not, because the oath is considered an oath by Allah at the time of the condition.

The passage ends with a discussion of the phrase 'أنت علي حرام' (you are forbidden to me) said to a specific wife. It was stated earlier in Bab al-Ila' that this turns to the wife and she is divorced without intention. The author then gives his own observation that common people in his land use such phrases only to prohibit intercourse temporarily, making it an oath of ila', not divorce. He emphasizes that custom must be clear; if the usage is common (mushtarak), then intention must be considered and the swearer believed, as per the earlier scholars.

Finally, a note from al-Nahr and al-Dirayah mentions a dispute about multiple wives: some say divorce falls on all, but the more apparent view (al-azhar) is that it falls on only one wife, and clarification is given to him, similar to saying 'my wife is such' when having multiple wives. This opposes the ruling in al-Zahiriyyah that divorce occurs on all.

Key Terms
الأظهر (al-azhar): The more apparent or preferred view among multiple opinions.
تبين المتزوجة (tabayyan al-mutazawwijah): The married woman becomes separated (divorced).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the mufta bihi position when a man with no wife says the phrase about a future action, later marries, and then does the action?
A) The new wife is divorced, and expiation is also due.
B) The new wife is not divorced; only expiation is due.
C) Nothing is binding because the marriage occurred after the oath.
D) Only the new wife is divorced; no expiation.

Key Takeaway
When the phrase is used with multiple wives or after later marriage, the more apparent views are: one divorce with clarification, and no divorce for later marriage.

Oath in Purchase: Qiyas vs Istihsan Regarding the Number Ten
Comparison of qiyas and istihsan regarding oath-breaking in a purchase scenario

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between qiyas and istihsan in oath rulings and apply the correct ruling based on custom.

Core Idea
Custom overrides literal wording in oaths: selling for nine after swearing 'not for ten' breaks the oath by istihsan, but not by qiyas.

This lesson explores a classic Hanafi scenario where a seller swears an oath regarding a sale price. The literal wording of the oath suggests a specific number, but customary usage may indicate a different intent. We will see how qiyas (analogy based on literal wording) and istihsan (juristic preference based on custom) lead to opposite rulings. The passage from al-Khulasa, quoted in al-Bahr al-Raiq, illustrates this methodological tension.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the oath
The seller swears: 'My slave is free if I sell this to you for ten (dirhams).' Determine what exactly triggers the oath.
The oath is conditional on selling for ten.
2
Apply qiyas: literal interpretation
By qiyas, the oath is only broken if the sale matches the exact literal condition. Selling for eleven or for nine does not match 'ten exactly', so no breach.
Qiyas adheres to the explicit wording.
3
Apply istihsan: custom-based interpretation
By istihsan, consider what people customarily mean by such oaths. The custom is that 'not for ten' means 'not for less than ten.' So selling for nine breaks the oath.
Istihsan overrides literal meaning when custom dictates.
4
Understand the methodological basis
Qiyas builds rulings on the literal wording (alfaz); istihsan builds on purposes (aghrad). Recognize that this is an intra-Hanafi difference, not inter-madhhab.
The school accepts both methods, with istihsan prevailing here by custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخلاصة من الجنس الخامس من اليمين في الشراء : ولو أن البائع هو الذي حلف فقال عبده حر أن بعت هذا منك بعشرة فباعه بعشرة دراهم ودينار أو بأحد عشر درهماً لم يحنث، ولو باعه بتسعة لا يحنث أيضاً. هذا جواب القياس؛ وفي الاستحسان على عكس هذا فإن العرف بين الناس أن من حلف لا بعشرة أن لا يبيعه إلا بأكثر من عشرة فإذا باعه بتسعة يحنث استحساناً اهـ. فالحاصل أن بناء الحكم على الألفاظ هو القياس والاستحسان بناؤه على الأغراض وسيأتي أنه هل يعتبر في العرف عند التخاطب أو العمل .
And in al-Khulasa, from the fifth category of oaths in purchase: If the seller is the one who swore and said, 'My slave is free if I sell this from you for ten,' and he sold it for ten dirhams and a dinar, or for eleven dirhams, he does not break his oath. And if he sold it for nine, he also does not break his oath. This is the answer by qiyas. In istihsan, it is the opposite, because the custom among people is that whoever swears 'not for ten' means not to sell except for more than ten. So if he sells for nine, he breaks his oath by istihsan. End. The conclusion is that building the ruling on the literal wording is qiyas, and istihsan builds it on purposes. It will come later whether custom is considered in speech or action.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage is a quoted ruling from al-Khulasa on oaths in purchase. The scenario: a seller swears that his slave will be free if he sells an item for ten (dirhams). The question is whether selling for ten plus a dinar, or for eleven dirhams, or for nine, triggers the oath. According to qiyas (analogy based on literal wording), the oath is not broken in any of these cases because the literal condition (sale for exactly ten) is not met. However, by istihsan (juristic preference based on custom), the oath may be broken: if he sells for nine, he breaks it because customarily the oath 'not for ten' means 'not for less than ten.' The author notes that the difference is that qiyas adheres to the letter, while istihsan considers the underlying purpose of the oath. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that custom ('urf) can override literal meaning in oath rulings.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning based on literal wording; here, it means adhering to the explicit condition (ten) without considering custom.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that departs from qiyas in favor of a more suitable ruling based on custom or utility.
العرف (al-'urf): Customary practice of the people; in oaths, it determines the intended meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to istihsan in the given passage, what is the ruling if a seller swears 'not for ten' and then sells for nine dirhams?
A) He breaks his oath because custom interprets 'not for ten' as not selling for less than ten.
B) He does not break his oath because nine is less than ten.
C) He breaks his oath only if he sells for exactly ten.
D) The oath is invalid because it was not specified.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, custom can override literal wording in oaths, leading to differing rulings between qiyas and istihsan.

The Threshold and Entry in Oaths: Hanafi Conditions and Dispute
Explains conditions for entry via threshold in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for entry via the threshold and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding partial entry.

Core Idea
Standing at the outer threshold does not break an oath of entry; complete entry requires both feet inside.

This lesson examines the precise definition of 'entry' when a person stands at the threshold of a door. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the inner and outer sill, and also considers the distribution of body weight when only one foot is inside. A subtle dispute with al-Sarakhsi regarding what constitutes complete separation is also explored.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is standing in the threshold (طاق الباب). The default ruling is that this does not constitute entry, because the door secures the house and the threshold is outside.
The threshold here means the outer sill (أسكفة الباب) that is outside when the door is closed.
2
Distinguish between inner and outer sill
The inner sill (the part inside when the door is closed) is considered part of the house. Standing on it breaks the oath. The outer sill does not.
This distinction is crucial for the oath.
3
Apply the weight condition for partial entry
If one foot is on the sill and the other inside, the ruling depends on which foot bears the body's weight. If the inner foot is lower and bears weight, the person is considered inside and breaks the oath. If the outer foot is lower or equal, the oath is not broken.
The text says 'اعتماد جميع بدنه على رجله' – weight distribution is key.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Al-Sarakhsi holds that the oath is never broken by standing at the threshold, regardless of weight distribution, because complete separation requires both feet to be inside.
The text labels al-Sarakhsi's view as 'الصحيح' (the correct) and 'وهو ظاهر' (evident), but the common view from 'كثير من الكتب' differs.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The common view (weight condition) is relied upon in fatwa, as it is more detailed and accounts for partial entry. Al-Sarakhsi's view is considered a minority position.
The text endorses al-Sarakhsi's view by saying 'وهو ظاهر', but does not overturn the common practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما لا يكون داخلاً إذا وقف في طاق الباب لأن الباب لإحراز الدار وما فيها فلم يكن الخارج من الدار. والمراد بطاق الباب عتبته التي إذا أغلق الباب كانت خارجة وهي المسماة بأسكفة الباب، وأما العتبة التي لو أغلق الباب تكون داخلة فهي من الدار فيحنث بالدخول فيها، ولو كان المحلوف عليه الخروج انعكس الحكم كما نص عليه الحاكم . وقيد بكونه واقفاً في طاق الباب أي بقدميه لأنه لو وقف بإحدي رجليه على العتبة وأدخل الأخرى، فإن استوى الجانبان أو كان الجانب الخارج أسفل لم يحنث، وإن كان الجانب الداخل أسفل حنث لأن اعتماد جميع بدنه على رجله التي هي في الجانب الأسفل، كذا في كثير من الكتب. وفي الظهيرية معزياً إلى السرخسي : الصحيح أنه لا يحنث مطلقاً اهـ. وهو ظاهر لأن الانفصال التام لا يكون إلا بالقدمين.
And he is not considered an entrant when he stands in the threshold of the door, because the door is for securing the house and what is in it, so he is not outside the house. And what is meant by 'threshold of the door' is its sill that when the door is closed is outside, and it is called the 'uskuffah' of the door. As for the sill that when the door is closed is inside, it is part of the house, so he breaks the oath upon entering it. And if the subject of the oath was leaving, the ruling is reversed as al-Hakim stated. And it is conditioned that he is standing in the threshold, meaning with his two feet, because if he stands with one foot on the sill and inserts the other, if both sides are equal or the outer side is lower, he does not break the oath; and if the inner side is lower, he breaks the oath because the weight of his entire body is on his foot that is on the lower side. Thus in many books. And in al-Zahiriyya, attributing to al-Sarakhsi: the correct position is that he does not break the oath absolutely, and it is evident because complete separation is only achieved with both feet.
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on what constitutes 'entering' for the purpose of an oath. The commentator explains that standing at the threshold of a door does not count as entry because the door itself is a barrier that contains the house. The key distinction is between the inner and outer sill: the outer sill is outside the house, so standing on it does not break the oath, while the inner sill is part of the house, so stepping onto it breaks the oath. If the oath is about leaving, the opposite applies. A further detail: if one stands with one foot on the sill and the other inside, the ruling depends on which foot bears the body's weight—if the inner foot is lower, one is considered inside. However, al-Sarakhsi holds that one does not break the oath in any case because complete entry requires both feet inside. This reflects a subtle dispute within the Hanafi school: the preferred view is that complete separation from outside requires both feet, so partial entry does not suffice to break the oath.

Key Terms
طاق الباب (Taq al-Bab): The arch or threshold of the door, specifically the outer sill that is outside when the door is closed.
أسكفة الباب (Uskuffat al-Bab): The outer sill of the door, synonymous with طاق الباب in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the common Hanafi view, when does standing with one foot on the sill and one foot inside the house cause an oath of entry to be broken?
A) When the outer foot is lower than the inner foot
B) When the inner foot is lower than the outer foot and bears the body's weight
C) When both feet are on the threshold
D) When the inner foot is inside but the body weight is on the outer foot

Key Takeaway
Entry via a threshold depends on both foot placement and weight distribution; the outer sill does not count as entry, but partial entry with weight inside may break an oath.

Determining Part of the House for Oath Purposes: Attached Structures
Rulings on when entering specific structures breaks an oath of entry

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which structures (kanif, zullah, hanut, garden, stable, courtyard) are considered part of the house for oath purposes and apply the condition of functional integration.

Core Idea
A structure is part of the house if it is functionally integrated, not merely physically attached.

When a person swears not to enter a house, what counts as 'entering the house'? Classical Hanafi jurists compiled rulings on various attached and detached structures to determine when the oath is broken. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq summarises those rulings, distinguishing between structures that are functionally part of the house (like a garden in the middle) and those that are not (like a stable). Understanding these fine distinctions is crucial for issuing correct fatwas on oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure type
Read the passage and note each structure mentioned: kanif (protruding lavatory), zullah (canopy), hanut (shop), garden, stable (istabl), courtyard (hawsh), and bench (dukkan).
The passage also mentions roof, wall, tree, sill, vestibule, courtyard as part of the house.
2
Check the condition of the opening
For kanif and zullah, determine whether the opening is inside the house (مفتح الكنيف والظلة في الدار). If the opening is inside, they are part of the house; if outside, they are not.
The phrase 'بالشرط المذكور' refers back to this condition.
3
Assess structural enclosure and connection
For the shop (hanut), even if it opens to the street, it is part of the house because it is enclosed by the house walls (لأن من جملة الدار ما أحاطت به الدور). For a garden, distinguish: if it is connected to the house (متصلاً بها), it does not break the oath; if it is in the middle of the house (في وسطها), it does break the oath.
A garden in the middle is fully surrounded by the house, hence functionally part.
4
Apply the rule of dependency and benefit
For structures like a stable (اصطبل) and a courtyard (حوش), stable is not part of the house; courtyard adjacent to the house (بجنب البيت) is considered part. For a bench (دكان) at the door, if the person sworn about benefits from it and it is a dependent of the house, the oath is broken (though the author notes consideration).
The principle is that structures dependent on and beneficial to the house are included.
5
Synthesise the general rule
Summarise: any structure that is functionally integrated (whether inside the house walls or directly attached with internal access) is part of the house; independent or detached structures are not. The manner of entry (riding, walking, etc.) is irrelevant; only the fact of entering matters, provided the person is free in choice.
The final sentence in the passage affirms this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية : ولو قام على كنيف شارع أو ظلة شارعة إن كان مفتح الكنيف والظلة في الدار كان حانثاً. وفي المحيط : لو دخل حانوتاً مشرعاً . من هذه الدار إلى الطريق وليس له باب في الدار فإنه يحنث لأن من جملة الدار ما أحاطت به الدور، وإن دخل بستاناً في : الدار، فإن كان متصلاً بها لم يحنث وإن كان في وسطها حنث .اهـ. وفي القنية : حلف لا يدخل داره فدخل اصطبله لا يحنث وفي الخلاصة معزياً إلى فتاوى النسفي : لو حلف لا يدخل بيت فلان فجلس على دكان على بابه إن كان ينتفع به المحلوف عليه وهو تبع لبيته يحنث - قال رحمه الله - وفيه نظر اهـ . وعلى هذا لو دخل حوشاً بجنب البيت يحنث . والحاصل أنه إذا حلف لا يدخل هذه الدار أو دار فلان فإنه يحنث بالوقوف على سطحها أو حائطها أو شجرة فيها أو عتبة داخل الباب ودهليزها أو صحنها أو كنيفها أو ظلتها بالشرط المذكور أو بستانها الذي في وسطها، ويحنث بدخولها على أي صفة كان الحالف راكباً كان أو ماشياً أو محمولاً بأمره حافياً أو منتعلاً بشرط أن يكون مختاراً لما في
And in al-Zahiriyya: If he stands on a protruding lavatory or a protruding canopy, if the opening of the lavatory and canopy is in the house, he breaks the oath. And in al-Muhit: If he enters a shop that projects from this house to the road and has no door in the house, he breaks the oath, because part of the house is what the walls enclose. And if he enters a garden that is in the house, if it is connected to it, he does not break the oath; if it is in the middle of it, he breaks the oath. And in al-Qunyah: If he swears not to enter his house and enters his stable, he does not break the oath. And in al-Khulasah, attributing to Fatawa al-Nasafi: If he swears not to enter so-and-so's bayt and sits on a bench at its door, if the one sworn about benefits from it and it is a dependent of his house, he breaks the oath. The author said: There is consideration in this. And based on this, if he enters a courtyard (hawsh) next to the house, he breaks the oath. And the conclusion: That if he swears not to enter this house or the house of so-and-so, he breaks the oath by standing on its roof, its wall, a tree in it, the inner sill of the door, its vestibule, its courtyard, its lavatory, its canopy with the mentioned condition, or its garden that is in its middle. And he breaks the oath by entering it in any manner — riding, walking, carried by his command, barefoot, or shod — provided he is free in choice, for what is in...
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage compiles rulings on various structures attached to or within a house. The general principle is that anything encompassed by the house's walls or functionally integrated counts as part of the house for oath purposes. Thus, standing on a protruding lavatory or canopy that opens into the house breaks the oath. Entering a shop that projects from the house but has no door inside still breaks the oath because it is enclosed by the house walls. A garden connected to the house does not break the oath, but one in the middle of the house does. Conversely, a stable is not considered part of the house, so entering it does not break the oath. Sitting on a bench at the door may break the oath if it is dependent on the house. The summary reiterates many of these points and emphasizes that any manner of entry (riding, walking, etc.) breaks the oath as long as the person is acting voluntarily.

Key Terms
كَنِيف (Kanif): A protruding lavatory or toilet structure attached to the house.
ظُلَّة (Zullah): A canopy or awning that projects from the house.
حَانُوت (Hanut): A shop that is part of the house structure, even if it opens to the street.
حَوْش (Hawsh): A courtyard or enclosure adjacent to the house.
اصْطَبْل (Istabl): A stable for animals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, entering which of the following structures would break an oath not to enter the house?
A) A stable (istabl)
B) A garden that is connected to the house externally (muttasil)
C) A shop (hanut) that projects from the house and has no internal door
D) A canopy (zullah) whose opening is outside the house

Key Takeaway
Functional integration, not mere attachment, determines whether a structure is part of the house for oath purposes.

Involuntary Entry and Its Effect on Oaths
Explains when forced or accidental entry breaks an oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on involuntary entry breaking an oath and apply the condition of volition to scenarios of force and accident.

Core Idea
Involuntary entry does not break an oath; volition is the key condition.

When someone swears not to enter a place, but enters due to an accident or coercion, does the oath break? The Hanafi school carefully examines the element of choice. This passage from Al-Zahiriyya explains that involuntary acts—like stumbling, being pushed by wind, or carried by a runaway animal—do not violate the oath. However, if a forced entry is followed by a voluntary one, the voluntary act does break the oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Ruling
Read the scenarios: stumbling, wind, runaway animal, and forced entry. For each, determine whether the oath is broken according to the correct view.
The correct view is that none of the involuntary entries break the oath.
2
Locate the Condition (Volition)
Understand that the ruling hinges on the condition of voluntary action. If the entry is not chosen freely, the oath is not breached.
The text explicitly states that if the person cannot prevent the entry, the oath remains intact.
3
Identify the Ikhtilaf (Disagreement)
Note that there is disagreement on each scenario ('ikhtalafu fih'), but the text declares the preferred view as 'al-sahih' (the correct).
This shows that even within the Hanafi school, there were differing opinions on borderline cases.
4
Understand the Ta'lil (Reasoning)
The justification is that the condition for breaking the oath—voluntary entry—was absent. For the forced-then-voluntary case, the first entry lacked this condition, but the second entry fulfilled it.
The reasoning is straightforward: the oath breaks only when the prohibited act is done deliberately.
5
Apply the Rule to the Special Case
Consider the scenario where a person is forced in, leaves, and then enters voluntarily. The forced entry does not count, so the voluntary entry triggers the breach.
This is the mufta bihi position according to the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الظهيرية : ولو جاء إلى بابها وهو يشتد في المشي أي يعدو فانعثر أو انزلق فوقع في الدار اختلفوا فيه، والصحيح أنه لا يحنث وإن دفعته الريح وأوقعته في الدار اختلفوا فيه والصحيح أنه لا يحنث إن كان لا يستطيع الامتناع وإن كان على دابة فجمحت وانفلتت وأدخلته في الدار وهو لا يستطيع إمساكها لا يحنث، وإن أدخله إنسان مكرهاً فخرج منها ثم دخل بعد ذلك مختاراً اختلفوا فيه والفتوى على أنه يحنث .اهـ. ووجهه أن الشرط لم يوجد بالدخول مكرهاً بدليل عدم الحنث وقد وجد بالدخول ثانياً مختاراً فحنث وسيأتي بعد ذلك إيضاحه.
Al-Zahiriyya: If he comes to her door while walking fast, i.e., running, and stumbles or slips and falls into the house, they differed, and the correct view is that he does not break the oath. And if the wind pushes him and throws him into the house, they differed, and the correct view is that he does not break the oath if he cannot prevent it. And if he was on an animal that bolts and runs away and brings him into the house while he cannot control it, he does not break the oath. And if a person brings him in by force, and he leaves the house and then enters later voluntarily, they differed, and the fatwa is that he breaks the oath. The justification is that the condition was not met by the forced entry, evidenced by the lack of breach, and it was met by the subsequent voluntary entry, so he breaks the oath. Explanation will follow later.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage addresses the effect of coercion or accident on oaths of entry. The general rule is that involuntary entry does not break the oath because the condition of voluntary action is absent. Thus, if one stumbles or is pushed by wind or carried by a runaway animal, the oath is not broken. However, there is a difference of opinion on these cases, and the correct view is as stated. A special case is when a person is forced in and then later enters voluntarily: the forced entry does not count, but the subsequent voluntary entry does break the oath. The reasoning is that the first entry lacked the requisite volition, but the second entry has it.

Key Terms
إِكْرَاه (Ikrah): Coercion or force that removes free choice; entry under compulsion does not break the oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if someone is forced into a house by another person, then leaves, and then later enters voluntarily, what is the ruling on the oath?
A) He does not break the oath because the first entry was forced
B) He breaks the oath because of the voluntary entry
C) He breaks the oath only if he intended the first entry
D) He does not break the oath because he left before the voluntary entry

Key Takeaway
Involuntary entry does not break an oath; only voluntary entry does, and each entry is judged independently.

Oaths Involving Houses: Residency over Ownership and Wad' al-Qadam
Expansion on oaths and houses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule regarding what constitutes 'his house' for oath purposes and apply the principle that placing the foot is equivalent to entering.

Core Idea
A house attributed to a person means where they reside, not what they own; and placing the foot equals entering.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq clarifies two interconnected issues in oaths involving houses. First, the term 'entering' includes the metaphorical act of placing one's foot inside, as this has become a recognized legal metaphor. Second, when an oath refers to 'so-and-so's house,' the focus is on the house where that person actually dwells, regardless of ownership. This ruling has practical implications, especially when a person owns multiple properties or lives in another's home.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the oath
The oath involves not entering a specific person's house. Determine the legal effect of entering.
2
Apply the principle of Wad' al-Qadam
Understand that 'placing the foot' is a metaphor for entry, so it triggers the oath's consequence.
3
Define 'his house' in the context of the oath
The house attributed to a person is the one they inhabit, not necessarily own. Investment properties (dar ghallah) are excluded.
4
Consider special cases: leased property, daughter/husband's house
If a person lives in a house under lease or borrowing, it is considered 'his' for oath purposes.
The case from al-Khaniyya shows that a daughter living in her husband's house is still considered her father's house for oath purposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووضع القدم كالدخول فيما ذكرنا لأنه صار مجازاً عن الدخول وهي مسألة الحقيقة والمجاز في الأصول. وهذا كله باعتبار الدار، وأما باعتبار صفتها بالإضافة إلى فلان فإنه يحنث إذا دخل داراً مضافة إلى فلان، سواء كان يسكتها بالملك أو بالإجارة أو بالعارية. وفي المجتبى : لو قال إن دخلت دار زيد فعبدي حر وإن دخلت دار عمرو فامرأتي طالق فدخل دار زيد وهي في يد عمرو بإجارة يعتق وتطلق إذا لم ينو فإن نوى شيئاً صدق اهـ. وفي المحيط : لو حلف لا يدخل دار فلان وله دار يسكنها ودار غلة فدخل دار الغلة لا يحنث إذا لم يدل الدليل على دار الغلة وغيرها لأن داره مطلقاً دار يسكنها .اهـ. وفي الخانية: لو حلف لا يدخل دار ابنته وابنته تسكن في دار زوجها أو حلف لا يدخل دار أمه وأمه تسكن في بيت زوجها فدخل الحالف حنث .اهـ.
And placing the foot is like entering in what we have mentioned, because it became a metaphor for entry, and this is an issue of literal and metaphorical in usul. And all of this is with regard to the house; as for its attribute in relation to someone, he breaks the oath if he enters a house attributed to so-and-so, whether he inhabits it by ownership, lease, or borrowing. And in al-Mujtaba: If he says 'If I enter Zayd's house, my slave is free, and if I enter Amr's house, my wife is divorced,' and he enters Zayd's house which is in the hand of Amr by lease, both the slave is freed and the divorce occurs if he did not intend; if he intended something, he is believed. And in al-Muhit: If he swears not to enter so-and-so's house, and he has a house he lives in and an investment property, and he enters the investment property, he does not break the oath if there is no evidence indicating the investment property etc., because his house absolutely is the one he lives in. And in al-Khaniyya: If he swears not to enter his daughter's house and she lives in her husband's house, or swears not to enter his mother's house and she lives in her husband's house, and he enters, he breaks the oath.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage establishes two important principles. First, 'placing the foot' inside a house is legally equivalent to entering, a metaphorical extension of the literal meaning of entry. Second, when an oath refers to a house attributed to a specific person, it means the house where that person resides, regardless of ownership. Thus, entering a house that is owned but not lived in does not break the oath. However, if the person lives in a house belonging to someone else (e.g., a daughter in her husband's house), that house is considered 'his' for oath purposes. The case from al-Mujtaba illustrates a scenario where two oaths are made regarding different houses; if one house is under the other's lease, both oaths are broken unless the swearer had a specific intention.

Key Terms
وَضْعُ الْقَدَم (Wad' al-Qadam): Literally 'placing the foot', used metaphorically to mean entering the house.
دَارُ غَلَّة (Dar Ghallah): An investment property that generates rental income and is not inhabited by the owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Muhit, if a person swears not to enter so-and-so's house, and so-and-so owns two houses—one he lives in and one rented out—entering which house breaks the oath?
A) The house he lives in
B) The rented house
C) Both houses
D) Neither house

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oath jurisprudence, a house attributed to a person is defined by residency, not ownership, and the metaphor of placing the foot is legally equivalent to entering.

Oaths with Collective Nouns: Definite vs Indefinite Plurals
Explains how oaths with definite plurals are broken by one individual

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between definite and indefinite plural nouns in oaths and apply the Hanafi rule that a definite plural (ism jins) is broken by one individual.

Core Idea
An oath using a definite plural is broken by one; indefinite requires all, unless intent specifies otherwise.

When a person swears an oath using a collective noun like 'the poor', does one act toward a single poor person break the oath? The answer hinges on the grammatical definiteness of the noun. In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi rule from al-Bahr al-Raiq that a definite plural with al- is treated as a generic noun (ism jins), making a single individual sufficient to break the oath, while indefinite plurals require involvement with multiple individuals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical form of the noun
Look at the noun in the oath: is it definite (with al- prefix) or indefinite (without al-)? This determines the ruling.
E.g., 'al-fuqara' (the poor) is definite; 'rijalan' (men) is indefinite.
2
Apply the rule for definite plurals
If the noun is a definite plural, it is considered a generic noun (ism jins). The oath is broken by acting toward one individual or instance.
Supported by the Quranic verse 'la yahillu laka al-nisa' (33:52), which does not mean all women.
3
Apply the rule for indefinite plurals
If the noun is indefinite (e.g., 'rijalan'), the oath is not broken by one individual unless the swearer intended otherwise.
The indefinite plural requires plural action absent contrary intent.
4
Consider the swearer's intent
If the swearer specifies an intention (e.g., intending all), that intention is considered judicially and religiously.
Intention can affect the ruling even for definite nouns, but for definite nouns the default is one.
5
Extend the principle to other cases
Apply the same logic to oaths about food and drink: an oath not to eat 'this food' requires eating all at once (unless unable), while an oath not to drink 'this water' is broken by a single drop.
The purpose of the oath is self-restraint, interpreted according to capability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد وقعت حادثة أن رجلاً حلف بالطلاق أن أولاد زوجته لا يطلعون إلى بيته فطلع واحد هل يحنث ؟ فأجبت بأنه لا يحنث ولا بد من الجمع لأنه جمع ليس فيه الألف واللام قال في الواقعات : إذا قال والله لا أكلم الفقراء أو المساكين أو الرجال فكلم واحداً منهم بحنث لأنه اسم جنس بخلاف قوله رجالاً أو نساء هي (لا أكلم الفقراء أو المساكين (الخ) ولو قال إن كلمت بني آدم أو الرجال أو النساء حنث بالفرد إلا أن ينوي الكل إلحاقاً للجمع المعرف بالجنس لقوله تعالى لا يحل لك النساء ﴾ [الأحزاب: ٥٢] فإنه لا يختص بالجمع فإذا لم ينو حنث بالفرد لأن غرضه باليمين منع نفسه من المحلوف عليه وليس في وسعه إثبات كل الجنس فينصرف إلى ما دونه وذلك مجهول فصرفناه إلى الأدنى وهو الواحد لتيقنه . ولهذا لو حلف لا يشرب ماء هذا البحر ينصرف إلى قطرة منه وفي ماء هذا الكوز إلى جميعه، وفي لا يأكل هذا الطعام لا يحنث ما لم يأكله كله دفعة وإن لم يقدر يحنث بأكل بعضه، وفي رواية إن أمكنه أكله في عمره لا يحنث بالبعض والأول أصح. ولو كان مكان الأكل بيع لا يحنث بالبعض لأن البيع يرد على جميعه. هذا كله إذا لم ينو شيئاً، فلو نوى الكل صدق ديانة وقضاء. ولو قال إن كلمت الرجل فكلم رجلاً وقال عنيت باليمين غيره يصدق قضاء لأنه اسم جنس بخلاف إن كلمت رجلاً لأنه منكر فلا تصح نية التخصيص فيه . ولو قال لا أكل التمر أو تمراً أو الطعام أو طعاماً أو لا أشرب الماء أو ماء فإن المعرف والمنكر فيه سواء لكونه اسم جنس فيقع على الأدنى وإن كان منكراً وفي الجمع المنكر
An incident occurred: a man swore by divorce that his wife's children would not ascend to his house; one ascended. Does he break the oath? I answered: He does not break the oath; there must be plural, because it is a plural without alif-lam. It is said in al-Waqi'at: If he says 'By Allah, I will not speak to the poor (al-fuqara') or the destitute (al-masakin) or the men (al-rijal)', and he speaks to one of them, he breaks the oath because it is a generic noun (ism jins), unlike his saying 'men' (rijalan) or 'women' (nisa'an) without alif-lam. And if he says 'If I speak to the sons of Adam (bani Adam) or the men (al-rijal) or the women (al-nisa')', he breaks by one individual unless he intends all, analogizing the definite plural to the generic, because of His statement 'It is not lawful for you [to marry] women (al-nisa')' [33:52], so it does not specifically mean all. So if he does not intend, he breaks by one, because his purpose in swearing is to prevent himself from the sworn matter, and it is not in his ability to establish the entire genus, so it turns to what is less, and that is unknown, so we turn it to the least which is one for certainty. For this reason, if he swears not to drink the water of this sea, it refers to a drop of it; and for water of this pitcher, to all of it; and for 'I will not eat this food', he does not break until he eats all of it at once, and if he cannot, he breaks by eating some; and in a narration, if he can eat it in his lifetime, he does not break by some; the first is more correct. And if the place of eating was a sale, he does not break by some because sale applies to the whole. All this is if he did not intend anything; if he intended all, he is believed religiously and judicially. And if he says 'If I speak to the man (al-rajul)', and he speaks to a man, and says 'I meant by the oath someone else', he is believed judicially because it is a generic noun; unlike if he said 'If I speak to a man (rajulan)', because it is indefinite, so an intention of specification is not valid. And if he says 'I will not eat dates (al-tamr) or tamran, or food (al-ta'am) or ta'aman, or I will not drink water (al-ma') or ma'an', then the definite and indefinite are the same because it is a generic noun, so it applies to the least, even if indefinite, and in the indefinite plural...
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage addresses a common issue in oath jurisprudence: when an oath uses a collective noun (e.g., 'the poor'), does one act toward a single individual break the oath? The answer depends on the grammatical form. If the noun is a definite plural with 'al-' (ال) like 'al-fuqara'' (the poor), it is considered a generic noun (ism jins) and breaking the oath with one individual suffices. This is analogized to the Quranic verse 'la yahillu laka al-nisa'' (it is not lawful for you [to marry] women), which does not mean all women. If the noun is indefinite (e.g., 'rijalan'), then the oath is not broken by one individual unless the swearer intends all. The underlying principle is that the purpose of an oath is self-restraint, and since it is impossible to guarantee complete avoidance of an entire genus, the oath is interpreted as applying to the least possible unit (a single individual). The passage also discusses similar principles for oaths involving food and drink: an oath not to eat a specific food is not broken until the entire food is consumed, but if the food is a liquid (like water in a pitcher), the oath is broken by drinking any part of it.

Key Terms
اسْمُ جِنْس (Ism Jins): A generic noun that refers to the category or type, not a specific individual; in oaths, it is broken by action toward one instance.
جَمْعٌ لَيْسَ فِيهِ الْأَلِفُ وَاللَّام (Jam'un laysa fihi al-alif wa al-lam): A plural noun without the definite article; considered indefinite and does not break the oath by one individual.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'By Allah, I will not eat this food' (referring to a specific dish), according to the preferred view in the passage, when is the oath broken?
A) As soon as he eats any part of it
B) Only if he eats all of it at once
C) Only if he eats all of it over time
D) When he eats more than half

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a definite plural noun in an oath is treated as a generic noun (ism jins), and performing the sworn act on one individual breaks the oath.
