Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Riding a Slave's Animal
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath scope regarding slave's animal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on whether an oath not to ride Zayd's animal includes Zayd's slave's animal, and identify the practical consequence of each view.

Core Idea
A slave's animal is customarily attributed to the slave, not the master, so an oath about the master's animal does not include it without intention and absence of consummate debt.

This lesson examines a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the scope of an oath when the property in question is actually owned by a slave. The discussion reveals how custom (urf), legal reality, and intention interact in determining whether an oath is broken. Understanding these distinctions is essential for issuing correct fatwas in cases where ownership and attribution diverge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the different positions of the three Hanafi imams
Abu Hanifa requires both intention and no consummate debt; Abu Yusuf requires intention regardless of debt; Muhammad holds it always breaks the oath.
2
Examine the dalil (hadith) and its implications
The hadith 'Whoever sells a slave and he has wealth' supports the customary attribution of the slave's property to the slave, disrupting the master's direct attribution.
3
Analyse the role of consummate debt (mustaghriq)
Abu Hanifa's unique concept: if the slave's debt covers all his property, the master has no ownership of the slave's earnings, so the slave's animal is not considered the master's at all.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Without intention: no breaking under Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf; breaking under Muhammad. With intention: breaks under all if no consummate debt; under Abu Hanifa, does not break if consummate debt.
5
Note the analogous case of manumission
The dispute parallels the case where someone says 'Every slave of mine is free' regarding the slaves of his own slave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومركب عبده مركبه إن ينو ولا دين) يعني لو حلف لا يركب دابة فلان فركب دابة عبد فلان فإنه يحنث بشرطين : الأول أن ينويها الثاني أن لا يكون عليه دين أي مستغرق فإن لم ينو لا حنث مطلقاً لأن الملك وإن كان للمولى إلا أنه يضاف إلى العبد عرفاً وكذا شرعاً لأن قال عليه السلام من باع عبداً وله مال الحديث فتختل الإضافة إلى المولى فلا بد من النية، فإن نواها ولا دين على العبد أو كان دينه غير مستغرق حنث لأنه شدد على نفسه بنيته، وإن كان الدين مستغرقاً فلا حنث وإن نوى لأنه لا ملك للمولى في كسب عبده المديون المستغرق عند أبي حنيفة وقال أبو يوسف : يحنث في الوجوه كلها إذا نوى الملك للمولى لكن الإضافة إليه قد اختلت لما ذكرنا فلا يدخل إلا بالنية . وقال محمد يحنث في الوجوه كلها نوى أو لم ينو اعتباراً للحقيقة لأن العبد وما في يده ملك المولى حقيقة ونظير هذا الاختلاف ما لو قال كل مملوك لي حر فعند أبي يوسف لا يدخل عبيد عبده التاجر إلا بالنية سواء كان على العبد دين أو لا، وعند محمد عتقوا نواهم أو لا، كان عليه دين أو لا . وعند أبي حنيفة إن لم يكن عليه دين عتقوا إذا نواهم وإلا فلا، وإن كان على العبد دين لم يعتقوا وإن نواهم وفي المحيط ولو ركب دابة مكاتبة لا يحنث لأن ملكه ليس بمضاف إلى المولى لا ذاتاً ولا يداً اهـ.
His statement: 'And the mount of his slave is his mount if he intends and there is no debt.' Meaning: If he swore not to ride the animal of so-and-so, then he rode the animal of the slave of so-and-so, he breaks the oath with two conditions: first, that he intends it; second, that there is no debt on the slave (i.e., consummate debt). If he does not intend, there is no breaking at all, because ownership, although it belongs to the master, is attributed to the slave customarily and also legally because the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever sells a slave and he has wealth,' etc. So attribution to the master is disturbed; thus, intention is necessary. If he intends it and the slave has no debt or his debt is not consummate, he breaks the oath because he imposed severity on himself by his intention. If the debt is consummate, then no breaking even if he intends, because, according to Abu Hanifa, there is no ownership for the master in the earning of his indebted slave if the debt is consummate. Abu Yusuf said: He breaks the oath in all cases if he intends ownership for the master, but attribution to him has been disturbed for what we mentioned, so it is not included except with intention. Muhammad said: He breaks the oath in all cases, whether he intends or not, in consideration of the reality, because the slave and what he possesses are the property of the master in reality. An analogous difference is if he said 'Every slave of mine is free.' According to Abu Yusuf, the slaves of his merchant slave are not included except with intention, whether the slave has a debt or not. According to Muhammad, they are freed whether he intended them or not, whether he has a debt or not. According to Abu Hanifa, if he has no debt, they are freed if he intended them, otherwise no; if the slave has a debt, they are not freed even if he intended them. And in al-Muhit: If he rides the animal of a mukatab, he does not break the oath because his property is not attributed to the master, neither in substance nor in possession.
Source pages p. 531

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the interpretation of an oath not to ride the animal of a specific person, when the animal is actually owned by that person's slave. The ruling hinges on commercial custom and legal ownership. According to the majority of the school, the animal is customarily attributed to the slave, not the master, so the master is not included in the oath without explicit intention. The dispute among the three imams revolves around whether the master's ownership is considered real or nominal, and whether debt on the slave affects the attribution. Abu Hanifa distinguishes based on whether the slave's debt is consummate: if it is, the master's ownership over the slave's earnings (and thus the animal) is absent. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ on the need for intention and the effect of debt. An analogous issue is the manumission of the slaves of one's own slave. The text also notes that a mukatab's animal is not attributed to the master at all.

Key Terms
مستغرق (mustaghriq): Consummate debt: a debt that covers the entire value of the slave's property, thereby negating the master's ownership over the slave's earnings according to Abu Hanifa.
مكاتبة (mukatabah): A female slave who has a contract of manumission; her property is fully hers and not attributed to the master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, when does riding a slave's animal break a 'no riding' oath about the master?
A) Only if the slave's debt is consummate.
B) Always, because the master owns the slave.
C) Only if the master intended it and the slave has no consummate debt.
D) Never, because the animal is not the master's.

Key Takeaway
The inclusion of a slave's animal in an oath about the master's animal depends on intention and the presence of consummate debt, with varying positions among the Hanafi imams.

The Ruling on Oaths About the First and Last Slave Owned
Rulings on oaths regarding the first and last slave owned

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for an oath about the first slave owned, identify when the oath is fulfilled, and explain how the word 'alone' (وحده) modifies the ruling.

Core Idea
In oaths about the 'first' slave, simultaneous ownership prevents fulfillment; adding 'alone' enforces exclusivity.

Oaths often hinge on precise wording. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies how Hanafi jurists interpret expressions like 'the first slave I own' and 'alone' when determining which slave is freed. Understanding these nuances prevents unintended violations of oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and its wording
Recognize the exact phrase used: 'أول عبد أملكه فهو حر' (first slave I own is free) or 'آخر عبد أملكه فهو حر' (last slave I own is free). Note that the wording determines the ruling.
The word 'first' (أول) implies precedence in time; 'last' (آخر) implies a succeeding individual.
2
Understand the condition of 'first'
For 'first' to be fulfilled, the slave must be owned before any other. If two are owned simultaneously, there is no 'first' because neither precedes the other.
Simultaneous ownership by purchase, gift, or inheritance prevents determination of the first.
3
Apply to scenarios without 'alone'
Consider the cases: if the owner says 'first slave I own' and then owns one slave, that slave is freed. If he owns two at once, none is freed. If he owns two then a third, none of the three is freed because the third came after the pair.
The ruling for 'last' is similar: if the owner dies after owning one slave, the slave is not freed because 'last' requires a later individual to exist.
4
Analyze the effect of adding 'alone'
Adding 'وحده' (alone) changes the ruling. It means 'the first slave I own exclusively, without any other sharing in that ownership at the moment of acquisition.' Thus, if the owner first acquires two slaves together, they are not 'alone'; but if he later acquires a third, that third is the first slave owned 'alone' because at the moment of its acquisition, no other slave was being acquired simultaneously.
The word 'وحده' in accusative (نصب) denotes negation of sharing in the essence (ownership), while in jar (جر) it would be a description. The correct interpretation is accusative, meaning exclusivity in the state of ownership.
5
Distinguish between 'one' and 'alone'
If the oath uses 'واحداً' (one) instead of 'وحده', the ruling is the same as for 'first' without 'alone'—it does not add exclusivity. The passage uses the example of saying 'in the house there is one man' (which is true even if a child is present) versus 'in the house there is a man alone' (which requires no one else).
This distinction is based on common linguistic usage and is applied analogically to oaths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عتق الحي وحده أول عبد أملكه فهو حر فملك عبداً عتق ولو ملك عبدين ثم آخر لا يعتق واحد منهم ولو زاد وحده عتق الثالث فلو قال آخر عبد أملكه فهو حر فملك عبداً ومات لم يعتق لأن الآخر بكسر الخاء فرد لاحق ولا فملك الذات ووحده يقتضيه في الفعل المقرون به دون الذات ولهذا صدق الرجل في قوله في الدار رجل واحد وإن كان معه فيها صبي أو امرأة، وكذب في ذلك إذا قال وحده. وإذا كان كذلك قلنا إذا قال واحداً أنه أضاف العتق إلى عبد مطلق لأن قوله واحداً» لم يفد أمراً زائداً على ما أفاده لفظ أول فكان حكمه كحكمه، وإذا قال وحده فقد أضاف العتق إلى أول عبد لا يشاركه غيره في التملك والثالث بهذه الصفة فيعتق .اهـ. قال في النهر بعد ذكره الحاصل ما ذكر وبهذا التقرير علمت أن ما في البحر من أن الجر على أنه صفة للعبد كالإضافة أعني وحده مدفوع بل هو كالنصب لأنه يفيد نفي المشاركة الذات اهـ. وفي تلخيص الجامع : لو قال أول عبد سأملکه حر فملك عبدين ثم عبداً لم يحنث لفقد التفرد في المثنى والسبق في الفرد كذا أملكه واحداً لأنه مناوب لا مغير وحقه الكسر كما في نسخة والنصب لأتباع الفاشي دون الحال إلا أن يعينه فيعتق الثالث كما في وحده إذ هي للتفرد في الحالة والواحد لتفرد الذات .اهـ. وتمام بيانه في شرحه للفارسي هذا وفي حاشية الحموي على
He freed the live one alone. (If he said) 'The first slave I own is free,' and he owned a slave, that slave is freed. If he owned two slaves then another, none of them is freed. If he added 'alone', the third is freed. If he said 'The last slave I own is free,' and he owned a slave and then died, that slave is not freed because 'last' (with kasr al-kha) means a succeeding individual, and he did not possess the essence, and 'alone' requires it in the action connected to it, not in the essence. Thus a man is truthful when he says 'In the house there is one man' even if there is a child or woman with him, but he is lying if he says 'alone'. Since this is so, we say: if he says 'one' (as accusative), he added freedom to an absolute slave because his saying 'one' does not add anything beyond what 'first' indicated, so its ruling is like its ruling. If he says 'alone' (as accusative/status), then he added freedom to a first slave whom no other shares in ownership, and the third is of this description, so he is freed. The author of al-Nahr said after mentioning the gist: By this explanation you know that what is in al-Bahr about jar (case) being as a description of the slave like idafa (i.e., 'alone') is refuted; rather it is like nasb (accusative) because it denotes negation of sharing in essence. In Talkhis al-Jami': If he said 'The first slave I will own is free,' and he owned two then a slave, he does not violate the oath due to lack of uniqueness in the pair and precedence in the individual. Similarly, if he said 'I own one' (with nasb), it is a synonym, not a changer, and its right is kasr as in one copy, and nasb is following the common usage, not an adverb, unless he specifies it; then the third is freed as in 'alone' because it denotes uniqueness in the state, and 'one' denotes uniqueness in essence. Its full explanation is in his commentary on al-Farsi and in Hashiyat al-Hamawi.
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage explains oaths about the 'first' or 'last' slave a person owns. The key is that 'first' refers to precedence in time and uniqueness in ownership at the moment of acquisition. If a man says 'The first slave I own is free' and he owns one slave, that slave is freed. If he owns two at the same time, no slave is freed because the 'first' cannot be determined between two simultaneous acquisitions. If he adds 'alone', then the third slave (after two owned together) is freed because 'alone' enforces exclusivity at the moment of acquisition. For 'last', it refers to the final slave owned; if he owns a slave and dies, the slave is not freed because death terminates ownership and 'last' requires a later individual. The passage also distinguishes between 'one' (واحداً) and 'alone' (وحده): 'one' does not add specificity beyond 'first', while 'alone' adds exclusivity. The commentator resolves a dispute in al-Bahr about the grammatical case of 'وحده' concluding it is for negation of sharing in essence.

Key Terms
الأول (al-awwal): The first individual in time; requires precedence and uniqueness at the moment of acquisition.
وحده (wahdahu): An adverb/noun meaning 'alone' used to exclude any other from sharing in the attribute (here, ownership).
آخر (akhar): The last or succeeding individual; in oaths, it requires a later individual to exist.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a man says 'The first slave I own is free' and then owns two slaves at the same time, what is the ruling?
A) Both slaves are freed.
B) The first acquired is freed.
C) Neither slave is freed.
D) The older slave is freed.

Key Takeaway
The precise wording of an oath determines its fulfillment; 'first' requires uniqueness in time, and 'alone' adds exclusivity.

The Effect of 'Alone' in Oaths of Manumission
Explains the legal effect of the oath with and without 'wahdahu'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the legal effects of the oath 'The first slave I own is free' with and without the word 'alone' (wahdahu) and apply the ruling to variations involving conditional descriptions.

Core Idea
Adding 'alone' changes the ruling: the first slave owned in a state of solitude is freed, not merely the first in sequence.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the commentator expands on a simple oath about manumission to show how a single word—'alone'—fundamentally alters the outcome. Understanding this nuance is essential for correctly applying oaths in practice, as it determines which slave (if any) is freed when multiple slaves are owned.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base oath and its plain meaning
Consider the oath: 'The first slave I own is free.' Without any qualifiers, the word 'first' indicates temporal precedence. If the person owns a single slave, that slave is freed because he is the first.
Here, 'first' is simply a name for a preceding individual.
2
Determine the ruling when multiple slaves are owned
If the person owns two slaves simultaneously and then a third, none is freed. The first two are not individually the first (they share the moment), and the third is not preceding. Thus, no slave fits the description.
Exclusivity (تفرد) is absent in the first two, and precedence (سبق) is absent in the third.
3
Analyze the effect of adding 'wahdahu' (alone)
When the oath is 'The first slave I own alone is free', the word 'alone' refers to the state of ownership, not the essence. The first slave owned in a state of solitude (without another slave at the same moment) is freed. If two slaves are owned together, they are not 'alone', so they are not freed. When a third slave is later owned alone, he is the first owned in solitude, so he is freed.
Linguistically, 'wahdahu' indicates a condition of the action (حال), not the subject.
4
Extend to variations with conditions
The same logic applies if the oath specifies a quality, e.g., 'bought with dinars' or 'black'. The first slave meeting that condition is freed. If the condition is attached to the purchase medium (e.g., 'with dinars'), a slave bought with dirhams is not considered.
The addition of a description for the first slave includes cases like 'first slave I buy that is black'.
5
Contrast with measurable goods
If the oath is about a measurable quantity (e.g., 'the first kurr I own is charity'), owning a kurr and a half does not trigger the oath because the half mixes with the whole in measurable items, forming a single entity. This differs from slaves and garments, which remain distinct.
The principle: in weighable/measurable goods, partial quantities merge; in discrete items, they do not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان باب اليمين في الطلاق والعتاق
عتق الحي وحده أول عبد أملكه فهو حر فملك عبداً عتق ولو ملك عبدين ثم آخر لا يعتق واحد منهم ولو زاد وحده عتق الثالث
قوله : (أول عبد أملكه فهو حر فملك عبد اعتق ولو ملك عبدين ثم آخر لا يعتق واحد منهم لأن الأول اسم لفرد سابق وقد وجد في المسألة الأولى وانعدم التفرد في الثانية في الأوليين وانعدم السبق في الثالث فانعدمت الأولية قوله : ولو زاد وحده عتق الثالث) أي لو قال أول عبد أملكه وحده فهو حر فملك عبدين ثم ملك آخر عتق العبد الثالث لأنه يراد به التفرد في حال الملك لأن سبب وحده للحال لغة والثالث سابق في هذا الوصف، ولا فرق بين أن يذكر الملك أو الشراء، ومراد المصنف من زيادة وحده أنه زاد وصفاً للأول سواء كان وحده أو لا فيشمل ما لو قال أول عبد أشتريه بالدنانير فهو حر فاشترى عبداً بالدراهم أو بالعروض ثم اشترى عبداً بالدنانير فإنه يعتق وكذا لو قال أول عبد أشتريه أسود فهو حر فاشترى عبد أبيض ثم أسود فإنه يعتق وقيد بوحده لأنه لو قال أول عبد أشتريه واحداً فهو حر فاشترى عبدين ثم اشترى عبداً فإنه لا يعتق الثالث لاحتمال أن يكون حالاً للعبد أو للمالك فلا يعتق بالشك وتمامه في التبيين وواحداً بالنصب على أنه حال، وأما إذا كان مجروراً فهو صفه للعبد فهو كوحده كما لا يخفى ولو قال أول عبد أملكه فهو حر عبداً ونصف عبد عتق العبد الكامل لأن نصف العبد ليس بعبد فلم يشاركه في اسمه فلا يقطع عنه اسم الأولية والفردية كما لو ملك معه ثوباً أو نحوه بخلاف ما إذا قال أول كر أملكه فهو هدي فملك كراً ونصفاً حيث لا يلزمه شيء لأن النصف يزاحم الكل في المكيلات والموزونات لأنه بالضم يصير شيئاً واحداً بخلاف الثياب والعبيد
The book of oaths, chapter on oaths in divorce and manumission. The emancipation of the living alone: 'The first slave I own is free.' If he owns a slave, it is free. If he owns two slaves and then another, none of them is free. If he added 'alone,' the third is free. He said: (The first slave I own is free, so if he owns a slave, it is free; if he owns two slaves and then another, none is free because 'first' is a name for a preceding individual, which is found in the first case but in the second case, exclusivity is absent in the first two, and precedence is absent in the third, so 'firstness' is lost. He said: 'If he added 'alone,' the third is free.') That is, if he said 'The first slave I own alone is free,' then he owned two slaves and then owned another, the third slave is free because what is intended is exclusivity at the time of ownership, because 'alone' refers to the state linguistically, and the third precedes in this description. There is no difference between mentioning ownership or purchase. The author's intent in adding 'alone' is that he added a description for the first, whether it is 'alone' or not. This includes if he said 'The first slave I buy with dinars is free,' then he bought a slave with dirhams or goods, then bought a slave with dinars; he is free. Similarly, if he said 'The first slave I buy is black is free,' then he bought a white slave and then a black one; the black is free. He restricted it with 'alone' because if he said 'The first slave I buy is one is free,' then bought two slaves and then bought a slave, the third is not free, due to the possibility that 'one' may be a circumstantial accusative for the slave or the owner, so he does not become free due to doubt. Its completion is in al-Tabyin. 'One' with nasb as a circumstantial accusative; if it is majrur, it is an adjective for the slave, so it is like 'alone,' as is not hidden. If he said 'The first slave I own is free, a slave and a half,' the complete slave is free because half a slave is not a slave, so it does not share its name, so the name of 'firstness' and individuality is not cut off from it, as if he owned with it a garment or the like. Unlike if he said 'The first kurr I own is a charity,' then he owned a kurr and a half; nothing is incumbent on him because the half competes with the whole in measurable and weighable items because by joining it becomes one thing, unlike garments and slaves.
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq commenting on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It explains the oath 'The first slave I own is free.' The ruling: if the person owns a single slave, that slave is freed because he is the first. If he owns two slaves simultaneously and then a third, none is freed because the first two are not individually the first (they share the moment), and the third is not preceding. But if he adds 'alone' (وحده) to the oath, then only a slave owned without any other slave at the same time is considered 'first'. Thus, if he first owns two slaves together, they are not 'alone', so they are not freed. When he later owns a third slave alone, that third is the first slave owned in a state of solitude, so he is freed. The sharh elaborates on the linguistic nuance: 'alone' (وحده) refers to the state of the action, not the essence. So the third slave is the first to be owned in a state of solitude. The commentator extends this to variations: if the oath specifies a condition like 'bought with dinars' or 'black', the first slave meeting that condition is freed. Also, if he says 'one' (واحداً) instead of 'alone', it may not free the third because 'one' could be an adjective for the slave (making it like 'alone') or a circumstantial accusative for the owner (meaning 'alone by himself'), leading to doubt; doubt prevents emancipation. The passage also contrasts with measurable goods: if one swears 'the first kurr I own is charity' and owns a kurr and a half, nothing is due because the half mixes with the whole in measureables, but not in slaves or garments.

Key Terms
أول (awwal): The first in a sequence; a name for a preceding individual
وحده (wahdahu): Alone; indicates exclusivity in the state of the action, not the essence
أول عبد (awwal 'abd): The first slave owned; requires both precedence and exclusivity when 'alone' is added

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The first slave owned
B) The third slave
C) None
D) All three

Key Takeaway
The addition of 'alone' to an oath about 'first slave' changes the requirement from temporal precedence to temporal isolation, freeing only a slave owned without concurrent ownership of another slave.

When a Single Slave Cannot Be 'Last': The Logical Ruling
Ruling on a single slave in the 'last slave' oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when only one slave is owned upon death and explain why he is not freed using the definition of 'ākhir' as a subsequent individual.

Core Idea
A slave cannot be 'last' if there is no preceding slave; the term requires a prior.

The oath 'The last slave I own is free' seems straightforward, but Hanafi jurists dissect it carefully. What if the owner dies having only ever owned one slave? Can that slave be considered 'last'? The answer lies in the precise meaning of the term 'ākhir' (last) in Arabic. In this lesson, we explore the ruling and the linguistic-logical reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and the scenario
The oath is 'The last slave I own is free' (ākhir ʿabd amlikuhu huwa ḥurr). The scenario: the owner dies having purchased only one slave during his lifetime.
The slave is the only slave ever owned.
2
Understand the Hanafi definition of 'ākhir'
The text defines 'ākhir' (with kasra on the khā') as 'a subsequent individual' (fard lāḥiq) – one that comes after a preceding one. Without a prior, there can be no subsequent.
This is a semantic analysis of the word.
3
Apply the definition to the single slave case
Since there is no earlier slave, the lone slave cannot be described as 'last'. Therefore, the condition of the oath is not fulfilled, and the slave is not freed.
The ruling is 'lam yuʿtaq' (not freed).
4
Contrast with the concept of 'first' (awwal)
The passage refers to a previous similar issue: in 'first slave I buy is free', the first slave is determined by lack of any prior purchase. Here, 'last' requires actual existence of a prior, not just absence of a later one.
This distinction is critical from Fath al-Qadir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (فلو قال آخر عبد أملكه فهو حر فملك عبداً ومات لم يعتق لأن الآخر بكسر الخاء فرد لاحق ولا سابق له فلا يكون لاحقاً ولهذا يدخل في الأول فيستحيل أن يدخل في ضده، وفي فتح القدير : وهذه المسألة مع التي تقدمت تحقق أن المعتبر في تحقق الآخرية وجود سابق بالفعل وفي الأولية عدم تقدم غيره لا وجود آخر متأخر عنه وإلا لم يعتق المشتري في قوله أول عبد أشتريه فهو حر إذا لم يشتر بعده غيره .اهـ. والضمير في «مات) راجع إلى المالك قوله : (فلو اشترى عبداً ثم عبداً ثم مات عتق الآخر لأنه فرد لاحق فاتصف بالآخرية، ولم يذكر المصنف وقت عتقه للاختلاف فعند الإمام يستند العتق إلى وقت الشراء حتى يعتبر من جميع المال إن كان اشتراه في صحته عند أبي حنيفة وإلا عتق من الثلث وعندهما يعتق مقتصراً على حالة الموت فيعتبر من الثلث على كل حال لأن الآخرية لا تثبت إلا بعدم شراء غيره بعده وذلك يتحقق بالموت فكان الشرط متحققاً عند الموت فيقتصر عليه، ولأبي حنيفة أن الموت معرف، فأما اتصافه بالآخرية فمن وقت الشراء فيثبت مستنداً وعلى هذا الخلاف تعليق الطلقات الثلاث به كما إذا قال آخر امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق ثلاثاً فيقع عند الموت عندهما وترث بحكم أنه فار ولها مهر واحد وعليها العدة أبعد الأجلين من عدة الطلاق والوفاة، فإن كان الطلاق رجعياً فعليها عدة الوفاة وتحد، وعنده يقع منذ تزوجها، فإن كان دخل بها فلها مهر ونصف مهر بالدخول بشبهة ونصف مهر بالطلاق قبل الدخول وعدتها بالحيض بلا حداد ولا ترث منه، ولو قال آخر امرأة أتزوجها طالق فتزوج امرأة ثم أخرى ثم طلق الأولى ثم تزوجها ثم مات طلقت التي تزوجها مرة لأن التي أعاد عليها التزوج اتصفت بكونها أولى فلا تتصف بالآخرية للتضاد كمن قال آخر عبد أضربه فهو حر فضرب عبداً ثم ضرب آخر ثم أعاد الضرب في الأول ثم مات عتق المضروب مرة بخلاف الفعل كما قدمناه أول الباب وقيد بموت المولى لأنه لا يعلم أن الثاني آخراً لا بموت المولى الجواز أن يشتري غيره فيكون هو الآخر ولم يذكر المصنف الأوسط.
He said: 'If he said: The last slave I own is free, then he owned a slave and died, it is not free, because 'last' (with kasr of kha') is a subsequent individual, and there is no preceding one for him, so he cannot be a subsequent. Thus, it enters into the first, and it is impossible to enter into its opposite. In Fath al-Qadir: This issue together with the preceding one confirms that what is considered in realizing 'lastness' is the actual existence of a prior, and in 'firstness' the non-existence of anything else before it, not the existence of another after it; otherwise, the buyer in his statement 'The first slave I buy is free' would not be freed if he did not buy another after it.' The pronoun in 'died' refers to the owner. He said: 'If he bought a slave, then a slave, then died, the last is free, because he is a subsequent individual, so he is described with lastness. The author did not mention the time of its emancipation due to disagreement: according to Imam (Abu Hanifa), the emancipation is based on the time of purchase, so it is considered from all the wealth if he bought it during his health according to Abu Hanifa; otherwise, it is from one-third. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is restricted to the state of death, so it is from one-third in all cases, because lastness is not established except by not buying another after it, and that is realized by death, so the condition is realized at death, so it is restricted to it. And for Abu Hanifa, death is a definer (mu'arif), but its being described with lastness is from the time of purchase, so it is established as based (on that time). And on this difference depends the suspension of triple divorce, as if he said 'The last woman I marry is thrice divorced'; it occurs at death according to them, and she inherits as a widow, and she gets one mahr, and her 'iddah is the longer of the two terms: the 'iddah of divorce and of death. If the divorce is revocable, then she has the 'iddah of death and observes mourning. According to him (Abu Hanifa), it occurs from the time he married her. If he had consummated, she gets one and a half mahr: half mahr for consummation by shubha and half mahr for divorce before consummation, and her 'iddah is by menstruation without mourning, and she does not inherit from him. If he said 'The last woman I marry is divorced' and he married a woman, then another, then divorced the first, then remarried her, then died; the woman he married once is divorced, because the one he remarried is described as first, so she cannot be described as last due to opposition. Like one who said 'The last slave I beat is free' and he beat a slave, then beat another, then repeated beating on the first, then died; the one beaten once is freed, unlike actions as we presented at the beginning of the chapter. And it is restricted to the death of the master, because it is not known that the second is last; without the master's death, it is possible that he may buy another, making that one the last. And the author did not mention the middle (slave).
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the oath 'The last slave I own is free.' If the owner dies having owned only one slave, that slave is not freed because there is no previous slave to make him 'last'. But if he owned two slaves sequentially and then dies, the second slave is the last and is freed. The key ikhtilaf: at what moment does the emancipation take effect? According to Abu Hanifa, the emancipation is retroactive to the time of purchase, so if he bought the slave in health, it is from all his wealth; if in illness, from one-third. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn), the emancipation takes effect only at death, because 'lastness' is confirmed only when no further purchase occurs, which is at death. Therefore, it is always from one-third. The passage also applies this to marriage and divorce: if a man says 'The last woman I marry is thrice divorced,' and he marries sequentially, then dies, the last wife is divorced at death according to Sahibayn, and she inherits and observes the longer 'iddah. According to Abu Hanifa, the divorce occurs at the time of marriage, so she does not inherit and her 'iddah is by menstruation without mourning. The commentary also addresses a case where a man remarries a former wife after divorce; she becomes the first again, so she cannot be the last. The ruling about the 'middle' slave is not mentioned in the matn but is taken from al-Bada'i'.

Key Terms
آخر (ākhir): Last; a subsequent individual that comes after a previous one. Here, it requires an actual prior.
أول (awwal): First; the one that has no prior. Contrast with 'ākhir'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi analysis in this passage, what makes a slave the 'last' (ākhir)?
A) Being the only slave owned at death
B) Being owned after at least one other slave
C) Being the most recently purchased slave
D) Being freed by the master's death

Key Takeaway
A slave cannot be 'last' without a prior slave; the term itself implies a sequence.

When Does the Last Slave Become Free? Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the timing of emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the timing of emancipation in the 'last slave' oath, and apply the consequences to wealth distribution and analogous cases in marriage and divorce.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: emancipation at purchase; Sahibayn: at death – the difference turns on when 'lastness' is determined.

When a person owns two slaves sequentially and dies, the second slave is the last and is freed. But at what moment does the emancipation take effect? This seemingly technical point has significant practical consequences: whether the slave is freed from all assets (if purchased in health) or only from one-third. The Hanafi school is divided: Abu Hanifa holds that emancipation retroacts to the time of purchase, while his two disciples Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) maintain that it takes effect only at death. This lesson unpacks the proofs and extends the same dispute to marriage and divorce.

Guided Path
1
State the shared scenario
The oath: 'The last slave I own is free.' The owner buys slave A, then slave B, then dies. All agree that slave B is the last and is freed.
The ikhtilaf is not about which slave is freed, but when the emancipation is considered to have occurred.
2
Present Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa: emancipation is based on the time of purchase. If bought in health, from all wealth; if in illness, from one-third. His proof: death is a 'definer' (muʿarif) – it reveals that the second slave is last, but the attribute of lastness was established at the time of purchase because no later purchase occurred. Thus the emancipation is retroactive.
The key term is 'muʿarif' – death uncovers, not creates, the condition.
3
Present the Sahibayn's position and proof
Sahibayn: emancipation only at death. Since lastness is only confirmed when no further purchase is possible (i.e., at death), the condition is realized at death. Therefore, the slave is freed from one-third regardless of when purchased.
Their proof: 'lastness is not established except by not buying another after it, and that is realized by death.'
4
Analyze the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the second slave was bought during health: Abu Hanifa says from all wealth (so the heirs get less); Sahibayn say from one-third (estate can use up to one-third for emancipation). If bought during illness: both agree from one-third? Actually Abu Hanifa says from one-third if bought in illness, so same as Sahibayn. The real difference is when purchased in health.
Also affects the slave's status retroactively.
5
Extend to analogous cases: marriage and divorce
The passage applies the same logic to an oath: 'The last woman I marry is thrice divorced.' According to Sahibayn, divorce occurs at death; she inherits, gets one mahr, and observes the longer 'iddah. According to Abu Hanifa, divorce occurs at marriage; she does not inherit, gets one and a half mahr (if consummation occurred via shubha), and her 'iddah is by menstruation without mourning.
This shows the dispute pervades all similar conditional oaths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
مُعَرِّف (mu'arif): Definer; something that reveals or uncovers a pre-existing fact. Here, death is a mu'arif for lastness.
الأخرىة (al-ākhirayya): Lastness; the attribute of being last.
الثلث (al-thuluth): One-third of the estate; the maximum that can be bequeathed to non-heirs or used for emancipations in terminal illness.

Key Takeaway

Determining the Middle Slave in an Oath
Defining the 'middle' slave in sequential purchases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which slave (or purchased item) is freed under an oath concerning the 'middle slave' when purchasing sequentially, applying the condition of equal numbers before and after.

Core Idea
The middle slave exists only when the total purchased is odd and has the same number before and after.

Sometimes a person makes an oath regarding a future purchase, such as 'The middle slave I buy is free.' This seems straightforward, but the legal definition of 'middle' requires exact parity: the slave must have an equal number of purchases before and after it. This lesson from al-Bada'i' explains how to calculate which slave qualifies as the middle in a sequence of purchases.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of 'أوسط'
The term 'middle' means having equal numbers of purchases on both sides. The first and last can never be middle because they lack one side.
2
Recognize the parity condition
The middle exists only when the total number of purchases is odd (وتر), not even (شفع). If even, no slave has equal before and after.
3
Apply to examples
Work through the given examples: 3 slaves → second is middle; 4 slaves → no middle; 5 slaves → third is middle.
4
Generalize the pattern
With each new purchase, when the total becomes odd again, a new slave becomes the middle. The middle shifts as the sequence lengthens.
5
Note the temporal aspect
The determination is based on the final set of purchases (e.g., when the person stops buying or the oath is fulfilled). The middle is not evaluated at intermediate steps.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في البدائع : ولو قال أوسط عبد أشتريه فهو حر فكل عبد فرد له حاشيتان متساويتان فيما قبله وبعده فهو أوسط ولا يكون الأول ولا الآخر وسطاً أبداً ولا يكون الوسط إلا في وتر ولا يكون في شفع، فإذا اشترى عبداً ثم عبداً ثم عبداً فالثاني هو الوسط، فإذا اشترى رابعاً خرج الثاني من أن يكون أوسط، فإذا اشترى خامساً صار الثالث هو الوسط، فإذا اشترى سادساً خرج من أن يكون أوسط وعلى هذا فقس اهـ.
He said in al-Bada'i': If he said 'The middle slave I buy is free,' then every slave that has two equal sides in terms of what is before and after it is the middle. Neither the first nor the last can ever be the middle. The middle exists only in an odd number, not in an even number. So if he buys a slave, then a slave, then a slave, the second is the middle. If he buys a fourth, the second ceases to be the middle. If he buys a fifth, the third becomes the middle. If he buys a sixth, it ceases to be the middle. And so on.
Source pages p. 575

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from al-Bada'i' (a Hanafi work) cited in the sharh. It deals with an oath concerning the 'middle slave' (أوسط عبد) one purchases. The ruling: the middle slave is determined by having an equal number of slaves purchased before and after him. Therefore, the middle exists only when an odd total number of slaves are purchased. For example, if three slaves are bought in sequence, the second is the middle. If a fourth is bought, the second is no longer the middle because now there are two before (first and second? careful: the fourth purchase makes the sequence: first, second, third, fourth. The second has one before (first) and two after (third and fourth), so not equal. So the middle disappears. If a fifth is bought, the third becomes the middle (two before: first and second; two after: fourth and fifth). The rule: with each additional purchase, the middle shifts or disappears. The oath applies only to the slave who is the middle at the time the oath's condition is triggered (usually when the last slave is bought? but here it's a conditional statement: 'the middle slave I buy is free' means whichever slave turns out to be the middle at the end of purchases? The text does not specify the timing. Typically, in such oaths, the condition is fulfilled when the person stops buying (e.g., at death or when he says he will buy no more). But here it's a general rule from al-Bada'i' explaining the definition of middle.

Key Terms
أوسط (awsaṭ): Middle; having equal numbers before and after
وتر (watr): Odd number (as opposed to شفع, even)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The second
B) The third
C) None
D) The first

Key Takeaway
The middle slave is defined by having an equal number of purchases before and after, so it exists only when the total number of purchases is odd.

Oath on Slaves Bringing News: Multiple Bearers and the Diyanatan/Qada'an Distinction
Explaining the ruling when multiple slaves bring news under a conditional oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when multiple slaves bring news under an oath to free the bearer, and distinguish between diyanatan and qada'an in interpreting the oath.

Core Idea
First to bring news is freed; if together, all are freed. Inner intent affects religious truth but not court rulings.

This lesson examines a conditional oath common in historical contexts: a master says 'Every slave who brings me this news is free.' When multiple slaves bring the news, complications arise. The Hanafi school provides detailed guidance based on whether the bearers came separately or together, and distinguishes between religious truth (diyanatan) and judicial enforcement (qada'an). Understanding this ruling requires appreciating the linguistic nature of 'bisharah' (bringing news) and the principle of intention in oaths.

Guided Path
1
Understand the oath and its condition
The oath is: 'Every slave who brings me such news is free.' The condition is the act of bringing the specified news. Identify that the condition is a single act that can be fulfilled by multiple agents.
The condition is worded as 'who brings me', which can apply to one or many.
2
Determine the ruling when bearers come separately
If three slaves bring the news one after another, the first bearer fulfills the condition. Subsequent bearers do not cause further release because the condition has already been met.
The first bearer is freed; later bearers are not.
3
Determine the ruling when bearers come together
If all bring the news simultaneously (مَعاً), the plural expression 'who bring me' applies to all. The condition is satisfied by all at once, so all are freed.
The text says: عتقوا (they are all freed).
4
Analyze the linguistic nature of 'bisharah'
The commentator explains that 'bisharah' (bringing news) is an 'ism al-ashbah' (name of similarity), a generic term that applies to any act of bringing good news. This allows flexibility: if the speaker intends by one bearer another (i.e., he means the one who brings the news first), he is truthful because the common meaning (unity of the act) connects them.
This linguistic point underpins the distinction between separate and simultaneous fulfillment.
5
Distinguish between diyanatan and qada'an
Diyanatan (religiously) considers the speaker's true intent; qada'an (judicially) relies on the apparent expression. If the speaker intends 'one' slave alone (e.g., by saying 'واحداً'), he is truthful both diyanatan and qada'an due to strictness. In the opposite case (when he intends multiple by using the singular?), he is truthful diyanatan but not qada'an due to leniency. This distinction affects how a court would rule vs. the master's religious obligation.
Refer to the commentary for the exact nuance: 'في عكسه يصدق ديانة لا قضاء'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل عبد بشرني بكذا فهو حر فبشره ثلاثة متفرقون عتق الأول وإن بشروه معاً عتقوا وصح شراء أبيه
قوله : (كل عبد بشرني بكذا فهو حر فبشره ثلاثة متفرقون عتق الأول لأن البشارة اسم الاشباه : فإن عنى بأحدهما الآخر صدق لما بينهما من المعنى الجامع وهو الوحدة لكنه إن عنى بقوله واحداً وحده يصدق ديانة وقضاء لما فيه من التغليظ، وفي عكسه يصدق ديانة لا قضاء لما فيه من التخفيف اهـ وهو مستفاد من عبارة التلخيص كما أوضحه شارحه فراجعه .
Every slave who brings me such news is free. If three bring news separately, the first is freed. If they bring news together, they are all freed. And the purchase of his father is valid. He said: (Every slave who brings me such news is free: if three bring news separately, the first is freed because 'bringing news' is a name of similarity: if he intends by one of them the other, he is truthful due to the common meaning between them, which is unity. But if he intends by his saying 'one' alone, he is truthful both religiously and judicially because of the strictness in it. In the opposite case, he is truthful religiously but not judicially because of the leniency in it.) This is derived from the expression of al-Talkhis as clarified by its commentator; refer to it.
Source pages p. 575

Commentary
This passage covers the oath 'Every slave who brings me such news is free.' If three slaves bring the news separately, the first one is freed because he is the first to bring the news. If they all bring it together, they are all freed because the plural 'who bring me' can apply to all. The commentator introduces a linguistic nuance: the word 'بشارة' (bringing news) is a name for similarity (اسم الاشباه), meaning it applies to any act of bringing news. If the oath-taker intends by one slave the other (i.e., he means the one who brings the news first), he is truthful because the common meaning is 'bringing news'. However, if he intends by his words 'one' (واحداً) i.e., he says 'every slave who brings me such news, one (of them) is free', then there is a difference: if he means 'one alone', he is truthful both religiously (diyanatan) and judicially (qada'an) because it is a strict interpretation. But if he means the opposite (i.e., that all are free?), the text is ambiguous; the commentator says in the opposite case (i.e., when he intends multiple) he is truthful religiously but not judicially because of leniency. The passage also mentions 'the purchase of his father is valid', which is another part of the matn indicating that if a slave buys his own father, the purchase is valid (probably a separate ruling from another context).

Key Terms
البشارة (al-bishārah): Bringing news; a name of similarity (اسم الاشباه) applying to any act of bringing good news
ديانة (diyānatan): Religiously, in the sight of Allah; based on the person's true intent
قضاء (qaḍā'an): Judicially, in court; based on apparent expressions

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an owner says 'Every slave who brings me such news is free' and three slaves bring the news together, what is the ruling?
A) Only the first is freed
B) All three are freed
C) None are freed
D) The last is freed

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when multiple slaves bring news under an oath to free the bearer, the first is freed if they come separately; all are freed if they come together, and the distinction between diyanatan and qada'an applies to the speaker's intent.

When Does the Last Slave Go Free? The Ruling on Oaths of Manumission
Explains the ruling for oaths regarding the last purchased slave and the intra-Hanafi disagreement on timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the time of manumission for the last purchased slave, distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and determine the financial consequence (from whole estate vs one-third).

Core Idea
The last slave is freed, but when—at purchase or death—determines whether the value comes from the whole estate or one-third.

In this passage, Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a classic Hanafi oath: 'The last slave I purchase is free.' Both Abu Hanifa and his two disciples agree the last slave is freed, but they differ on when the manumission takes effect. This disagreement has real financial consequences, especially if the owner dies before the slave is freed. The text also draws an analogy to divorce to illustrate the same principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the agreed ruling
The person says, 'The last slave I purchase is free.' He buys two slaves, then dies. The second slave is freed. This is agreed upon.
2
Isolate the point of disagreement
The disagreement is about the time of manumission. Does it relate back to purchase or only occur at death?
3
Understand Abu Hanifa's argument
Abu Hanifa says the quality of 'lastness' is acquired at the moment of purchase, so the manumission is retroactive to that time. If he was healthy at purchase, the slave's value comes from the whole estate; if ill, from one-third.
Key: al-ākhariyya is a description that attaches at purchase.
4
Understand the Sahibayn's argument
The Sahibayn say 'lastness' is only confirmed at death because no further purchase is possible. Thus manumission occurs at death and always from one-third, protecting creditors.
They prioritize caution for heirs and creditors.
5
Apply the ikhtilaf to the divorce analogy
The same reasoning applies if he says 'The last woman I marry is divorced three times.' The Sahibayn say divorce occurs at death; Abu Hanifa says at marriage.
This shows the principle is consistent across different oaths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو اشترى عبداً ثم عبداً ثم مات عتق الآخر لأنه فرد لاحق فاتصف بالآخرية، ولم يذكر المصنف وقت عتقه للاختلاف فعند الإمام يستند العتق إلى وقت الشراء حتى يعتبر من جميع المال إن كان اشتراه في صحته عند أبي حنيفة وإلا عتق من الثلث وعندهما يعتق مقتصراً على حالة الموت فيعتبر من الثلث على كل حال لأن الآخرية لا تثبت إلا بعدم شراء غيره بعده وذلك يتحقق بالموت فكان الشرط متحققاً عند الموت فيقتصر عليه، ولأبي حنيفة أن الميت [؟] معرف، فأما اتصافه بالآخرية فمن وقت الشراء فيثبت مستنداً وعلى هذا الخلاف تعليق الطلقات الثلاث به كما إذا قال آخر امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق ثلاثاً فيقع عند الموت عندهما وترث بحكم أنها فار ولها مهر واحد وعليها العدة أبعد الأجلين من عدة الطلاق والوفاة، فإن كان الطلاق رجعياً فعليها عدة الوفاة وتحد، وعنده يقع منذ تزوجها، فإن كان دخل بها فلها مهر ونصف مهر بالدخول بشبهة ونصف مهر بالطلاق قبل الدخول وعدتها بالحيض بلا حداد ولا ترث منه، ولو قال آخر امرأة أتزوجها طالق فتزوج امرأة ثم أخرى ثم طلق الأولى ثم تزوجها ثم مات طلقت التي تزوجها مرة لأن التي أعاد عليها التزوج اتصفت بكونها أولى فلا تتصف بالآخرية للتضاد كمن قال آخر عبد أضربه فهو حر فضرب عبداً ثم ضرب آخر ثم أعاد الضرب في الأول ثم مات عتق المضروب مرة بخلاف الفعل كما قدمناه أول الباب وقيد بموت المولى لأنه لا يعلم أن الثاني آخراً لا بموت المولى الجواز أن يشتري غيره فيكون هو الآخر ولم يذكر المصنف الأوسط. قال في البدائع : ولو قال أوسط عبد أشتريه فهو حر فكل عبد فرد له حاشيتان متساويتان فيما قبله وبعده فهو أوسط ولا يكون الأول ولا الآخر وسطاً أبداً ولا يكون الوسط إلا في وتر ولا يكون في شفع، فإذا اشترى عبداً ثم عبداً ثم عبداً فالثاني هو الوسط، فإذا اشترى رابعاً خرج الثاني من أن يكون أوسط، فإذا اشترى خامساً صار الثالث هو الوسط، فإذا اشترى سادساً خرج من أن يكون أوسط وعلى هذا فقس اهـ
If he purchased a slave, then another slave, then died, the last slave is freed, because he is a later individual who is described as last. The author (al-Quduri) did not mention the time of his manumission due to a difference. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the manumission is linked to the time of purchase, so it is considered from all of the wealth if he purchased it during his health, otherwise it is from one-third. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is confined to the state of death, so it is from one-third in all cases, because 'lastness' is only established by no other purchase after him, and that is realized at death, so the condition is fulfilled at death, so it is confined to it. Abu Hanifa's proof is that death is the identifier. As for its being described as last, it is from the time of purchase, so it is established retroactively. Based on this difference, three divorces are suspended similarly, as if he said, 'The last woman I marry is divorced three times' – it occurs at death according to the two companions, and she inherits because she is a widow, and she has one mahr, and her iddah is the longer of the two terms: iddah of divorce and iddah of death. If the divorce was revocable, she observes the iddah of death and must observe mourning (ihadad). According to Abu Hanifa, it occurs at the time he married her. If he had consummated, she gets mahr plus half mahr due to doubtful intercourse and half mahr due to divorce before consummation, and her iddah is by menses without mourning, and she does not inherit from him. And if he said, 'The last woman I marry is divorced' and he married a woman, then another, then divorced the first, then married her again, then died, the woman he married once (the first) is divorced, because the woman he remarried is described as first, not last, due to contradiction, like if he said, 'The last slave I beat is free' and he beat a slave, then beat another, then beat the first again, then died, the slave beaten once is freed, unlike the action (beating) as we mentioned at the beginning of the chapter. This is conditioned on the master's death, because it is not known that the second is last except by the master's death, as he could still purchase another and thus be the last. The author did not mention the middle. Al-Badai'i said: If he said, 'The middle slave I purchase is free' – then every slave that has two equal sides before and after is the middle, and the first and last are never middle, and the middle only exists in odd numbers, not even. So if he purchased a slave, then another, then another, the second is the middle. If he purchases a fourth, the second ceases to be middle. If he purchases a fifth, the third becomes middle. If he purchases a sixth, the third ceases to be middle. And so on, analogize.
Source pages p. 575

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discussing oaths involving ordinal numbers (last, middle) in manumission. It begins by presenting the ruling for the last slave purchased: if someone says 'the last slave I purchase is free' and then purchases two slaves sequentially and dies, the second slave is freed. The critical question is when the manumission takes effect. Abu Hanifa holds that it relates back to the moment of purchase, so if he was healthy at purchase, the slave's value is taken from the entire estate, not from the one-third bequest limit. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the condition of being 'last' is only confirmed at death, so the manumission occurs at death and is always from the one-third. Abu Hanifa argues that 'lastness' is a description acquired at purchase, so the manumission is linked to that moment. The text then analogizes to divorce: if a man says 'the last woman I marry is divorced three times' and marries two women, the second is divorced at death according to the Sahibayn, with full inheritance rights, while Abu Hanifa says the divorce occurs at marriage, so she is divorced immediately and inherits nothing. Finally, the author explains the ruling for the middle slave: it requires an odd number, and the middle changes as more slaves are purchased. This passage is a typical example of Hanafi extrapolation and intra-school disagreement, centering on the interpretation of conditions in oaths.

Key Terms
الآخرية (al-ākhariyya): The quality of being last; the condition that defines which slave is freed under the oath 'the last slave I purchase is free'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school (mufta bihi), when does the manumission of the last slave purchased take effect?
A) At the time of purchase
B) At the owner's death
C) At the time of the oath
D) At the time the slave is given to the freer

Key Takeaway
The last slave purchased is freed, but the timing of manumission (at purchase or at death) determines whether the slave's value comes from the whole estate or only one-third, with the Sahibayn view being the operative position in the Hanafi school.

Who Is the Middle Slave? Determining the Awsat in Oaths of Manumission
Defines the middle slave in oaths and explains its dynamic nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the middle (al-awsat) slave in Hanafi law and apply the rule that it exists only in odd-numbered series and changes as further purchases occur.

Core Idea
The middle slave is determined by having equal numbers before and after; it only exists in odd counts and changes as the series grows.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines the 'middle' slave in oaths of manumission. Unlike the 'last' slave, which is fixed at death, the 'middle' is dynamic—it can shift as the master purchases more slaves. Understanding this rule requires careful ordinal analysis.

Guided Path
1
Define 'middle' (awsat)
The middle slave is the one with an equal number of slaves before and after him in the order of purchase.
2
Recognize the odd-number requirement
The middle can only exist in an odd-numbered series. There is no middle in an even number because no single slave has equal before and after.
1st and last are never middle.
3
Trace how the middle changes with additions
If you purchase three slaves, the second is middle. If you add a fourth, the second ceases to be middle. If you add a fifth, the third becomes middle. Every addition resets the middle.
This is a dynamic definition.
4
Apply the rule to an oath
If someone swears 'The middle slave I purchase is free,' the identity of the freed slave changes as new purchases are made, until the series ends (usually at death).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Ruling on Bearing Good News (Bishara) in Oaths of Manumission
Details the conditions for manumission when an oath is conditioned on receiving good news

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for bishara (good news) in oaths of manumission and apply the Hanafi distinction between bishara, ikhbar, and i'lam to determine when a slave is freed.

Core Idea
Bishara requires truth and joy; the first bearer of good news fulfills the oath, while subsequent ones do not unless they bear together.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling when a master swears that any slave who brings good news (bishara) of a specific event is free. The key is understanding what legally constitutes 'good news' — it must be a true, joyful report of something previously unknown to the master. The commentary clarifies who is freed when multiple slaves bring the news, and how the medium (speech, writing, messenger) affects the oath. A hadith from Ibn Mas'ud provides the proof for the distinction between the first bearer (mubashshir) and subsequent informers (mukhbir).

Guided Path
1
Define bishara
Identify the three conditions: the report must be true, joyful, and of something unknown to the recipient. This distinguishes it from ikhbar (mere report) and i'lam (informing).
2
Apply the ruling to separate bearers
If multiple slaves give news separately, only the first is freed because he first brought the unknown joyful news. Subsequent bearers merely confirm known news and do not trigger manumission.
If they give news together, all are freed because the condition is realized from all simultaneously.
3
Examine the hadith proof
The Prophet's hadith about Ibn Mas'ud shows that Abu Bakr was called 'mubashshir' because he first brought the joyful news, while Umar was merely 'mukhbir'. This establishes the legal distinction.
4
Extend to writing and messengers
Determine that writing and messengers are equivalent to speech for bishara, but for ordinary news (khabar), only face-to-face speech breaks the oath. The condition of truth remains for bishara and i'lam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (كل عبد بشرني بكذا فهو حر فبشره ثلاثة متفرقون عتق الأول لأن البشارة اسم الاشباه : فإن عنى بأحدهما الآخر صدق لما بينهما من المعنى الجامع وهو الوحدة لكنه إن عنى بقوله واحداً وحده يصدق ديانة وقضاء لما فيه من التغليظ، وفي عكسه يصدق ديانة لا قضاء لما فيه من التخفيف اهـ وهو مستفاد من عبارة التلخيص كما أوضحه شارحه فراجعه .  روي كتاب الأيمان باب اليمين في الطلاق والعتاق لخبر سار صدق ليس للمبشر به المخبر وجمع الحروف، وفي الأخريين إفادة البشر والعلم بخلاف ما إذا قال بقدومه لأن باء الإلصاق تقتضي الوجود وهو بالصدق. ويحنث بالإيماء في أعلمتني وبالكتاب والرسول في الكل قوله : (فبشروه
His saying: (Every slave who gives me good news of such-and-such is free, then three people gave him good news separately, the first is freed) because 'bishara' (good news) is a name for joyous information. If he meant by one the other, it is truthful due to the common meaning of unity. But if he meant by his saying 'one' alone, he is truthful in conscience and court due to severity, and in the opposite, he is truthful in conscience only due to leniency. This is derived from the expression of al-Talkhis as its commentator clarified, so refer to it. [PAGE_576] It is reported: The Book of Oaths, Chapter on Oath in Divorce and Manumission. (The good news is) a true joyful report of which the recipient had no knowledge customarily, and this is realized from the first (bearer) not the others. Its origin is that the Prophet (peace be upon him) passed by Ibn Mas'ud while he was reciting the Quran and said: 'Whoever loves to recite the Quran fresh and new as it was revealed, let him recite according to the recitation of Ibn Umm Abd.' So Abu Bakr and Umar (may Allah be pleased with them) hurried to him, and Abu Bakr preceded Umar, and he (Ibn Mas'ud) used to say: 'Abu Bakr gave me good news (bassharani) and Umar informed me (akhbarani).' And if one of them wrote him a letter with the good news, the slave is freed unless he intended face-to-face, because good news may be by writing, since writing from an absent person is like speech from a present one. Similarly, if he sent a messenger, he is freed regarding good news and news (khabar), unlike speech (hadith) where he is not guilty unless face-to-face. And if he swore not to call so-and-so, then he wrote to him calling him, he breaks his oath, as in al-Dhakhira. We qualified it with truthfulness, because if he gave him false good news, it does not occur (the manumission), because although joy appears on the face outwardly, it disappears when the falseness becomes clear. Unlike if he said 'Whoever informs me that so-and-so has arrived, then such-and-such,' and one informed him falsely, he is freed, because it applies to both false and true. Unlike if he said 'Whoever informs me of his arrival,' then truthfulness is necessary, as we have mentioned. So in good news, there is no difference between using the preposition 'bi' or not, unlike news. The difference has been learned in the discussion of the preposition 'bi' in Usul. And writing is like news. So if he said 'If I write that so-and-so has arrived, then such-and-such,' and he wrote falsely, he is freed, because it is the collection of letters and it has occurred. Unlike if he said 'If I write of his arrival,' then his actual arrival is necessary. So if he wrote of his arrival without knowing it and he had actually arrived, he is freed whether the news reached the swearer first or not, because the condition is fulfilled, as in al-Muhit. As for i'lam (informing), truthfulness is necessary, because i'lam is to establish knowledge, and falsehood does not establish it; so in al-Bada'i. And there is no difference between using 'bi' or not, as in al-Dhakhira. Harmful news is excluded from 'bishara' customarily, even if Allah calls it 'bishara' in His saying 'Give them good news of a painful torment' (3:21), because it is 'bishara' linguistically, but the discussion is about custom. In al-Muhit: If he said 'The first of my slaves to give me good news of the arrival of so-and-so is free,' and one slave sent another slave saying 'Tell the master that so-and-so says: so-and-so has arrived,' and the slave conveyed that to him, he said: the sender is freed, not the messenger, and it is like writing. If the messenger said 'So-and-so has arrived' and did not say 'So-and-so sent me to you with this,' then the messenger is freed, not the sender. His saying: (And if they gave him good news together, they are all freed) because it is realized from all. Allah said: 'So they gave him good news of a knowledgeable boy' (51:28). Similarly, his saying: ('So in good news there is no difference...') contradicts what he presented before this chapter in explaining the author's saying 'Do not speak to him' and he called him while he was sleeping. And likewise his saying ('As for i'lam...') contradicts what has passed, as we have noted. In Talkhis al-Jami' al-Kabir: If he said 'If you inform me that Zayd has arrived, then such-and-such,' he breaks his oath with false news, similarly if he wrote to me even if it did not arrive. And in 'basshartani' or 'a'lamtani', truthfulness and the swearer's ignorance are conditions, because the essential element in the first two (writing and informing) is the indication of the informer and the collection of letters, and in the latter two (good news and informing) is the imparting of joy and knowledge, unlike if he said 'of his arrival' because the preposition 'bi' of attachment necessitates existence, which is truthfulness. And he breaks his oath with gesture in 'a'lamtani' and with writing and messenger in all. His saying: ('So they gave him good news'...).
Source pages p. 575

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'i q continues the commentary on oaths in manumission, now focusing on the condition of giving good news (bishara). The matn states: 'Every slave who gives me good news of such-and-such is free; if three give news separately, the first is freed; if together, all are freed.' The commentary explains that 'bishara' is a joyful true report of something unknown. The first bearer qualifies because he first brought the news, and subsequent ones merely confirm it. The proof is the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud, where the Prophet recommended his recitation, and Abu Bakr raced to inform Ibn Mas'ud, so Ibn Mas'ud said 'Abu Bakr gave me good news (bassharani) and Umar informed me (akhbarani)'. This shows that the one who first brings joyful news is the 'mubashshir', while the other is just a 'mukhbir'. The text then expands to writing and messengers: a letter or messenger can fulfill the oath for bishara, as they are equivalent to face-to-face speech for good news. However, for ordinary news (khabar) without joy, only face-to-face speech breaks the oath. The crucial distinction is truthfulness: for bishara and i'lam (informing), truth is required, while for ikhbar (merely reporting), truth is not required because the word 'akhbara' applies to both true and false reports. The preposition 'bi' (bi-qudumihi) often indicates actual existence, requiring truth. Harmful news is not bishara customarily, even if linguistically called so. The passage concludes by reconciling apparent contradictions with earlier statements about the use of 'bi' and the distinctions between bishara, ikhbar, and i'lam. This is a dense, technical discussion typical of later Hanafi scholarship.

Key Terms
بشارة (bishāra): Good news that is true, joyful, and of something previously unknown to the recipient; it must be a report that brings happiness.
إعلام (i‘lām): Informing someone of something in order to impart knowledge; it requires truthfulness because falsehood cannot establish knowledge.
خبر (khabar): A report that may be true or false; in oaths, 'akhbara' can refer to both truthful and false reports.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'any slave who gives me good news of Zayd's arrival is free' and a slave writes a letter falsely stating Zayd has arrived, is he freed?
A) Yes, because writing is like speech for good news.
B) No, because good news must be true.
C) Yes, because the letter is a collection of letters.
D) No, because good news must be face-to-face.

Key Takeaway
Bishara, as defined by the Hanafi school, requires truth and joy and must be the first report (or together); false or subsequent reports do not trigger manumission.

Categories of Contracts in Oaths: Direct Action vs. Command
Classifying contracts to determine oath-breaking by command

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify contracts into three types and determine whether an oath is broken by the principal's direct action or by commanding an agent.

Core Idea
Oath-breaking by command depends on contract type: some attach rights to the agent, others to the principal.

When you swear not to do something, does commanding an agent break your oath? The answer depends on the nature of the contract. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq classifies contracts into three categories—those whose rights attach to the beneficiary, those that attach to the contracting party, and those with no rights—and derives the ruling for oath-breaking by command. The distinction hinges on whether the agent is a pure intermediary or a real contracting party.

Guided Path
1
Understand the threefold classification
Read the classification: (1) rights attach to the one for whom the contract is made (e.g., marriage), (2) rights attach to the contracting party (e.g., sale), (3) no rights attached (e.g., loan).
This is from Fatawa Qadikhan and is preferred over the twofold division.
2
Identify rights attachment
Determine who bears the rights and obligations of the contract: the contracting party (agent) or the commander (principal)?
For marriage, rights attach to the beneficiary, so the agent is a pure intermediary.
3
Apply the oath-breaking rule
If rights attach to the contracting party (e.g., sale), the oath is broken only by direct action, not by command. If rights attach to the commander (e.g., loan) or no rights, the oath is broken by command.
Marriage is an exception: it is broken by both direct action and command.
4
Consider special cases
Rulers (emirs, judges) who habitually delegate break the oath by command. Settlement (sulh) depends on acknowledgment vs. denial.
The passage refines absolute statements with these exceptions.

Chapter of Oaths in Sale, Purchase, Marriage, Fasting, Prayer, etc. What is broken by direct action not by command: sale, purchase, hire, leasing, settlement for property, division, contention, and beating a child. And what is broken by both: marriage, divorce, khul', and manumission. Chapter of Oaths in Sale, Purchase, Marriage, Fasting, Prayer, etc. Since oaths concerning these transactions are more frequent than those concerning prayer, fasting, hajj, etc., he preceded them. The gist is that every chapter its occurrence is less than what preceded it and more than what follows it. Know that contracts are of three types: some have their rights attached to the one for whom the contract is made, not to the contracting party, like marriage; some have their rights attached to the contracting party if he is qualified for rights to attach to him, like sale and purchase; and some contracts have no rights at all, like loan, release, payment in full, and recovery. This is in Fatawa Qadikhan. This is more appropriate than what is in al-Tabyin, Fath al-Qadir, etc., of dividing them into two types: a type whose rights attach to the contracting party, and a type whose rights do not attach to the commander. For that excludes contracts that have no rights at all. So for what has rights attached to the contracting party, the swearer does not break his oath by the direct action of his agent, because the action occurs from the agent in reality and in legal effect. And for what has rights attached to the commander and what has no rights at all, the swearer does break his oath by the action of his agent just as he breaks it by his direct action, because the agent in it is a pure intermediary. It has been established in al-Muhit that loan and similar are of the type whose rights attach to the commander. His saying: What is broken by direct action not by command: sale, purchase, hire, leasing, settlement for property, division, contention, and beating a child, because the contract occurs from the contracting party so that rights are upon him and for him. For this reason, if the contracting party is the swearer, he would break his oath by his oath, but the condition does not occur, which is the contract, from the commander. Rather, only the effect of the contract is established for him, unless he intends otherwise. The author gave it absolutely, and it is restricted to when the swearer personally conducts the contracts. But if the swearer has authority like an emir or judge who does not personally conduct the contract, then he does break the oath by command too, because he refrains from what he is accustomed to. If he sometimes personally conducts and sometimes delegates, the more frequent is considered, as in al-Muhit. He gave absolutely regarding settlement for property, and it is restricted to it being based on acknowledgment, because then it is a sale. As for settlement based on denial, it is a ransom for the oath regarding the defendant, so the agent on his behalf is a pure intermediary, so it falls under the second type, as we will explain in the Book of Agency. Based on this, if the plaintiff swears not to settle with so-and-so concerning this claim or this property, and then appoints an agent, he does not break his oath. But if the defendant swears and then appoints an agent: if it is based on acknowledgment, he breaks his oath; if it is based on denial or silence, he does not break his oath. Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq introduces the chapter on oaths related to transactions like sale, purchase, marriage, etc. The central theme is classifying contracts to determine whether an oath broken by directly performing the action also applies when the swearer commands his agent to do it. The author classifies contracts into three types: (1) those where rights attach to the person for whom the contract is made (e.g., marriage), (2) those where rights attach to the contracting party (e.g., sale), and (3) those with no rights at all (e.g., loan). For the first type, the swearer breaks his oath both by his own action and by commanding an agent, because the agent is a mere intermediary. For the second type, the swearer breaks his oath only by his own direct action, because the contract is attributed to the agent. For the third type, similar to the first, the swearer breaks his oath by command as well. The author notes that this threefold division is more precise than the twofold division in some earlier works. He then refines the rule for rulers like emirs who normally delegate; for them, commanding breaks the oath because that is their habitual way. Regarding settlement (sulh), a distinction is made: if it is a settlement based on acknowledgment, it is essentially a sale and follows the sale rule; if based on denial, it is a ransom and follows the marriage rule. The passage ends by applying these rules to specific cases of plaintiff and defendant swearing.

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): An oath; this chapter deals with oaths related to specific actions like buying, selling, etc.
وكيل (wakil): Agent appointed to perform an action on behalf of the principal.
الصلح عن إقرار (al-sulh 'an iqrar): Settlement based on acknowledgment (of a debt or right) considered a sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following contracts is broken by direct action only, not by command?
A) Marriage
B) Sale
C) Divorce
D) Manumission

Key Takeaway
Contracts are classified into three types based on rights attachment; the ruling for oath-breaking by command depends on this classification, with exceptions for rulers and settlement types.

Classification of Contracts for Oaths: Three Types
Defining three contract types for oath-breaking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any contract into one of three categories (beneficiary-rights, contractor-rights, no rights) and determine whether an oath not to perform the act is broken by an agent's action.

Core Idea
Contracts divide into three types for oaths: rights on beneficiary, rights on contractor, no rights — only the first and third cause oath-breaking by agent.

In Hanafi law, oaths concerning transactions are common and require precise rules. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a classification of contracts that determines whether an oath not to perform a certain act is broken when an agent performs it instead. This classification, based on where the rights of the contract attach, is more comprehensive than earlier twofold divisions and covers all contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract
Determine the specific contract in question (e.g., sale, marriage, loan, divorce).
The contract must be valid and relevant to an oath.
2
Determine where rights attach
Classify the contract into one of three types: (1) rights attach to the beneficiary (e.g., marriage), (2) rights attach to the contractor (e.g., sale), or (3) no rights attach (e.g., loan).
Rights refer to the primary legal obligations or benefits of the contract.
3
Apply the oath-breaking rule
If rights attach to the contractor, the swearer does NOT break his oath by the agent's act. If rights attach to the beneficiary or there are no rights, the swearer DOES break his oath by the agent's act.
The agent is considered the real actor in contractor-rights contracts; a mere messenger otherwise.

Source Text
