باب اليمين في البيع والشراء والتزويج والصوم والصلاة وغيرها
مال ما يحنث بالمباشرة لا بالأمر البيع والشراء والإجارة والاستئجار والصلح عن والقسمة والخصومة وضرب الولد وما يحنث بهما النكاح والطلاق والخلع والعتق
لما كانت الأيمان على هذه التصرفات أكثر منها على الصلاة والصوم والحج وما بعدها قدمها عليها. والحاصل أن كل باب فوقوعه أقل مما قبله وأكثر مما بعده. واعلم أن العقود أنواع ثلاثة : منها ما يتعلق حقوقه بمن وقع له العقد لا بالعاقد كالنكاح، ومنها ما يتعلق حقوقه بالعاقد إذا كان العاقد أهلاً لتعلق الحقوق به كالبيع والشراء، ومن العقود ما لا حقوق له أصلاً كالإعارة والإبراء والقضاء والاقتضاء؛ كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان. وهذا أولى مما في التبيين وفتح القدير وغيرهما من تقسيمها إلى نوعين : نوع تتعلق حقوقه بالعاقد، ونوع لا تتعلق حقوقه بالآمر . فإنه يخرج عنها ما ليس له حقوق أصلاً فما تتعلق حقوقه بالعاقد فإن الحالف لا يحنث بمباشرة وكيله لوجود الفعل من الوكيل حقيقة وحكماً، وما تتعلق حقوقه بالآمر وما لا حقوق له أصلاً فإنه يحنث الحالف أن لا يفعله بفعل وكيله كما يحنث بمباشرته لأن الوكيل فيه سفير ومعبر، وقد جعل في المحيط العارية ونحوها مما تتعلق حقوقها بالأمر.
Book of Oaths, Chapter of Oaths concerning Sale, Purchase, Marriage, Fasting, Prayer and others. Something: what breaks an oath by direct action but not by command: sale, purchase, hire, lease, settlement for money, division, litigation, beating the child; and what breaks by both: marriage, divorce, khul', emancipation. Since oaths concerning these transactions are more numerous than those concerning prayer, fasting, hajj, etc., they were placed before them. The summary is that each chapter's occurrence is less than the previous and more than the next. Know that contracts are three types: those whose rights attach to the one for whom the contract is made, not to the contractor, like marriage; those whose rights attach to the contractor if he is qualified for rights to attach, like sale and purchase; and contracts that have no rights at all, like loan, release, settlement, and payment; as in Qadikhan's Fatawa. This is more appropriate than what is in al-Tibyan, Fath al-Qadir, and others, their division into two types: a type whose rights attach to the contractor, and a type whose rights do not attach to the principal. Then what has no rights at all is excluded. So for those whose rights attach to the contractor, the swearer does not break his oath by the direct action of his agent, due to the action occurring from the agent in reality and legally. For those whose rights attach to the principal and those with no rights at all, the swearer breaks his oath if he swore not to do it and his agent does it, just as he would by his own direct action, because the agent is a messenger and carrier. In al-Muhit, loan and similar are placed among those whose rights attach to the principal.
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on oaths concerning transactions. The author first states the matn: certain acts break an oath only if done directly (sale, purchase, etc.), while others break it whether done directly or by an agent (marriage, divorce, etc.). The commentary then explains why this chapter precedes others: such oaths are more common. The key contribution is the classification of contracts into three categories based on whose rights attach: first, contracts where rights attach to the beneficiary (e.g., marriage); second, where rights attach to the contractor (e.g., sale); third, contracts with no rights (e.g., loan). This threefold division is preferred over the twofold division found in some earlier Hanafi works, which only distinguish based on whether rights attach to the contractor or the principal, thereby excluding no-rights contracts. The rule: for the second category (rights on contractor), the swearer is not liable if his agent acts because the agent is the real actor. For the first and third, the swearer is liable because the agent is merely a vehicle. The reference to al-Muhit places loan in the third category but elsewhere it is considered as rights attaching to the principal, showing some nuance.

Key Terms
الحالف (al-haalif): The person who takes an oath (swears not to do something).
الوكيل (al-wakeel): An agent appointed by the swearer to act on his behalf.
العقد (al-'aqd): A legal contract, such as sale, marriage, loan, etc.
الحقوق (al-huqooq): Legal rights or obligations that arise from a contract and attach to a person (either the beneficiary or the contractor).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the three-type classification, under which category does a contract of loan (عارية) fall?
A) Rights attach to the beneficiary (like marriage)
B) Rights attach to the contractor (like sale)
C) Contracts with no rights at all
D) It is not included in any category

Key Takeaway
The threefold classification of contracts for oath-breaking provides a complete and precise rule for determining liability when an agent acts.

Refining the General Oath Rule: Ruler Exception and Settlement Distinction
Explaining exceptions for rulers and settlement types in oath rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the ruler exception and distinguish between settlement based on acknowledgment vs. denial when determining oath-breaking by commanding an agent.

Core Idea
Rulers break oath by command; settlement on denial is a ransom — both refine the rule that oath is not broken by agent.

The general rule states that an oath not to do a contract is not broken by commanding an agent. However, the Hanafi madhhab introduces critical refinements: a ruler who customarily does not handle contracts himself breaks his oath by commanding. Similarly, settlement contracts are not all alike — a settlement based on denial is a ransom for the oath, making the agent a pure messenger, which affects whether the principal's oath is broken. This lesson dissects these refinements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Recall the general rule that swearing not to perform a contract (e.g. sale, hire) is not broken by commanding an agent. This applies when the rights of the contract attach to the contractor.
The matn originally states this without qualification.
2
Apply the ruler exception
If the swearer is a ruler (amir, judge, etc.) who does not personally handle contracts, then commanding an agent DOES break the oath. This is because the ruler's custom is to act through agents, so commanding is equivalent to doing.
If the ruler sometimes does it himself and sometimes delegates, the majority practice determines.
3
Classify the settlement
Settlement (sulh) can be based on acknowledgment (iqrar) or denial (inkar). Acknowledgment-based settlement is a sale; denial-based settlement is a ransom for the oath.
This classification is crucial for the oath ruling.
4
Determine oath-breaking for plaintiff
If the plaintiff swears not to settle and appoints an agent, he does not break his oath, regardless of settlement type, because the plaintiff's role is not affected by the type.
The agent acts on behalf of the defendant typically.
5
Determine oath-breaking for defendant
If the defendant swears not to settle and appoints an agent, the outcome depends on the settlement type: if based on acknowledgment (iqrar), the oath is broken (it is a sale); if based on denial (inkar) or silence, the oath is not broken (it is a ransom).
The agent in case of denial is a pure messenger.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ما يحنث بالمباشرة لا بالآمر البيع والشراء والإجارة والاستئجار والصلح عن والقسمة والخصومة وضرب (الولد لأن العقد وجد من العاقد حتى كانت الحقوق عليه ولهذا لو كان العاقد هو ما الحالف يحنث في يمينه فلم يوجد هو الشرط وهو العقد من الأمر وإنما الثابت له حكم العقد إلا أن ينوي غير ذلك أطلقه المصنف وهو مقيد بما إذا كان الحالف يتولى العقود بنفسه، أما إذا كان الحالف ذا سلطان كالأمير والقاضي ونحوهما لا يتولى العقد بنفسه فإنه يحنث بالأمر أيضاً لأنه يمنع نفسه عما يعتاده ، فإن كان الأمر يباشره مرة ويفوض أخرى يعتبر الأغلب كما في المحيط. وأطلق في الصلح عن مال وهو مقيد بأن يكون عن الإقرار لأنه حينئذ بيع أما الصلح عن إنكار فهو فداء لليمين في حق المدعى عليه فيكون الوكيل من جانبه سفيراً محضاً فكان من القسم الثاني كما سنبينه في كتاب الوكالة. فعلى هذا إذا حلف المدعي أن لا يصالح فلاناً عن هذه الدعوى أو عن هذا المال فوكل فيه لا يحنث أ، وإذا حلف المدعى عليه ثم وكل به فإن كان عن إقرار حنث، وإن كان عن انكار أو سكوت لا يحنث كذا في عدة من النسخ التي بقلا سكوت لا يحنث وقيد بالصلح عن المال احترازاً عما صرح به في القسم الثاني من الصلح عن دم العمد، وفي المحيط : لو حلف لا يصالح رجلاً في حق يدعيه عليه فوكل رجلاً فصالحه لم يحنث ولو قال والله لا أصالح فلاناً فأمر غيره فصالحه حنث في القضاء لأن الصلح لا عهدة فيه .اهـ. ولعل المراد بالفرع الثاني الصلح اللغوي بمعنى عدم العدواة والغيظ لا بمعنى أنه عقد برفع النزاع الذي هو الصلح الفقهي.
His saying: 'What breaks an oath by direct action but not by command: sale, purchase, hire, lease, settlement for money, division, litigation, beating the child' because the contract occurred from the contractor such that the rights were upon him, and if the contractor were the one the swearer doesn't break his oath, the condition is not met and the contract is from the principal, but the principal only has the effect of the contract unless he intends otherwise. The author made it absolute, but it is restricted to when the swearer handles contracts himself. However, if the swearer is a ruler like an amir or judge and the like, who does not handle contracts himself, then he breaks his oath by command as well because he prohibits himself from what he is accustomed to; if he sometimes does it himself and sometimes delegates, the majority is considered, as in al-Muhit. And he made absolute in the settlement for money, but it is restricted to when it is based on acknowledgment because then it is a sale. But settlement based on denial is a ransom for the oath regarding the defendant, so the agent from his side is a pure messenger, and it falls into the second category as we will explain in the Book of Agency. Thus, if the plaintiff swears not to settle with someone regarding this claim or this money and appoints an agent, he does not break his oath. And if the defendant swears and then appoints an agent, then if it is based on acknowledgment, he breaks his oath; if based on denial or silence, he does not break his oath, as in several copies... silence does not break. And it is restricted to settlement for money as a precaution from what is stated in the second category concerning settlement for intentional blood. In al-Muhit: if he swears not to settle with a man regarding a right he claims on him and appoints an agent who settles, he does not break his oath. But if he says 'By Allah, I will not settle with so-and-so' and commands another to settle, he breaks his oath judicially because settlement has no liability. Perhaps the meaning in the second branch is the linguistic settlement meaning cessation of enmity and anger, not the technical settlement which is a contract to remove conflict.
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This lesson continues the commentary on the matn phrase about what breaks an oath by direct action only. The commentator clarifies that the general rule applies only when the swearer personally conducts his own contracts. For a ruler (sultan, judge, etc.) who customarily does not handle contracts himself, commanding an agent breaks the oath because the ruler is preventing himself from his usual practice. If the ruler sometimes does it himself and sometimes delegates, the majority practice determines. Next, the commentator discusses settlement (sulh). The matn's 'settlement for money' is absolute but qualified: if based on acknowledgment (iqrar), it is a sale and thus falls under contracts where rights attach to the contractor (so oath not broken by agent). If based on denial (inkar), it is a ransom for the oath regarding the defendant, making the agent a pure messenger, so it falls under the second type (where oath is broken by agent). A practical result: if the plaintiff swears not to settle and appoints an agent, he does not break his oath (since it's based on denial from the defendant). If the defendant swears, then: if settlement acknowledges debt, oath broken; if denial or silence, not broken. The margin adds that this is for money settlements; intentional blood settlement is different. Finally, al-Muhit gives a nuance: swearing 'not to settle' (linguistic) vs. 'not to make a sulh contract' affects whether the oath is broken by commanding an agent.

Key Terms
الصلح (al-sulh): A settlement contract; can be based on acknowledgment (sale) or denial (ransom for oath).
ذا سلطان (dhu sultan): A person with authority like an amir or judge who does not personally handle transactions.
المدعي (al-mudda'i): The plaintiff who claims a right against the defendant.
المدعى عليه (al-mudda'a 'alayhi): The defendant against whom a claim is made.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a defendant swears not to settle and then appoints an agent to settle based on denial of the claim, does the defendant break his oath?
A) Yes, because settlement is a contract
B) No, because settlement based on denial is a ransom, making the agent a pure messenger
C) Yes, because the defendant's oath prohibits settlement
D) No, because any settlement by an agent does not break the oath

Key Takeaway
Rulers break oath by commanding due to custom; settlement on denial is a ransom — both are critical refinements to the general rule that oath is not broken by agent.

Court of Custom: How the Hanafi Imams Differ on Oaths over Gold, Silver, and Iron
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath interpretation with custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of 'urf (custom) to determine whether an oath over a general category (e.g., gold, iron) is broken by specific items, and explain the difference between Abu Yusuf's literal approach and Muhammad's custom-based approach.

Core Idea
In oaths, custom can override literal meaning: Muhammad excludes coins from 'gold/silver' because people treat them as currency.

This lesson explores a classic intra-Hanafi dispute on oath interpretation. When someone swears not to buy 'gold' or 'silver', does buying a coin count? Abu Yusuf says yes, literally; Muhammad says no, because custom treats coins as currency, not as raw gold/silver. The same principle applies to iron, brass, and other materials. Understanding this dispute clarifies how custom ('urf) functions as a source of law in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
State the general Hanafi principle for interpreting oaths
Hanafis interpret oaths by the common usage ('urf) of the swearer, not necessarily the literal linguistic meaning. This is the foundation for the dispute.
The principle is derived from the maxim: 'Al-ummur bi maqasidiha' – but here custom defines the scope.
2
Identify the specific case: oath not to buy gold/silver
The text from al-Badai' presents an oath not to buy gold or silver. The question is whether buying coins (dirhams/dinars), vessels, bullion, or jewelry breaks the oath.
All items are physically gold/silver, but custom may treat coins differently.
3
Compare the two views: Abu Yusuf vs Muhammad
Abu Yusuf holds that any gold/silver item breaks the oath. Muhammad excludes coins (dirhams/dinars) due to custom – they are not referred to as 'gold/silver' in trade.
This is the core ikhtilaf: literal vs custom.
4
Apply the same reasoning to other examples (iron, brass, etc.)
For iron, Abu Yusuf includes all manufactured iron; Muhammad requires that the item be commonly called 'iron' (e.g., a needle is not called 'iron'). For brass, both agree that common brass items break the oath. For wool and meat: buying an animal does not break an oath against buying wool/meat because the desired item is not the primary object.
Consistency: Muhammad's custom-based view restricts scope; Abu Yusuf's literal view expands it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الواقعات : حلف لا يشتري من فلان فأسلم إليه في ثوب حنث لأنه اشترى مؤجلاً. حلف لا يشتري عبد فلان فآجر به داره لا يحنث لأنه ليس بشراء ألا ترى أنه لا شفعة فيها مع أن الشفعة تثبت في الشراء. حلفه السلطان أن لا يشتري طعاماً للبيع ثم اشترى طعاماً لبيته ثم بداله فباعه لا يحنث لأنه ما اشترى للبيع، وهذا كمن حلف لا تخرج امرأته إلى بيت والدتها فخرجت للمسجد ثم زارت والدتها لا يحنث. حلف لا يشتري ثوباً جديداً فتفسير الجديد ما لا ينكسر حتى يصير شبه الخلق ويجب أن يكون جديداً قبل الغسل وبعده لا لاعتبار العرف. حلف لا يشتري فاشترى أرضاً فيها مبقلة قد نبتت وشرط ذلك معها حنث . وكذلك لو حلف لا يشتري رطباً فاشترى نخلاً بها رطب وشرط ذلك حنث لأنه لو لم يشترط لا يدخل في البيع فإذا شرطه حتى دخل يكون له حصة من الثمن فصار مشترياً له. حلف أن لا يبيع داره فأعطاها امرأته في صداقها حنث، كذا ذكر هنا ويجب أن يكون الجواب على التفصيل إن تزوجها على الدار لا يحنث لأن هذا ليس ببيع، وإن تزوجها على الدراهم ثم أعطاها عوضاً عن تلك الدراهم بيع اهـ. وفي البدائع : حلف لا يشتري ذهباً ولا فضة فاشترى من دراهم أو دنانير أو آنية أو تبراً أو مصوغ حلية أو غير ذلك مما هو ذهب أو فضة فإنه يحنث في قول أبي يوسف . وقال محمد: لا يحنث في الدراهم والدنانير للعرف ولو حلف لا يشتري حديداً فهو على مضروبه وإبره سلاحاً كان أو غير سلاح في قول أبي يوسف. وقال محمد: إن اشترى شيئاً من الحديد يسمى يحنث وإلا فلا، وبائع الإبر لا يسمى حلف لا يشتري صفراً فاشترى طست صفر أو كوزاً أو توراً حنث وكذلك عند محمد. وقال محمد: لو اشترى فلوساً لا يحنث ولو حلف لا يشتري صوفاً فاشترى شاة على ظهرها صوف لم يحنث، وكذا لو حلف لا يشتري لحماً فاشترى شاة حية لم يحنث. ولو حلف لا دهناً فهو على دهن جرت العادة بالأدهان ،به ولو حلف لا يشتري بنفسجاً أو لا حنث لأن هذا مشترياً بائعه حداداً.
And in al-Waqiat: He swore not to buy from so-and-so, then gave salam for a garment: he breaks his oath because it is a deferred purchase. He swore not to buy the slave of so-and-so, then leased his house: he does not break his oath because it is not a purchase; do you not see that there is no preemption in it while preemption is established in purchase? The sultan made him swear not to buy food for sale, then he bought food for his household, then changed his mind and sold it: he does not break his oath because he did not buy for sale. This is like a man who swore that his wife would not go to her mother's house, then she went to the mosque and then visited her mother: he does not break his oath. He swore not to buy a new garment; the interpretation of 'new' is that which is not torn until it becomes like old, and it must be new before washing and after, not by custom. He swore not to buy, then bought land with a vegetable plot that had grown and stipulated that: he breaks his oath. Similarly, if he swore not to buy fresh dates and bought a palm tree with fresh dates and stipulated that, he breaks his oath, because if he had not stipulated, it would not be included in the sale; when he stipulates so that it enters, it has a share of the price, so he becomes a buyer of it. He swore not to sell his house, then gave it to his wife as her dower: he breaks his oath; it is mentioned here, but the answer should be detailed: if he married her on the house, he does not break his oath because this is not a sale; if he married her on dirhams then gave it as compensation for those dirhams, it is a sale, he breaks. In al-Badai': He swore not to buy gold or silver, then bought dirhams, dinars, vessels, bullion, jewelry, or anything that is gold or silver: he breaks in the view of Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: he does not break for dirhams and dinars due to custom. If he swore not to buy iron, it applies to its manufactured products and needles, weapon or not, in Abu Yusuf's view. Muhammad said: if he bought something of iron that is called iron, he breaks, otherwise no; a needle seller is not called a blacksmith. If he swore not to buy brass, then bought a brass basin, jug, or kettle: he breaks, and similarly with Muhammad. Muhammad said: if he buys coins (fulus), he does not break. If he swore not to buy wool, then bought a sheep with wool on its back, he does not break. Similarly, if he swore not to buy meat, then bought a live sheep, he does not break. If he swore against oil, it applies to oil that is customary. If he swore not to buy violets or...? he breaks because this is ...
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This lesson compiles practical examples from al-Waqiat and al-Badai' on how specific oaths are evaluated, highlighting differences between Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani. The examples cover: buying through salam (deferred sale) breaks an oath not to buy; leasing does not equal buying. A ruler's oath regarding buying food for sale is interpreted by intent. Defining 'new' garment: it is based on physical condition, not custom. Buying land with produce that is stipulated as part of the sale makes one a buyer of the produce. Giving a house as dower: if it is the subject of marriage, it's not a sale; if given in lieu of stipulated dower, it is a sale. Key ikhtilaf: Abu Yusuf holds that any gold/silver item including coins breaks an oath not to buy gold/silver, while Muhammad says coins are excluded due to common usage (they are not considered 'gold/silver' in custom). Similarly for iron: Abu Yusuf includes all manufactured iron; Muhammad requires that the item be commonly referred to as 'iron'. For brass, both agree that common brass items break the oath. For coins (fulus), Muhammad exempts them. For wool and meat: buying the animal itself does not break an oath against buying wool or meat because the wool/meat is not the primary object. The lesson demonstrates that custom (urf) plays a significant role in determining the scope of oath items, and the imams differ on its application.

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): Custom or common usage that determines the meaning of terms in oaths.
الدراهم والدنانير (al-darahim wa al-dananir): Silver and gold coins used as currency.
الحديد (al-hadid): Iron; in oaths, it may or may not include manufactured items like needles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, if a person swears not to buy iron and then buys a needle, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because a needle is made of iron
B) No, because a needle seller is not called a 'blacksmith' and the needle is not commonly referred to as 'iron'
C) Yes, because all iron products are included
D) No, because buying a needle is not a purchase

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recognizes custom ('urf) as a key factor in interpreting oaths, leading to nuanced differences among the imams.

Oath Regarding Settlement (Sulh) – Deputy's Action
Ruling on deputy's settlement breaking an oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between legal and linguistic sulh to determine whether an oath is broken when a deputy settles.

Core Idea
An oath about 'sulh' is broken by a deputy only if the oath refers to legal sulh (a contract); if it refers to linguistic reconciliation, the deputy's action does not break it.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on oaths concerning settlement. The key insight is that the word 'sulh' can carry two meanings: a legal contract to end a dispute, or a general state of reconciliation. The Hanafi school uses this distinction to determine whether an oath is broken when a deputy acts on behalf of the oath-taker.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath formulation
Determine whether the oath is personal ('I will not settle myself') or general ('I will not settle with X'). This affects the ruling.
Personal oath limits the action to the oath-taker.
2
Determine the type of sulh intended
Decide whether the oath refers to legal sulh (a contract to end dispute) or linguistic sulh (absence of enmity). The text in al-Muhit treats the second branch as linguistic.
The commentator suggests that the second case (general oath) refers to linguistic meaning.
3
Apply the rule for deputy's action
If the oath is general and the sulh is legal, the deputy's settlement breaks the oath judicially (in qada). If the oath is personal (e.g., 'I myself will not settle'), the deputy's action does not break it.
Sulh as a contract is delegable, so the deputy's act is attributed to the principal.
4
Distinguish judicial vs. religious breaking
In the general/legal case, the oath is broken judicially but not necessarily religiously (diyaanatan) because the intention may be reconciliation, not contract.
The thamarah al-khilaf: practical consequence for kaffarah vs. legal consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سكوت لا يحنث وقيد بالصلح عن المال احترازاً عما صرح به في القسم الثاني من الصلح عن دم العمد، وفي المحيط : لو حلف لا يصالح رجلاً في حق يدعيه عليه فوكل رجلاً فصالحه لم يحنث ولو قال والله لا أصالح فلاناً فأمر غيره فصالحه حنث في القضاء لأن الصلح لا عهدة فيه .اهـ. ولعل المراد بالفرع الثاني الصلح اللغوي بمعنى عدم العدواة والغيظ لا بمعنى أنه عقد برفع النزاع الذي هو الصلح الفقهي.
Silence does not cause him to break the oath. And it is restricted to financial settlement as a precaution against what was explicitly stated in the second section regarding settlement for premeditated blood. In al-Muhit: If he swore not to settle with a man regarding a claim he has against him, and he appointed a man as agent and he settled, he does not break the oath. And if he said, 'By Allah, I will not settle with so-and-so,' and he ordered someone else and he settled, he breaks the oath in judicial rulings because settlement has no right of recourse. End. Perhaps the meaning of the second branch is linguistic settlement in the sense of absence of enmity and anger, not in the sense of a contract that removes a dispute, which is the juristic settlement.
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses oaths related to settlement (sulh). The author first notes that silence does not break an oath about settlement. Then he explains that a settlement concerning property (mal) is different from one about intentional homicide (dam al-amd), where the ruling differs. From al-Muhit, we learn that if a man swears not to settle a claim directly and appoints an agent, the agent's settlement does not break the oath because the principal's intention was personal. However, if he swears generally 'I will not settle with so-and-so' and orders another to settle for him, he breaks the oath judicially, even if not in his conscience, because settlement (in the legal sense) is a contract that can be delegated and the oath was absolute. The commentator suggests that the second case refers to the linguistic meaning of reconciliation (ending hostility) rather than the legal contract of sulh. This distinction affects whether the oath is considered broken.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A contract to end a dispute; here distinguished between legal (رفع نزاع) and linguistic (دفع عداوة).
الصلح اللغوي (al-sulh al-lughawi): Linguistic settlement: reconciliation or absence of enmity, not a legal contract.
الصلح الفقهي (al-sulh al-fiqhi): Juristic settlement: a binding contract to end a dispute.
عهدة (uhdah): Right of recourse or liability; here, 'لا عهدة فيه' means settlement has no right of recourse, i.e., it is final.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case from al-Muhit, what is the ruling if a man swears 'I will not settle with so-and-so' and then orders someone else to settle on his behalf?
A) He does not break the oath because settlement is a personal act.
B) He breaks the oath judicially because settlement can be delegated.
C) He breaks the oath only if the settlement is financial.
D) He breaks the oath only if he is present.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, whether an oath about sulh is broken by a deputy depends on the wording (personal vs. general) and the meaning of sulh (legal contract vs. reconciliation).

Interpreting Oaths by Custom: The Dispute of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oaths and custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that oaths are interpreted according to custom (urf) to determine whether an oath is broken, and distinguish the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Core Idea
An oath on a substance is broken only by buying what is customarily called by that name.

The Hanafi school emphasizes custom (urf) in interpreting oaths. This passage from al-Bada'i' presents the ijtihad of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on various examples, illustrating how custom can override literal meaning. Understanding this helps in issuing fatwas on oaths in daily transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the item in the oath
Look at the word used in the oath, e.g., gold, iron, brass, wool, meat, oil, violet, henna, rose, or flaxseed.
The word may be ambiguous or have multiple meanings.
2
Determine the literal meaning
Consider the literal, dictionary definition of the term. For example, 'gold' literally covers all gold items.
Abu Yusuf stops here.
3
Consider customary usage
Examine how people commonly use the word in everyday speech and commercial transactions. Does 'gold' commonly refer to coins and jewelry, or is it restricted?
Muhammad considers custom (urf) as a qualifier.
4
Apply the two positions
Abu Yusuf holds the literal meaning, so buying any form breaks the oath. Muhammad holds the customary meaning, so only what is commonly called by that name breaks the oath.
Note exceptions: e.g., dirhams are not called 'gold' customarily.
5
Ruling based on custom
Apply the prevalent custom in the locale. If the item is commonly referred to by the oath word, the oath is broken; otherwise not.
The mufta bihi is Muhammad's view, followed by later Hanafi scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي البدائع : حلف لا يشتري ذهباً ولا فضة فاشترى من دراهم أو دنانير أو آنية أو تبراً أو مصوغ حلية أو غير ذلك مما هو ذهب أو فضة فإنه يحنث في قول أبي يوسف . وقال محمد: لا يحنث في الدراهم والدنانير للعرف ولو حلف لا يشتري حديداً فهو على مضروبه وإبره سلاحاً كان أو غير سلاح في قول أبي يوسف. وقال محمد: إن اشترى شيئاً من الحديد يسمى يحنث وإلا فلا، وبائع الإبر لا يسمى حداداً. ولو حلف لا يشتري صفراً فاشترى طست صفر أو كوزاً أو توراً حنث وكذلك عند محمد. وقال محمد: لو اشترى فلوساً لا يحنث ولو حلف لا يشتري صوفاً فاشترى شاة على ظهرها صوف لم يحنث، وكذا لو حلف لا يشتري لحماً فاشترى شاة حية لم يحنث. ولو حلف لا دهناً فهو على دهن جرت العادة بالأدهان به ولو حلف لا يشتري بنفسجاً أو لا يشمه فهو على الدهن والورق، وأما الحناء والورد فهو على الورق دون الدهن. ولو حلف لا يشتري بزراً فاشترى دهن بزر حنث، وإن اشترى حباً لم يحنث اهـ.
And in al-Bada'i: If he swore not to buy gold or silver, and then bought dirhams or dinars or vessels or bullion or jewelry or other things that are gold or silver, then he breaks the oath according to Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: He does not break the oath regarding dirhams and dinars due to custom. And if he swore not to buy iron, it applies to its wrought forms and needles, whether it is a weapon or not, according to Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: If he bought something of iron that is called [by that name], he breaks the oath, otherwise not; and the seller of needles is not called a blacksmith. And if he swore not to buy brass (sufr) and bought a brass basin or cup or trough, he breaks the oath, and likewise according to Muhammad. And Muhammad said: If he bought copper coins (fulus) he does not break the oath. And if he swore not to buy wool and bought a sheep with wool on its back, he does not break the oath. Similarly, if he swore not to buy meat and bought a live sheep, he does not break the oath. And if he swore not to buy oil, it applies to oil commonly used for oils. And if he swore not to buy or smell violet, it applies to the oil and the leaves. As for henna and rose, it applies to the leaves without the oil. And if he swore not to buy flaxseed and bought flaxseed oil, he breaks the oath; if he bought the seeds, he does not break. End.
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage from al-Bada'i' (by al-Kasani) records a disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding oaths on buying specific items. The general principle is that the interpretation of the oath follows the custom (urf) of the people. Abu Yusuf tends to hold that the literal meaning of the term applies to all forms of that substance, while Muhammad gives weight to the common usage. For example, if someone swears not to buy gold, Abu Yusuf says buying any form of gold (coins, vessels, bullion) breaks the oath. Muhammad makes an exception for dirhams and dinars because customarily they are not simply called 'gold' but are known as currency. Similarly, for iron: Abu Yusuf applies the oath to all wrought iron, including needles; Muhammad restricts it to items commonly called 'iron' (e.g., a blacksmith's products), so a needle seller is not called a blacksmith. For brass (sufr), both agree that buying items like a basin breaks the oath, but Muhammad excludes copper coins (fulus) because they are not called sufr. For wool, meat, and oil, the ruling depends on whether the item is commonly referred to by that name (e.g., a sheep is not called wool, a live sheep is not called meat, and 'oil' means the liquid, not the raw material). The passage also covers floral oils: violet oil includes both the oil and the leaves, while henna and rose only refer to the leaves because the oil is not primarily called by the flower's name. Finally, for flaxseed, buying oil breaks the oath because it is derived from the seed, but buying the seeds themselves does not. Note that the relied upon position (mufta bihi) is often that of Muhammad, following custom.

Key Terms
العرف (al-urf): Customary usage among people that determines the meaning of words in oaths.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Muhammad, if a man swears not to buy 'iron' and then buys a needle, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because a needle is made of iron.
B) No, because a needle is not commonly called 'iron'.
C) Yes, but only if the needle is a weapon.
D) No, because the oath was about buying, not selling.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, oaths are interpreted according to customary usage (urf), not just literal definitions, and the preferred view is that of Muhammad which follows custom.

Oath on Purchase: Direct vs. Indirect Acquisition
Ruling on oath broken by wife's purchase of water and by buying an incomplete garment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a direct purchase that breaks an oath and an indirect acquisition that does not, applying the Hanafi principle of actual contracting.

Core Idea
An oath on purchase is broken only by a direct sale; indirect acquisition like hiring a carrier is not a purchase.

This lesson examines a case from al-Zahiriyyah where a husband swears an oath conditioned on his wife's purchase. The ruling depends on whether she actually buys the item herself or merely hires someone to fetch it for her. We will see how the Hanafi school defines 'purchase' in the context of oaths, and how that definition affects the validity of the oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath condition
Read the husband's statement: 'If you buy something, you are divorced.' Note that the condition is tied to the wife's act of purchasing.
The condition is clear: the trigger is her purchase.
2
Analyze the two scenarios
Compare the first scenario (she buys water in a water skin) with the second (she gives the jar and bread to a water carrier). Determine who is the buyer in each case.
In the first, she directly acquires ownership; in the second, she hires a service.
3
Apply the Hanafi definition of purchase
Purchase (shira') in Hanafi fiqh requires a contract of sale (bay') with offer and acceptance, transferring ownership. Hiring (ijarah) is not a sale.
Hence, giving bread to the carrier is ijarah, not a purchase.
4
Derive the ruling for each scenario
If she buys directly, the oath is broken (talaq occurs). If she hires, no purchase occurs, so the oath is not broken.
The text explicitly states the outcomes.
5
Consider the shirt case analogously
Similarly, if someone swears not to buy a shirt, and buys an unsewn cloth, it is not a purchase of a complete shirt, so the oath is not broken.
The ruling depends on the common understanding of the item named in the oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية : ولو قال لامرأته إن اشتريت شيئاً فأنت طالق فاشترت الماء قالوا: إن اشترته في قربة أو جرة طلقت وإن دفعت الجرة إلى السقاء وخبزاً حتى يحمل لها الماء لا تطلق. ولو باع عبده من رجل وسلم إلى المشتري ثم حلف البائع أن لا يشتريه من فلان ثم إن المشتري أقال البيع وقبل البائع الإقالة لا يحنث، ولو كان الثمن ألف درهم فوقعت الإقالة بمائة دينار أو بأكثر من الثمن الأول أو أقل حنث. قيل : هذا قولهما. وأما على قول أبي حنيفة لا يحنث لكونه إقالة على كل حال على ما عرف ولو حلف وقال والله ما اشتريت اليوم شيئاً وقد كان اشترى في ذلك اليوم أشياء لكن بالتعاطي فقد قيل يحنث في يمينه. وفي مجموع النوازل: وضع المسألة في طرف المبيع فقال إذا حلف لا الخبز فجاء رجل فأعطاه يبيع دراهم لأجل الخبز ودفع هو إليه الخبز لا يحنث وذكر في شهادات القدوري ما يؤيد ما ذكر في مجموع النوازل فقال : لا يسع لمن عاين ذلك أن يشهد على البيع بل يشهد على التعاطي. وإلى هذا مال الماتريدي. ولو حلف لا يشتري قميصاً فاشترى قميصاً مقطعاً غير مخيط لا يحنث. ولو قال إن بعث غلامي هذا أحدا من الناس فامرأته كذا فباعه من رجلين حنث، وكذا إذا قال إن أكل هذا الرغيف أحد فأكله اثنان حنث في يمينه.
In al-Zahiriyyah: If a man said to his wife, 'If you buy something, you are divorced,' and she bought water—they said: If she bought it in a water skin or a jar, she is divorced; but if she gave the jar to the water carrier and bread so that he carries water for her, she is not divorced. And if a man sold his slave to a man and handed him over to the buyer, then the seller swore that he would not buy him from so-and-so, and then the buyer cancelled the sale (iqalah) and the seller accepted, he does not break his oath. But if the price was one thousand dirhams and the cancellation occurred for one hundred dinars, or more or less than the original price, he breaks the oath. It was said: This is their saying (the two disciples). As for the saying of Abu Hanifa, he does not break the oath because it is a cancellation in any case, as is known. And if he swore and said, 'By Allah, I did not buy anything today,' and he had bought things that day but through mutual transaction (ta'ati), it was said that he breaks his oath. In Majmu' al-Nawazil: The question was placed on the side of the sold item: if he swore not to buy bread, and a man came and gave him dirhams for the bread and he gave him the bread, he does not break his oath; and it is mentioned in Shihadat al-Quduri what supports this: 'It is not permissible for the one who witnesses that to testify on the sale, but rather he testifies on the transaction (ta'ati).' Al-Maturidi agreed with this. And if he swore not to buy a shirt and bought a cut, unsewn shirt, he does not break his oath. And if he said, 'If my slave sells to anyone among the people, then my wife is such,' and he sold to two men, he breaks his oath. Similarly, if he said, 'If anyone eats this loaf,' and two ate it, he breaks his oath.
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage collects several rulings from al-Zahiriyyah and other works on when an oath is broken. The first case: a man tells his wife 'If you buy something, you are divorced.' If she buys water in a container (skin or jar), she is divorced because she has directly purchased the water. But if she hires a water carrier by giving him a jar and bread to bring water, she has not bought the water herself, so she is not divorced. The distinction is based on who actually makes the purchase. Second case: a man sells a slave, then swears not to buy that slave from the buyer. Later, the buyer cancels the sale (iqalah) and the seller accepts. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, if the cancellation involves a different price (more or less), then it is considered a new purchase, so the oath is broken. But if the cancellation is exactly as the original sale (same price and terms), then it is not a new purchase but a return to the previous state, so the oath is not broken. Abu Hanifa holds that all iqalah is a cancellation, not a new purchase, so the oath is not broken regardless of the price difference. The established position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Third case: if someone swears he bought nothing today but actually bought via ta'ati (mutual consent without formal contract), some say he breaks the oath. However, from Majmu' al-Nawazil and Quduri, the preferred view is that ta'ati is not a sale in the legal sense, so he does not break his oath, and witnesses should not testify that it is a sale. Al-Maturidi agreed. Fourth: buying a cut, unsewn shirt does not break an oath about buying a 'shirt' because it is not complete. Fifth: if the condition is that the slave sells to 'anyone' and he sells to two people, the oath is broken because 'anyone' is general. Similarly, if the condition is that 'anyone eats this loaf' and two eat, the oath is broken.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqalah): Not used in this lesson. Refer to lesson 2.
تعاطي (ta'ati): Not used in this lesson. Refer to lesson 3.
شراء (shira'): Purchase: a contract of sale transferring ownership of a specific item.
إجارة (ijarah): Hiring or leasing: a contract for usufruct, not ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, when does a wife break an oath conditioned on her purchase?
A) Whenever she obtains the item, regardless of method.
B) Only if she enters into a valid sale contract herself.
C) Only if she pays the full price upfront.
D) If she asks someone else to buy it for her.

Key Takeaway
An oath on 'purchase' is broken only by entering into a valid sale contract, not by indirect acquisition through hiring or agency.

Iqalah and Oaths: Does Cancellation with Price Difference Constitute a New Purchase?
Ruling on oath broken by iqalah with price difference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on iqalah (cancellation of sale) to oaths, distinguishing between the position of Abu Hanifa and that of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Core Idea
Iqalah with a different price is a new sale according to the Sahibayn but always a cancellation according to Abu Hanifa.

This lesson deals with a scenario where a man sells a slave, then swears not to buy that slave again from the buyer. Later, the buyer cancels the sale (iqalah) but at a different price. The central question: is this iqalah considered a new purchase that breaks the oath, or a mere cancellation? The Hanafi school has an internal ikhtilaf on this point.

Guided Path
1
Understand the factual scenario
A sells slave to B, then swears not to buy that slave from B. B later cancels the sale (iqalah) and A accepts, but the terms differ (different price).
The oath is 'not to buy' – the iqalah is a mutual rescission.
2
Identify the legal nature of iqalah
Iqalah in Hanafi fiqh is generally a cancellation (faskh), returning the parties to pre-sale state. However, when the price is different, some scholars consider it a new sale.
Abu Hanifa holds it is always faskh; the Sahibayn distinguish based on price change.
3
Apply the Sahibayn's view
If the iqalah involves a different price (more or less), it is a new purchase, so the oath is broken. If the price and terms are identical, it is still a cancellation.
The text says 'qila' this is their saying.
4
Apply Abu Hanifa's view
Abu Hanifa considers any iqalah, regardless of price change, as cancellation. Thus, no new purchase occurs, and the oath is not broken.
His reasoning: iqalah is essentially a return of the item, not a fresh contract.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the school is that of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: if the price changes, it is a new purchase and breaks the oath.
The text indicates this is the established view for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية : ولو قال لامرأته إن اشتريت شيئاً فأنت طالق فاشترت الماء قالوا: إن اشترته في قربة أو جرة طلقت وإن دفعت الجرة إلى السقاء وخبزاً حتى يحمل لها الماء لا تطلق. ولو باع عبده من رجل وسلم إلى المشتري ثم حلف البائع أن لا يشتريه من فلان ثم إن المشتري أقال البيع وقبل البائع الإقالة لا يحنث، ولو كان الثمن ألف درهم فوقعت الإقالة بمائة دينار أو بأكثر من الثمن الأول أو أقل حنث. قيل : هذا قولهما. وأما على قول أبي حنيفة لا يحنث لكونه إقالة على كل حال على ما عرف ولو حلف وقال والله ما اشتريت اليوم شيئاً وقد كان اشترى في ذلك اليوم أشياء لكن بالتعاطي فقد قيل يحنث في يمينه. وفي مجموع النوازل: وضع المسألة في طرف المبيع فقال إذا حلف لا الخبز فجاء رجل فأعطاه يبيع دراهم لأجل الخبز ودفع هو إليه الخبز لا يحنث وذكر في شهادات القدوري ما يؤيد ما ذكر في مجموع النوازل فقال : لا يسع لمن عاين ذلك أن يشهد على البيع بل يشهد على التعاطي. وإلى هذا مال الماتريدي. ولو حلف لا يشتري قميصاً فاشترى قميصاً مقطعاً غير مخيط لا يحنث. ولو قال إن بعث غلامي هذا أحدا من الناس فامرأته كذا فباعه من رجلين حنث، وكذا إذا قال إن أكل هذا الرغيف أحد فأكله اثنان حنث في يمينه.

Similarly, if the condition is that 'anyone eats this loaf' and two eat, the oath is broken.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqalah): Cancellation of a sale by mutual agreement; if done at a different price, it may be considered a new sale according to the Sahibayn.
فك العقد (faskh al-'aqd): Rescission of a contract, returning parties to pre-contract state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi position, when does acceptance of iqalah break an oath not to buy the item?
A) Always, because iqalah is a purchase.
B) Only when the iqalah is for the same price.
C) Only when the iqalah involves a different price than the original.
D) Never, because iqalah is a cancellation.

Key Takeaway
Iqalah with a different price is considered a new purchase according to the Sahibayn, and this is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Ta'ati: Informal Transactions and Oaths on Purchase
Ruling on oaths and testimony regarding mutual consent transactions (ta'ati)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a transaction by mutual consent (ta'ati) is not considered a sale for the purpose of oaths and testimony in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Ta'ati is a valid transaction but not a 'sale' that breaks an oath or qualifies for testimonial evidence.

This lesson addresses transactions conducted by mutual consent without formal offer and acceptance (ta'ati). While such transactions are valid and enforceable, Hanafi jurists distinguish them from formal sales when it comes to oaths and court testimony. The passage from Majmu' al-Nawazil and al-Quduri clarifies that a person who swears not to 'buy' bread does not break his oath if he obtains it through ta'ati, and witnesses should not testify that a sale occurred.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and the transaction
A man swears 'I did not buy anything today' when he actually obtained items through ta'ati (mutual exchange without formal contract). The question is whether this breaks his oath.
Ta'ati means handing over goods and price without verbal offer/acceptance.
2
Understand the Hanafi definition of sale (bay')
Bay' in the strict sense requires offer and acceptance (ijab wa qabul) expressed verbally or by conduct indicating exchange of ownership. Ta'ati is a valid contract but not a 'sale' in the narrow meaning for oaths.
The text from al-Quduri: witnesses should not testify to a sale but to ta'ati.
3
Apply to the bread scenario
If a man swears not to buy bread, and someone gives him dirhams in exchange for bread without formal words, he has not 'bought' it in the legal sense that breaks the oath.
The oath is interpreted according to the common legal meaning.
4
Analyze the testimony implication
A witness who observes such an exchange should not testify that a sale occurred, but should describe it as ta'ati. This protects the legal significance of the distinction.
Al-Maturidi agreed with this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية : ولو قال لامرأته إن اشتريت شيئاً فأنت طالق فاشترت الماء قالوا: إن اشترته في قربة أو جرة طلقت وإن دفعت الجرة إلى السقاء وخبزاً حتى يحمل لها الماء لا تطلق. ولو باع عبده من رجل وسلم إلى المشتري ثم حلف البائع أن لا يشتريه من فلان ثم إن المشتري أقال البيع وقبل البائع الإقالة لا يحنث، ولو كان الثمن ألف درهم فوقعت الإقالة بمائة دينار أو بأكثر من الثمن الأول أو أقل حنث. قيل : هذا قولهما. وأما على قول أبي حنيفة لا يحنث لكونه إقالة على كل حال على ما عرف ولو حلف وقال والله ما اشتريت اليوم شيئاً وقد كان اشترى في ذلك اليوم أشياء لكن بالتعاطي فقد قيل يحنث في يمينه. وفي مجموع النوازل: وضع المسألة في طرف المبيع فقال إذا حلف لا الخبز فجاء رجل فأعطاه يبيع دراهم لأجل الخبز ودفع هو إليه الخبز لا يحنث وذكر في شهادات القدوري ما يؤيد ما ذكر في مجموع النوازل فقال : لا يسع لمن عاين ذلك أن يشهد على البيع بل يشهد على التعاطي. وإلى هذا مال الماتريدي. ولو حلف لا يشتري قميصاً فاشترى قميصاً مقطعاً غير مخيط لا يحنث. ولو قال إن بعث غلامي هذا أحدا من الناس فامرأته كذا فباعه من رجلين حنث، وكذا إذا قال إن أكل هذا الرغيف أحد فأكله اثنان حنث في يمينه.
In al-Zahiriyyah: If a man said to his wife, 'If you buy something, you are divorced,' and she bought water—they said: If she bought it in a water skin or a jar, she is divorced; but if she gave the jar to the water carrier and bread so that he carries water for her, she is not divorced. And if a man sold his slave to a man and handed him over to the buyer, then the seller swore that he would not buy him from so-and-so, and then the buyer cancelled the sale (iqalah) and the seller accepted, he does not break his oath. But if the price was one thousand dirhams and the cancellation occurred for one hundred dinars, or more or less than the original price, he breaks the oath. It was said: This is their saying (the two disciples). As for the saying of Abu Hanifa, he does not break the oath because it is a cancellation in any case, as is known. And if he swore and said, 'By Allah, I did not buy anything today,' and he had bought things that day but through mutual transaction (ta'ati), it was said that he breaks his oath. In Majmu' al-Nawazil: The question was placed on the side of the sold item: if he swore not to buy bread, and a man came and gave him dirhams for the bread and he gave him the bread, he does not break his oath; and it is mentioned in Shihadat al-Quduri what supports this: 'It is not permissible for the one who witnesses that to testify on the sale, but rather he testifies on the transaction (ta'ati).' Al-Maturidi agreed with this. And if he swore not to buy a shirt and bought a cut, unsewn shirt, he does not break his oath. And if he said, 'If my slave sells to anyone among the people, then my wife is such,' and he sold to two men, he breaks his oath. Similarly, if he said, 'If anyone eats this loaf,' and two ate it, he breaks his oath.
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage collects several rulings from al-Zahiriyyah and other works on when an oath is broken. The first case: a man tells his wife 'If you buy something, you are divorced.' If she buys water in a container (skin or jar), she is divorced because she has directly purchased the water. But if she hires a water carrier by giving him a jar and bread to bring water, she has not bought the water herself, so she is not divorced. The distinction is based on who actually makes the purchase. Second case: a man sells a slave, then swears not to buy that slave from the buyer. Later, the buyer cancels the sale (iqalah) and the seller accepts. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, if the cancellation involves a different price (more or less), then it is considered a new purchase, so the oath is broken. But if the cancellation is exactly as the original sale (same price and terms), then it is not a new purchase but a return to the previous state, so the oath is not broken. Abu Hanifa holds that all iqalah is a cancellation, not a new purchase, so the oath is not broken regardless of the price difference. The established position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Third case: if someone swears he bought nothing today but actually bought via ta'ati (mutual consent without formal contract), some say he breaks the oath. However, from Majmu' al-Nawazil and Quduri, the preferred view is that ta'ati is not a sale in the legal sense, so he does not break his oath, and witnesses should not testify that it is a sale. Al-Maturidi agreed. Fourth: buying a cut, unsewn shirt does not break an oath about buying a 'shirt' because it is not complete. Fifth: if the condition is that the slave sells to 'anyone' and he sells to two people, the oath is broken because 'anyone' is general. Similarly, if the condition is that 'anyone eats this loaf' and two eat, the oath is broken.

Key Terms
تعاطي (ta'ati): A transaction by mutual consent without formal offer and acceptance; not considered a full sale for oath purposes.
شهادة (shahada): Testimony in court; for ta'ati, the witness should not testify 'sale' but describe the act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view in this passage, how should a witness describe a transaction conducted by ta'ati?
A) As a sale (bay') because ownership transferred.
B) As a gift (hibah) because no formal contract.
C) As a transaction (ta'ati) and not as a sale.
D) As a loan because it is informal.

Key Takeaway
Ta'ati is a valid transaction but does not constitute a 'sale' for the purpose of oaths or testimony in Hanafi fiqh.

Does Hibah Bi Shart al-'Iwad Break an Oath Not to Sell?
Ruling on whether a conditional gift breaks an oath not to sell

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether hibah bi shart al-'iwad breaks an oath not to sell and explain the legal reasoning that classifies it as a sale.

Core Idea
A gift with a required compensation is legally a sale; it breaks an oath not to sell.

Oaths are common in daily transactions, and their validity depends on precise legal classification. One tricky case is a gift that requires something in return (hibah bi shart al-'iwad). Does such a transaction violate an oath not to sell? The Hanafi school, as explained in al-Bahr al-Raiq, treats this as a sale in effect, so the oath is broken. In this lesson, we unpack the reasoning and explore related cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key transaction and oath
Start by identifying the oath sworn (e.g., 'I will not sell') and the transaction performed (gift with condition of compensation).
The oath is about selling; the gift is the act in question.
2
Classify the transaction under Hanafi law
Determine the true legal nature of hibah bi shart al-'iwad. In Hanafi fiqh, it is considered a sale because compensation is required, even if called a gift.
The initial form is a gift, but the final effect is a sale.
3
Apply the oath to the classified transaction
Since the transaction is legally a sale, it falls under the oath not to sell, so the swearer breaks the oath.
The text says 'ينبغي أن يحنث' (it is proper that he breaks the oath).
4
Distinguish from other transactions
Note that a pure gift without compensation does not break the oath, nor does a fictitious sale (bay' al-talji'ah).
The oath is interpreted based on the reality of the transaction.
5
Consider the practical consequence (thamarah)
If the oath was accompanied by a penalty (e.g., expiation), breaking it triggers that requirement. Understand the legal effect.
Knowing this prevents accidental oath-breaking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي القنية : حلف لا يبيع فوهب بشرط العوض ينبغي أن يحنث . باع جاريته ثم قال إن دخلت هي في بيعي فهي حرة فإن ردت عليه بغير قضاء تعتق وإلا فلا حلف إن اشتراها يحنث بالإقالة حلف لا ببيع التلجئة اهـ. وعلى هذا فالهبة بشرط العوض داخلة تحت يمين لا نظراً إلى أنها يهب هبة ابتداء فيحنث، وداخلة تحت يمين لا يبيع نظراً إلى أنها بيع انتهاء فيحنث بها. ولو قال إن أجرت داري هذه فهي صدقة ثم احتاج إلى إجارتها فالمخرج له عن اليمين أن يبيعها الحالف من غيره ثم يوكل المشتري الحالف بالإجارة فيؤاجرها بعد القبض ثم يشتريها فتخرج عن يمينه بالإجارة على ملك المشتري .اهـ. وقد يقال لا حاجة إلى هذا التكليف لأنه لو وكل في إجارتها لا يحنث فكذا لا يلزمه التصدق بها إلا أن يفرق بين النذر واليمين، وسيأتي الفرق بين ضرب الولد وضرب الغلام وفي الذخيرة : حلف لا يؤجر وله مستغلات آجرتها امرأته . الديانة فتأمل
And in al-Qunyah: If he swore not to sell and then gave a gift with a condition of compensation, it is proper that he breaks the oath. He sold his slave girl then said, 'If she enters into my sale, she is free' – if she is returned to him without a court order, she becomes free, otherwise not. He swore not to buy her; he breaks the oath by cancellation. He swore not to sell by tadlisah. End. According to this, the gift with a condition of compensation is included under an oath not to give a gift, because initially it is a gift, so he breaks the oath; and it is also included under an oath not to sell, because ultimately it is a sale, so he breaks the oath by it. And if he said, 'If I lease this house of mine, it becomes charity,' and then he needed to lease it, the way out of the oath is for the swearer to sell it to someone else, then appoint the buyer as his agent for leasing, so the buyer leases it after taking possession, then the swearer buys it back. Thus he escapes his oath regarding leasing on the property of the buyer. End. And it may be said: There is no need for this effort, because if he appoints an agent for leasing, he does not break the oath, so he is not obligated to give it in charity, unless there is a difference between a vow and an oath. And the difference between hitting a child and hitting a slave will come later. And in al-Dhakhirah: He swore not to lease, and he had rental properties; his wife leased them – in religious matters, consider.
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage from al-Qunyah deals with oaths regarding selling and related transactions. The first ruling: if a man swears not to sell, and then makes a gift (hibah) with a condition that the recipient gives something in return (hibah bi shart al-'iwad), he breaks his oath. This is because such a gift is legally considered a sale in effect, even though it starts as a gift. The text then discusses a case where a man sells his slave girl and then says, 'If she enters my sale, she is free.' The slave girl might be returned to him via iqalah (cancellation). If she is returned without a court order (i.e., by mutual agreement), she becomes free. But if returned by court decree, she does not become free. If he swore not to buy her, the cancellation breaks his oath. The text also mentions 'بيع التلجئة' (a fictitious sale to protect property), but this does not break an oath not to sell. Then the author summarizes: hibah bi shart al-'iwad is included under both an oath not to give (because initially it is a gift) and an oath not to sell (because ultimately it is a sale), so it breaks both types of oaths. Next, a hila (legal stratagem) is presented to avoid an oath about leasing: if someone swears that if he leases his house, it becomes charity (sadaqah), and he later needs to lease it, he can sell it to someone else, then appoint that buyer as his agent to lease it, then after the lease, he buys it back. By doing so, he does not lease it himself, so his oath is not broken. The commentator notes that this might be unnecessary because if he appoints an agent to lease, he is not directly leasing, so the oath is not broken anyway, unless there is a distinction between a vow (nadhr) and an oath (yamin) that will be explained later. Finally, a reference from al-Dhakhirah: if a man swears not to lease, but his wife leases his property, the ruling depends on religious considerations (i.e., his intention).

Key Terms
الهبة بشرط العوض (al-hibah bi shart al-iwad): A gift given with the condition that the recipient gives something in return; considered a sale in Hanafi law.
بيع التلجئة (bay' al-talji'ah): A fictitious sale made to avoid something (e.g., to keep property away from creditors); not a real sale.
الإقالة (al-iqalah): Cancellation of a sale by mutual agreement; also treats the sale as if it never occurred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man swears not to sell and then gives a gift with a condition of compensation (hibah bi shart al-'iwad), does he break his oath according to the Hanafi school?
A) No, because it is a gift.
B) Yes, because it is considered a sale in effect.
C) No, unless the compensation is monetary.
D) Yes, but only if the gift is valuable.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a gift with a condition of compensation is treated as a sale and thus breaks an oath not to sell.

Rulings on Oaths Regarding Buying and Selling
Rulings on oaths in sales, focusing on the legal nature of transactions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for determining when an oath about buying or selling is broken, considering the literal wording and the legal nature of the transaction.

Core Idea
The ruling on an oath depends on the substance (mufrad) and the legal nature of the transaction, not merely the label.

This passage presents a series of concrete examples illustrating how Hanafi jurists determine whether an oath related to buying or selling has been violated. The key principle is that the oath is interpreted according to what the words commonly signify (urf) and the underlying legal reality. For instance, an oath not to buy linseed is broken by buying linseed oil because the oil is derived from linseed. Similarly, a gift with a condition of compensation is treated as a sale and thus breaks an oath not to sell. These cases demonstrate the Hanafi method of ta'lil (legal reasoning) applied to oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and the disputed transaction
Read each scenario and clearly state what the person swore and what act they later performed. For example, 'swore not to buy linseed' and 'bought linseed oil'.
The exact wording of the oath matters but the legal nature of the subsequent act is key.
2
Determine the substance (mufrad) of the oath
Identify the core item or action referred to by the oath. Is the subsequent transaction essentially that item or action? For instance, linseed oil is derived from linseed, so it falls under the oath.
Hanafi law looks at the true substance, not just the name.
3
Classify the legal nature of the subsequent transaction
Determine whether the transaction is a sale, gift, cancellation, etc., according to Hanafi definitions. For example, a gift with a condition of compensation is legally a sale.
The legal classification controls the ruling on the oath.
4
Check for any exception or nuance (e.g., ta'ati, iqala)
Note any special rules: mutual delivery (ta'ati) may not count as a sale for oath purposes; cancellation (iqala) may or may not break an oath depending on the price change and the imam's view.
Abu Hanifa and the two disciples differ on iqala, so the mufta bihi position is that of the latter when the price changes.
5
State the ruling: break or no break
Apply the above analysis to conclude whether the oath is broken. If broken, expiation (kaffarah) is required; otherwise, no sin.
The passage consistently applies the principle that the oath is judged by the reality of the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يشمه فهو على الدهن والورق، وأما الحناء والورد فهو على الورق دون الدهن. ولو حلف لا يشتري بزراً فاشترى دهن بزر حنث، وإن اشترى حباً لم يحنث اهـ .
وفي الظهيرية : ولو قال لامرأته إن اشتريت شيئاً فأنت طالق فاشترت الماء قالوا: إن اشترته في قربة أو جرة طلقت وإن دفعت الجرة إلى السقاء وخبزاً حتى يحمل لها الماء لا تطلق. ولو باع عبده من رجل وسلم إلى المشتري ثم حلف البائع أن لا يشتريه من فلان ثم إن المشتري أقال البيع وقبل البائع الإقالة لا يحنث، ولو كان الثمن ألف درهم فوقعت الإقالة بمائة دينار أو بأكثر من الثمن الأول أو أقل حنث. قيل : هذا قولهما. وأما على قول أبي حنيفة لا يحنث لكونه إقالة على كل حال على ما عرف ولو حلف وقال والله ما اشتريت اليوم شيئاً وقد كان اشترى في ذلك اليوم أشياء لكن بالتعاطي فقد قيل يحنث في يمينه. وفي مجموع النوازل: وضع المسألة في طرف المبيع فقال إذا حلف لا الخبز فجاء رجل فأعطاه يبيع دراهم لأجل الخبز ودفع هو إليه الخبز لا يحنث وذكر في شهادات القدوري ما يؤيد ما ذكر في مجموع النوازل فقال : لا يسع لمن عاين ذلك أن يشهد على البيع بل يشهد على التعاطي. وإلى هذا مال الماتريدي. ولو حلف لا يشتري قميصاً فاشترى قميصاً مقطعاً غير مخيط لا يحنث. ولو قال إن بعث غلامي هذا أحدا من الناس فامرأته كذا فباعه من رجلين حنث، وكذا إذا قال إن أكل هذا الرغيف أحد فأكله اثنان حنث في يمينه. وفي القنية : حلف لا يبيع فوهب بشرط العوض ينبغي أن يحنث . باع جاريته ثم قال إن دخلت هي في بيعي فهي حرة فإن ردت عليه بغير قضاء تعتق وإلا فلا حلف إن اشتراها يحنث بالإقالة حلف لا ببيع التلجئة اهـ. وعلى هذا فالهبة بشرط العوض داخلة تحت يمين لا نظراً إلى أنها يهب هبة ابتداء فيحنث، وداخلة تحت يمين لا يبيع نظراً إلى أنها بيع انتهاء فيحنث بها.
If he swears not to smell something, it applies to oil and paper, but for henna and rose it applies to paper without oil. If he swears not to buy linseed and buys linseed oil, he breaks his oath; if he buys seeds, he does not. If he swears, 'By Allah, I bought nothing today,' and he had bought things that day but by mutual delivery (ta'ati), it is said he breaks his oath. It is mentioned in Shihadat al-Quduri what supports that: it is not permissible for one who witnesses that to testify to a sale, but rather to testify to mutual delivery. Al-Maturidi inclined to this. If he swears not to buy a shirt and buys a cut, unsewn shirt, he does not break his oath. If he says, 'If this slave of mine sends anyone from the people,' then his wife is [divorced], and he sells him to two men, he breaks his oath. Similarly, if he says, 'If anyone eats this loaf,' and two eat it, he breaks his oath. In al-Qunyah: If he swears not to sell and gives a gift with a condition of compensation, it is appropriate that he breaks his oath. He sold his slave-girl and then said, 'If she enters into my sale, she is free.' If she is returned to him without a court ruling, she becomes free; otherwise not. If he swears 'If I buy her' he breaks his oath by cancellation. He swears not to sell by collusion. So a gift with a condition of compensation falls under an oath not to give a gift, because initially it is a gift, so he breaks his oath; and also falls under an oath not to sell, because ultimately it is a sale, so he breaks his oath by it.

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings on oaths related to buying and selling, drawn from multiple Hanafi sources. The author presents specific scenarios to illustrate when an oath is broken based on the literal wording and the underlying legal nature of the transaction. The first ruling distinguishes between buying oil (which breaks an oath not to buy linseed) vs. buying seeds. The second discusses a wife's purchase of water via an agent, showing that if she uses a middleman she avoids the oath. The third deals with cancellation (iqala) after an oath, where Abu Hanifa differs from the two disciples regarding whether cancellation is a new sale or a dissolution. The fourth addresses oaths about buying on a particular day, noting that informal exchange (ta'ati) may or may not break the oath. The fifth covers oaths about buying bread where the transaction is actually an exchange of dirhams for bread, not a sale. The sixth concerns a shirt that is cut but unsewn, which is not considered a shirt. The seventh covers oaths involving an agent sending someone. The eighth discusses a gift with compensation (hibah bi-shart al-iwad), which is treated as both a gift and a sale, thus breaking an oath not to do either. Throughout, the Hanafi school's reliance on the underlying intent and the specific wording of the oath is evident.

Key Terms
التعاطي (al-ta'ati): Mutual delivery without formal offer and acceptance, considered a valid sale in Hanafi law.
الإقالة (al-iqalah): Cancellation of a sale by mutual agreement; Hanafi view: not a new sale but a dissolution of the original contract.
الهبة بشرط العوض (al-hibah bi-shart al-iwad): A gift given with a condition that the recipient gives something in return; treated as a sale in effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He does not break his oath because oil is different from seeds.
B) He breaks his oath because the oil is derived from linseed.
C) It depends on his intention at the time of the oath.
D) He breaks his oath only if the oil is pressed from linseed grown on his own farm.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the ruling on an oath about buying or selling depends on the true substance of the matter and the legal nature of the transaction, not merely the words used.
