Oaths Concerning Gifts: Hibah, 'Umar, and Sadaqah
Rulings on oaths related to gifts, life grants, and charity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a specific gift-related action breaks an oath and state the ruling for each scenario presented in the passage.

Core Idea
Giving a gift through any means (agent, ratification) breaks an oath not to give, but charity (sadaqah) does not.

This lesson focuses on the first part of a longer passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, citing al-Zahiriyya. It details when an oath not to give a gift (hibah) or charity (sadaqah) is broken. The rulings cover various forms of gifting, including life grants ('umar), gifts of undivided property, and gifts given through agents or ratified from a third party. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for anyone who may swear such an oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and the action
Read the scenario: The oath is 'I will not give a gift to so-and-so.' Identify the action: what exactly did the person do?
For example, did he give an undivided gift, order someone else to give, or ratify a gift made by an unauthorized person?
2
Apply the ruling on direct gifts
If the gift is given directly (even undivided property) or through an agent, the oath is broken. Also, a life grant ('umar) is considered a gift for this purpose.
The text mentions 'hibah ghayr maqsumah' as valid and oath-breaking.
3
Consider indirect actions
If the person orders another to give the gift, or ratifies a gift made by a third party (fuduli), these actions also break the oath because they are deemed equivalent to giving personally.
Ratification (ijazah) makes the fuduli's act as if it were done by the principal.
4
Distinguish gift with compensation from pure gift
A gift given in exchange for something ('ala 'iwad) still breaks an oath not to give a gift. However, if the oath is specifically about 'hibah' and the action is 'sadaqah' (charity), it does not break the oath.
The intention and wording of the oath matter: sadaqah is not hibah in this context.
5
Synthesize the rule
Generally, any act that transfers ownership of a thing to the other person, whether directly, through an agent, or by ratification, breaks an oath not to give. But charity is an exception when the oath is specifically about gifts.
This principle helps in applying the rule to similar scenarios.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الهبة والصدقة ففي الظهيرية : حلف أن لا : يهب لفلان فوهب هبة غير مقسومة حنث، وكذلك الإعمار والنحل والإرسال إليه مع رسوله وصورة الإعمار أن يقول صاحب الدار لغيره هي لك ما دمت حياً فإذا مت ردت إلي ، وكذا لو أمر غيره حتى وهب حنث، وكذا لو أجاز هبة الفضولي عبده ولو حلف لا : لفلان فوهب على عوض حنث ولا يحنث يهب بالصدفة في يمين الهبة .اهـ. وأما القرض والاستقراض ففي الظهيرية : حلف لا يستقرض فاستقرض ولم يقرضه حنث . وأما الإعارة والاستعارة ففي الظهيرية : لو حلف لا . فبعث فلان وكيلاً إلى الحالف واستعاره فأعاره الحالف حنث، ولو حلف لا يستعير من فأردفه فلان على دابته فردفه لا يحنث اهـ . وفي الذخيرة : حلف لا يستعير من فلان شيئاً ينصرف يعير ثوبه فلاناً فلان شيئاً إلى كل موجود تصح إعارته وكان ذلك عيناً ينتفع به مع بقاء عينه، فإن دخل دار المحلوف عليه ليستقي من بئره فاستعار منه الرشا والدلو اختلف المشايخ فيه، قيل يحنث، وقيل لا لأنه لم تثبت يده عليهما لأنهما في يد صاحب الدار فلا يكون مستعيراً. وهذا إشارة إلى أن الإعارة لا تتم إلا بالتسليم وهذا هو الطريق فيما إذا أردفه على دابته فعلى قياس هذا التعليل إذا استعار منه الرشا والدلو من بئر ليس في ملك المحلوف عليه يحنث .اهـ. وقد زاد في الخانية أن من هذا القسم تسليم الشفعة والإذن فيحنث فيهما بالأمر أيضاً. وفي الظهيرية : حلف لا يسلم الشفعة فسكت ولم يخاصم حتى بطلت شفعته لا يحنث في يمينه، وإن وكل وكيلاً بالتسليم حنث . ولو حلف لا يأذن لعبده في التجارة فرآه يبيع ويشتري فسكت يصير العبد مأذونا له في التجارة ولا يحنث، وكذلك البكر إذا حلف أن لا تأذن في تزويجها فسكتت عند الاستيمار لا يحنث اهـ. وزاد الامام الاسبيجابي أن من هذا القسم النفقة فإذا حلف لا ينفق فوكل حنث . ولم يذكر المصنف الشركة . وفي الظهيرية : ولو حلف لا يعمل مع فلان في قصارة ففعل مع شريك فلان حنث، ولو عمل مع عبده المأذون لا يحنث لأن كل واحد من الشريكين يرجع بالعهدة على صاحبه ويصير الحالف عاملاً مع المحلوف عليه وإن كان عقد الشركة نفسه لا . يوجب الحقوق، أما العبد المأذون فلا يرجع بالعهدة على المولى فلا يصير الحالف شريكاً لمولاه . ولو حلف لا يشارك فلاناً في هذه البلدة ثم خرجا عنها وعقدا عقد الشركة ثم دخلاها وعملا فيها إن كان الحالف نوى في يمينه أن لا يعقد عقد الشركة في البلدة لا يحنث، وإن نوى أن لا يعمل بشركة فلان حنث . وإن دفع أحدهما إلى صاحبه مالاً مضاربة فهذا والأول سواء لأن المضاربة شركة في عرفنا . ولو حلف لا يشارك فلاناً فأخرج كل واحد منهما دراهمه واشتركا حنث الحالف خلطا أو لم يخلطا. ولو حلف لا يشارك فلاناً فشاركه بمال ابنه الصغير لا يحنث، ولو حلف لا يشارك فلاناً ثم إن الحالف دفع إلى رجل مالاً بضاعة وأمره أن يعمل فيه برأيه فشارك المدفوع إليه المال الرجل الذي حلف رب
As for gifts (hibah) and charity (sadaqah), in al-Zahiriyya: If he swore not to give a gift to so-and-so, and he gave a non-divided gift, he breaks his oath. Similarly for 'umar (life grant), nahl (gift), and sending with his messenger. The form of 'umar is that the owner of a house says to another: 'It is yours as long as you live; when you die it returns to me.' Similarly, if he ordered another to give the gift, he breaks his oath. Similarly, if he ratified the gift of a third party (fuduli) of his slave. And if he swore not to give a gift to so-and-so, and he gave a gift for compensation, he breaks his oath, but he does not break his oath by giving a gift in the sense of 'sadaqah' in the oath about gift. End. As for lending (qard) and borrowing, in al-Zahiriyya: If he swore not to borrow, and he borrowed and did not lend (to the lender), he breaks his oath. As for lending (i'arah) and borrowing, in al-Zahiriyya: If he swore not to lend his garment to so-and-so, and so-and-so sent an agent to the swearer and borrowed it, and the swearer lent it, he breaks his oath. And if he swore not to borrow from so-and-so something, and so-and-so mounted him on his animal and he mounted him, he does not break his oath. End. And in al-Dhakhira: If he swore not to borrow from so-and-so anything, it applies to every existing thing whose lending is valid, which is a chattel that can be used while its substance remains. If he entered the house of the one sworn against to draw water from his well, and borrowed a rope and bucket, the mashayikh differed: some said he breaks his oath, some said not, because he did not take possession of them since they were in the hand of the owner, so he is not a borrower. This indicates that lending is not complete except by delivery, and this is the way regarding the case of mounting on his animal; by analogy to this reasoning, if he borrowed the rope and bucket from a well not in the ownership of the one sworn against, he breaks his oath. End. And added in al-Khaniyya that from this section is the surrender of shuf'ah and permission; he breaks his oath by ordering them as well. And in al-Zahiriyya: If he swore not to surrender shuf'ah, and he remained silent and did not litigate until his shuf'ah right was lost, he does not break his oath. But if he appointed an agent to surrender, he breaks his oath. And if he swore not to permit his slave to trade, and he saw him buying and selling and remained silent, the slave becomes permitted to trade, and he does not break his oath. Similarly, if a virgin swore not to give permission for her marriage, and she remained silent when asked, she does not break her oath. End. And Imam al-Isbijabi added that from this section is spending (nafaqah): if he swore not to spend, and he appointed an agent, he breaks his oath. The author (of Kanz) did not mention partnership (shirkah). And in al-Zahiriyya: If he swore not to work with so-and-so in laundering, and he worked with so-and-so's partner, he breaks his oath. And if he worked with his authorized slave, he does not break his oath, because each of the two partners has recourse against the other, and the swearer becomes a worker with the one sworn against, even though the contract of partnership itself does not create rights. As for the authorized slave, he does not have recourse against the master, so the swearer does not become a partner of his master. And if he swore not to enter into partnership with so-and-so in this town, then they went out of it and made a partnership contract, then entered and worked in it: if the swearer intended by his oath not to make the partnership contract in the town, he does not break his oath; if he intended not to work with so-and-so's partnership, he breaks his oath. And if one of them gave money to the other as mudarabah, this is the same as the first case because mudarabah is considered a partnership in our usage. And if he swore not to enter into partnership with so-and-so, and each of them brought out his dirhams and they formed a partnership, the swearer breaks his oath whether they mixed the money or not. And if he swore not to enter into partnership with so-and-so, and he entered into partnership with him using the property of his minor son, he does not break his oath. And if he swore not to enter into partnership with so-and-so, then the swearer gave money to a man as a sleeping partnership (bida'ah) and ordered him to act on his own opinion, and the recipient of the money formed a partnership with the man against whom the oath was made, (then... incomplete).
Source pages p. 586

Commentary
This extensive passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, drawing from multiple sources (al-Zahiriyya, al-Dhakhira, al-Khaniyya), presents a series of rulings on oaths related to various financial and legal transactions. The common thread is determining whether an action (often done through an agent or by ratification) constitutes a breach of an oath. For gifts (hibah): an oath not to give is broken if the gift is given unconditionally, even through an agent, and also if a conditional gift like 'umar (life grant) is made. However, if the gift is actually a charity (sadaqah), it does not break an oath concerning gifts. For loans (qard): borrowing breaks an oath not to borrow. For lending (i'arah): lending an item directly or through an agent breaks an oath not to lend, but merely providing a ride (irdaf) is not considered lending and does not break the oath. There is a dispute about borrowing a rope and bucket from a well on the property of the one sworn against: some scholars say it breaks the oath, others say it does not because the items remain in the owner's possession until delivery. The passage also covers shuf'ah (pre-emption right), permission (idhn), spending (nafaqah), and partnership (shirkah). Key principle: remaining silent (sukut) when one has the right to object does not break an oath not to permit, but active delegation (tawkili) does. For partnerships, working with the partner of the one sworn against is like working with him directly and breaks the oath, but working with an authorized slave does not. The intention of the swearer plays a role in determining breach, especially regarding the location of the contract.

Key Terms
هبة غير مقسومة (hibah ghayr maqsumah): A gift of undivided property (e.g., a share in joint property), which is valid in Hanafi law.
الإعمار (al-i'mar): A life grant (usufruct) where the owner gives a property to another for the duration of the recipient's life, with reversion to the giver or his heirs upon the recipient's death.
الفضولي (al-fuduli): A third party who acts without authorization; his action is subject to ratification (ijazah) by the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following actions would NOT break an oath not to give a gift?
A) Giving an undivided gift.
B) Ordering another person to give the gift.
C) Ratifying a gift made by an unauthorized person.
D) Giving charity (sadaqah) instead of a gift.

Key Takeaway
An oath not to give a gift is broken by any act of transferring ownership, directly or through an agent, but giving charity is not considered a gift for oath purposes.

Oaths Concerning Loans and Borrowing (Qard and Istiqrad)
Ruling on oaths related to borrowing money.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when an oath not to borrow is broken according to Hanafi fatwa sources.

Core Idea
Borrowing money from someone breaks an oath not to borrow, even if you do not lend back.

This lesson covers the second section of the passage, concerning oaths related to loans (qard) and borrowing. The ruling is straightforward: if one swears not to borrow and then borrows, the oath is broken. Unlike lending (i'arah) where possession matters, borrowing is considered complete upon receiving the loaned item.

Guided Path
1
State the oath
The person swears: 'I will not borrow from so-and-so.' Identify the subject: borrowing (istiqrad).
2
Determine the action
The person actually borrows money (or something fungible) from that person. He does not lend anything back.
The text specifies 'استقرض ولم يقرضه' – he borrowed but did not lend to the other party.
3
Apply the ruling
According to al-Zahiriyya, this action breaks the oath. The simple act of borrowing suffices, even if there is no reciprocal lending.
The oath is broken upon taking the loan.
4
Contrast with other transactions
Note that for lending (i'arah), there is a nuance about possession and delivery. For borrowing (qard), the act of taking the loan is enough.
This is a key distinction between loan of money (qard) and loan of use (i'arah).

Commentary
This extensive passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, drawing from multiple sources (al-Zahiriyya, al-Dhakhira, al-Khaniyya), presents a series of rulings on oaths related to various financial and legal transactions. The common thread is determining whether an action (often done through an agent or by ratification) constitutes a breach of an oath. However, if the gift is actually a charity (sadaqah), it does not break an oath concerning gifts. For loans (qard): borrowing breaks an oath not to borrow. For lending (i'arah): lending an item directly or through an agent breaks an oath not to lend, but merely providing a ride (irdaf) is not considered lending and does not break the oath. There is a dispute about borrowing a rope and bucket from a well on the property of the one sworn against: some scholars say it breaks the oath, others say it does not because the items remain in the owner's possession until delivery. The passage also covers shuf'ah (pre-emption right), permission (idhn), spending (nafaqah), and partnership (shirkah). Key principle: remaining silent (sukut) when one has the right to object does not break an oath not to permit, but active delegation (tawkili) does. For partnerships, working with the partner of the one sworn against is like working with him directly and breaks the oath, but working with an authorized slave does not. The intention of the swearer plays a role in determining breach, especially regarding the location of the contract.

Key Terms
القرض (al-qard): A loan of fungible property (e.g., money) to be repaid with an equivalent.
الاستقراض (al-istiqrad): The act of borrowing (taking a loan).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears not to borrow from his neighbor. He then borrows $10 from his neighbor but gives back $10 the next day. According to al-Zahiriyya, has he broken his oath?
A) No, because he returned the money.
B) Yes, because he borrowed, regardless of return.
C) No, because the loan was small.
D) Yes, but only if he did not return it within a month.

Key Takeaway
Borrowing money from the person sworn against automatically breaks the oath, with no need for additional conditions.

Oaths Concerning Lending (I'arah) and Borrowing of Use
Rulings on oaths related to lending and borrowing for use, including a dispute on possession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when lending or borrowing an item for use breaks an oath, including the dispute among Hanafi mashayikh regarding possession.

Core Idea
Lending an item through an agent breaks an oath, but borrowing may require possession; the mashayikh differ on borrowing tools from the owner's property.

This lesson continues with oaths related to lending (i'arah) and borrowing for use. The passage from al-Zahiriyya and al-Dhakhira provides rulings on direct lending, lending via agent, and the disputed case of borrowing a rope and bucket from a well on the borrower's property. Understanding the role of possession (qabd) is key.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of loan
Determine whether the oath is about i'arah (loan of a non-consumable) or isti'arah (borrowing). Note that i'arah involves a chattel that remains intact after use.
The text defines i'arah as a thing whose lending is valid and which can be used while its substance remains.
2
Direct lending through agent
If the oath is not to lend to so-and-so, and so-and-so sends an agent who borrows, and the swearer lends it to the agent, the oath is broken.
The agent is considered an extension of the principal.
3
Borrowing a ride (irdaf)
If the oath is not to borrow from so-and-so, and so-and-so mounts the swearer on his animal (without lending the animal), the oath is not broken.
This is not considered borrowing (isti'arah) because the animal is not taken into possession.
4
Dispute on borrowing from the owner's well
If the swearer enters the house of the one sworn against to draw water and borrows a rope and bucket, the mashayikh differ: some say the oath is broken, others say not, because possession (qabd) is not established if the items remain in the owner's hand.
The second view argues that i'arah requires delivery; without actual possession, it is not borrowing.
5
Apply analogy
The text mentions that if the rope and bucket are borrowed from a well not in the owner's property, then on analogy the oath is broken because possession would be possible.
This clarifies that location and ownership affect the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الهبة والصدقة ففي الظهيرية : حلف أن لا : يهب لفلان فوهب هبة غير مقسومة حنث، وكذلك الإعمار والنحل والإرسال إليه مع رسوله وصورة الإعمار أن يقول صاحب الدار لغيره هي لك ما دمت حياً فإذا مت ردت إلي ، وكذا لو أمر غيره حتى وهب حنث، وكذا لو أجاز هبة الفضولي عبده ولو حلف لا : لفلان فوهب على عوض حنث ولا يحنث يهب بالصدفة في يمين الهبة .اهـ. وأما القرض والاستقراض ففي الظهيرية : حلف لا يستقرض فاستقرض ولم يقرضه حنث . وأما الإعارة والاستعارة ففي الظهيرية : لو حلف لا . فبعث فلان وكيلاً إلى الحالف واستعاره فأعاره الحالف حنث، ولو حلف لا يستعير من فأردفه فلان على دابته فردفه لا يحنث اهـ . وفي الذخيرة : حلف لا يستعير من فلان شيئاً ينصرف يعير ثوبه فلاناً فلان شيئاً إلى كل موجود تصح إعارته وكان ذلك عيناً ينتفع به مع بقاء عينه، فإن دخل دار المحلوف عليه ليستقي من بئره فاستعار منه الرشا والدلو اختلف المشايخ فيه، قيل يحنث، وقيل لا لأنه لم تثبت يده عليهما لأنهما في يد صاحب الدار فلا يكون مستعيراً. وهذا إشارة إلى أن الإعارة لا تتم إلا بالتسليم وهذا هو الطريق فيما إذا أردفه على دابته فعلى قياس هذا التعليل إذا استعار منه الرشا والدلو من بئر ليس في ملك المحلوف عليه يحنث .اهـ. وقد زاد في الخانية أن من هذا القسم تسليم الشفعة والإذن فيحنث فيهما بالأمر أيضاً. وفي الظهيرية : حلف لا يسلم الشفعة فسكت ولم يخاصم حتى بطلت شفعته لا يحنث في يمينه، وإن وكل وكيلاً بالتسليم حنث . ولو حلف لا يأذن لعبده في التجارة فرآه يبيع ويشتري فسكت يصير العبد مأذونا له في التجارة ولا يحنث، وكذلك البكر إذا حلف أن لا تأذن في تزويجها فسكتت عند الاستيمار لا يحنث اهـ. وزاد الامام الاسبيجابي أن من هذا القسم النفقة فإذا حلف لا ينفق فوكل حنث . ولم يذكر المصنف الشركة . وفي الظهيرية : ولو حلف لا يعمل مع فلان في قصارة ففعل مع شريك فلان حنث، ولو عمل مع عبده المأذون لا يحنث لأن كل واحد من الشريكين يرجع بالعهدة على صاحبه ويصير الحالف عاملاً مع المحلوف عليه وإن كان عقد الشركة نفسه لا . يوجب الحقوق، أما العبد المأذون فلا يرجع بالعهدة على المولى فلا يصير الحالف شريكاً لمولاه . ولو حلف لا يشارك فلاناً في هذه البلدة ثم خرجا عنها وعقدا عقد الشركة ثم دخلاها وعملا فيها إن كان الحالف نوى في يمينه أن لا يعقد عقد الشركة في البلدة لا يحنث، وإن نوى أن لا يعمل بشركة فلان حنث . وإن دفع أحدهما إلى صاحبه مالاً مضاربة فهذا والأول سواء لأن المضاربة شركة في عرفنا . ولو حلف لا يشارك فلاناً فأخرج كل واحد منهما دراهمه واشتركا حنث الحالف خلطا أو لم يخلطا. ولو حلف لا يشارك فلاناً فشاركه بمال ابنه الصغير لا يحنث، ولو حلف لا يشارك فلاناً ثم إن الحالف دفع إلى رجل مالاً بضاعة وأمره أن يعمل فيه برأيه فشارك المدفوع إليه المال الرجل الذي حلف رب
As for gifts (hibah) and charity (sadaqah), in al-Zahiriyya: If he swore not to give a gift to so-and-so, and he gave a non-divided gift, he breaks his oath. Similarly for 'umar (life grant), nahl (gift), and sending with his messenger. The form of 'umar is that the owner of a house says to another: 'It is yours as long as you live; when you die it returns to me.' Similarly, if he ordered another to give the gift, he breaks his oath. Similarly, if he ratified the gift of a third party (fuduli) of his slave. And if he swore not to give a gift to so-and-so, and he gave a gift for compensation, he breaks his oath, but he does not break his oath by giving a gift in the sense of 'sadaqah' in the oath about gift. End. As for lending (qard) and borrowing, in al-Zahiriyya: If he swore not to borrow, and he borrowed and did not lend (to the lender), he breaks his oath. As for lending (i'arah) and borrowing, in al-Zahiriyya: If he swore not to lend his garment to so-and-so, and so-and-so sent an agent to the swearer and borrowed it, and the swearer lent it, he breaks his oath. And if he swore not to borrow from so-and-so something, and so-and-so mounted him on his animal and he mounted him, he does not break his oath. End. And in al-Dhakhira: If he swore not to borrow from so-and-so anything, it applies to every existing thing whose lending is valid, which is a chattel that can be used while its substance remains. If he entered the house of the one sworn against to draw water from his well, and borrowed a rope and bucket, the mashayikh differed: some said he breaks his oath, some said not, because he did not take possession of them since they were in the hand of the owner, so he is not a borrower. This indicates that lending is not complete except by delivery, and this is the way regarding the case of mounting on his animal; by analogy to this reasoning, if he borrowed the rope and bucket from a well not in the ownership of the one sworn against, he breaks his oath. End. And added in al-Khaniyya that from this section is the surrender of shuf'ah and permission; he breaks his oath by ordering them as well. And in al-Zahiriyya: If he swore not to surrender shuf'ah, and he remained silent and did not litigate until his shuf'ah right was lost, he does not break his oath. But if he appointed an agent to surrender, he breaks his oath. And if he swore not to permit his slave to trade, and he saw him buying and selling and remained silent, the slave becomes permitted to trade, and he does not break his oath. Similarly, if a virgin swore not to give permission for her marriage, and she remained silent when asked, she does not break her oath. End. And Imam al-Isbijabi added that from this section is spending (nafaqah): if he swore not to spend, and he appointed an agent, he breaks his oath. The author (of Kanz) did not mention partnership (shirkah). And in al-Zahiriyya: If he swore not to work with so-and-so in laundering, and he worked with so-and-so's partner, he breaks his oath. And if he worked with his authorized slave, he does not break his oath, because each of the two partners has recourse against the other, and the swearer becomes a worker with the one sworn against, even though the contract of partnership itself does not create rights. As for the authorized slave, he does not have recourse against the master, so the swearer does not become a partner of his master. And if he swore not to enter into partnership with so-and-so in this town, then they went out of it and made a partnership contract, then entered and worked in it: if the swearer intended by his oath not to make the partnership contract in the town, he does not break his oath; if he intended not to work with so-and-so's partnership, he breaks his oath. And if one of them gave money to the other as mudarabah, this is the same as the first case because mudarabah is considered a partnership in our usage. And if he swore not to enter into partnership with so-and-so, and each of them brought out his dirhams and they formed a partnership, the swearer breaks his oath whether they mixed the money or not. And if he swore not to enter into partnership with so-and-so, and he entered into partnership with him using the property of his minor son, he does not break his oath. And if he swore not to enter into partnership with so-and-so, then the swearer gave money to a man as a sleeping partnership (bida'ah) and ordered him to act on his own opinion, and the recipient of the money formed a partnership with the man against whom the oath was made, (then... incomplete).
Source pages p. 586

Commentary
This extensive passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, drawing from multiple sources (al-Zahiriyya, al-Dhakhira, al-Khaniyya), presents a series of rulings on oaths related to various financial and legal transactions. The common thread is determining whether an action (often done through an agent or by ratification) constitutes a breach of an oath. For gifts (hibah): an oath not to give is broken if the gift is given unconditionally, even through an agent, and also if a conditional gift like 'umar (life grant) is made. However, if the gift is actually a charity (sadaqah), it does not break an oath concerning gifts. For loans (qard): borrowing breaks an oath not to borrow. For lending (i'arah): lending an item directly or through an agent breaks an oath not to lend, but merely providing a ride (irdaf) is not considered lending and does not break the oath. There is a dispute about borrowing a rope and bucket from a well on the property of the one sworn against: some scholars say it breaks the oath, others say it does not because the items remain in the owner's possession until delivery. The passage also covers shuf'ah (pre-emption right), permission (idhn), spending (nafaqah), and partnership (shirkah). Key principle: remaining silent (sukut) when one has the right to object does not break an oath not to permit, but active delegation (tawkili) does. For partnerships, working with the partner of the one sworn against is like working with him directly and breaks the oath, but working with an authorized slave does not. The intention of the swearer plays a role in determining breach, especially regarding the location of the contract.

Key Terms
الإعارة (al-i'arah): A loan of a non-consumable item (a 'ayn) that can be used while its substance remains.
الاستعارة (al-isti'arah): The act of borrowing a non-consumable item for use.
التسليم (al-taslim): Delivery of the borrowed item from the lender to the borrower.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage from al-Dhakhira, why do some mashayikh say that borrowing a rope and bucket from the well of the one sworn against does NOT break the oath?
A) Because the rope and bucket are not a valid lendable item.
B) Because the borrower did not take possession of them; they remained in the owner's hand.
C) Because the oath was about borrowing, not lending.
D) Because the borrower was a guest in the house.

Key Takeaway
In i'arah, the act of borrowing may require delivery and possession; borrowing items that remain under the owner's control may not break the oath according to some Hanafi scholars.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Oaths Concerning Permission (Idhn), Shuf'ah, and Spending
Rulings on oaths related to permission, pre-emption, and spending, emphasizing passive vs active acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between active and passive actions that break an oath not to permit, surrender shuf'ah, or spend, based on Hanafi fatwa sources.

Core Idea
Silence does not break an oath not to permit, but appointing an agent does.

This lesson covers additional rulings from al-Khaniyya and al-Zahiriyya on oaths concerning permission (idhn), pre-emption right (shuf'ah), and spending (nafaqah). The key principle is that remaining silent (sukut) when one has the right to object is not considered a breach, whereas active delegation (tawkili) is. This distinguishes between passive acquiescence and positive action.

Guided Path
1
Oath not to permit (idhn)
If a master swears not to permit his slave to trade, and then he sees the slave buying and selling and remains silent, the slave becomes permitted (by silent permission) but the master does not break his oath.
Silent permission is legally effective but does not violate the oath because it is not an active permit.
2
Oath not to permit marriage (for a virgin)
If a virgin swears not to permit her marriage, and she remains silent when asked, she does not break her oath. Silence is not considered permission for the purpose of the oath.
This applies even though in marriage, a virgin's silence is deemed consent.
3
Oath not to surrender shuf'ah
If a person swears not to surrender his right of pre-emption, and he remains silent and does not litigate until the right is lost, the oath is not broken.
Passively letting the right expire is not a surrender (taslim).
4
Active surrender/permitting breaks oath
If he appoints an agent to surrender shuf'ah, or orders someone to permit, the oath is broken.
Delegating the act is equivalent to doing it oneself.
5
Oath not to spend (nafaqah)
If a person swears not to spend, and he appoints an agent to spend on his behalf, the oath is broken.
Spending via agency is like spending directly.

Source Text

Commentary
This extensive passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, drawing from multiple sources (al-Zahiriyya, al-Dhakhira, al-Khaniyya), presents a series of rulings on oaths related to various financial and legal transactions. The common thread is determining whether an action (often done through an agent or by ratification) constitutes a breach of an oath. For gifts (hibah): an oath not to give is broken if the gift is given unconditionally, even through an agent, and also if a conditional gift like 'umar (life grant) is made. However, if the gift is actually a charity (sadaqah), it does not break an oath concerning gifts. For loans (qard): borrowing breaks an oath not to borrow. For lending (i'arah): lending an item directly or through an agent breaks an oath not to lend, but merely providing a ride (irdaf) is not considered lending and does not break the oath. There is a dispute about borrowing a rope and bucket from a well on the property of the one sworn against: some scholars say it breaks the oath, others say it does not because the items remain in the owner's possession until delivery. The passage also covers shuf'ah (pre-emption right), permission (idhn), spending (nafaqah), and partnership (shirkah). Key principle: remaining silent (sukut) when one has the right to object does not break an oath not to permit, but active delegation (tawkili) does. For partnerships, working with the partner of the one sworn against is like working with him directly and breaks the oath, but working with an authorized slave does not. The intention of the swearer plays a role in determining breach, especially regarding the location of the contract.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): Right of pre-emption entitling a co-owner or neighbor to buy property being sold to a third party.
الإذن (al-idhn): Permission, especially from a master to his slave to trade.
النفقة (al-nafaqah): Financial provision for family, especially wife and children.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'I will not surrender my right of shuf'ah.' He does nothing when the neighbor's house is sold, and the time for claiming shuf'ah passes. According to al-Zahiriyya, has he broken his oath?
A) Yes, because he allowed his right to lapse.
B) No, because he did not actively surrender it.
C) Yes, because his silence was equivalent to surrender.
D) No, because he can still claim later.

Key Takeaway
Passive inaction (silence) does not break an oath not to permit, surrender, or spend; only positive actions, including delegation, do.

Oaths Regarding Gifts and Charity
Rulings on when a sworn pledge not to give a gift is violated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which acts break an oath not to give a gift, and distinguish between gift (hibah) and charity (sadaqah) in the context of oaths.

Core Idea
An oath not to give a gift is broken by any valid gift transfer, including undivided shares and life grants, but not by charity.

When a person swears not to give a gift, the question arises whether certain transactions violate that oath. The Hanafi jurists in al-Zahiriyyah clarify that the oath is broken by acts that constitute a legitimate gift, while charity is treated differently. This lesson parses the key scenarios to help you apply the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath's subject
The oath is 'I will not give a gift (hibah) to X.' Understand that the scope is specific to gifts, not charity.
2
Recognize valid gift transactions
Any transaction that is considered a valid gift in Hanafi fiqh breaks the oath. Examples include: undivided gift, i'mar (life grant), nahl (gift), sending with a messenger, commanding another to give, ratifying a fuduli's gift, and giving for compensation.
3
Distinguish charity from gift
If the giver gives as charity (sadaqah), it does not break the oath because charity has a different legal nature (seeking reward).
4
Note the effect of compensation
Giving for compensation (e.g., in exchange for something) also breaks the oath, as the primary act is still a transfer of ownership regarded as a gift.
5
Apply the rule to new scenarios
For any given scenario, ask: Does this act constitute a valid gift (hibah) or charity? If hibah, the oath is broken; if sadaqah, it is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الهبة والصدقة ففي الظهيرية : حلف أن لا : يهب لفلان فوهب هبة غير مقسومة حنث، وكذلك الإعمار والنحل والإرسال إليه مع رسوله وصورة الإعمار أن يقول صاحب الدار لغيره هي لك ما دمت حياً فإذا مت ردت إلي ، وكذا لو أمر غيره حتى وهب حنث، وكذا لو أجاز هبة الفضولي عبده ولو حلف لا : لفلان فوهب على عوض حنث ولا يحنث يهب بالصدفة في يمين الهبة .اهـ.
As for gift and charity, in al-Zahiriyyah: If he swore not to give something to so-and-so, and he gave an undivided gift, he breaks his oath. Similarly, [giving] an i'mar (life grant), a nahl (gift), or sending to him with his messenger. The form of i'mar is that the owner of the house says to another, 'It is yours as long as you live; when you die it returns to me.' Similarly, if he commanded another so that he gave, he breaks his oath. Similarly, if he ratified the gift of a stranger (fuduli) regarding his slave. And if he swore not to give to so-and-so, and he gave for compensation, he breaks his oath. And he does not break his oath by giving by way of sadaqah (charity) in an oath concerning gift. End.
Source pages p. 586

Commentary
This passage from al-Zahiriyyah provides detailed rulings on oaths related to gifts (hibah) and charity (sadaqah). The key principle is that an oath not to give a gift is broken by any act that constitutes a valid gift. This includes: (1) giving an undivided share (e.g., a part of a shared property without partition) – such a gift is valid according to Hanafis? Actually the text says it breaks the oath. (2) an i'mar (life grant) where the recipient gets the property for life and it returns to the donor upon death – this is considered a gift. (3) a nahl (traditional gift) or sending a gift with a messenger. (4) commanding another person to give the gift – the oath-breaker is considered the giver. (5) ratifying a gift made by a fuduli (unauthorized agent) regarding his own slave – ratification makes it as if he gave it himself. However, if the oath was specifically not to give a gift (hibah), giving by way of charity (sadaqah) does not break the oath, because charity implies seeking reward while gift implies honouring. Also, giving for compensation (e.g., selling) breaks the oath not to give, because the compensation is not the primary purpose.

Key Terms
الهبة (al-hibah): A gratuitous transfer of ownership without compensation.
الإعمار (al-i'mar): A life grant: giving property to someone for their lifetime, with the condition that it returns to the donor upon the recipient's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person swears 'I will not give a gift to Zaid,' and then he gives Zaid a shirt in charity (sadaqah), does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because charity is a form of gift.
B) No, because charity is different from gift in the context of the oath.
C) Yes, if the shirt is valuable.
D) No, because charity cannot be given to a specific person.

Key Takeaway
An oath not to give a gift is violated by any valid gift transfer, including conditional or compensated transfers, but not by charity.

Oath Not to Borrow: The Ruling from al-Zahiriyyah
A single ruling from al-Zahiriyyah on oaths not to borrow.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a person swears not to borrow and then borrows, and identify the key elements that trigger the oath-breaking.

Core Idea
Borrowing itself breaks an oath not to borrow, regardless of prior lending.

Oaths concerning financial transactions can be subtle. This short ruling from al-Zahiriyyah settles a common scenario: a person swears 'I will not borrow' and then takes a loan. The ruling is clear – the act of borrowing (istiqrad) alone constitutes a breach, even if he had also lent money. Let's unpack the precise legal trigger.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath
What did the person swear? In the text, he swore 'لا يستقرض' (he will not borrow). The oath is about the act of borrowing, not about lending.
The oath is specific to istiqrad (borrowing), not qard (lending).
2
Identify the action taken
He then performed istiqrad: he took a loan (received fungible property with obligation to repay). This is the action that potentially breaks the oath.
The ruling states: 'فاستقرض' (then he borrowed).
3
Analyze the condition mentioned
The text adds 'ولم يقرضه' (and he had not lent to that person). This clarifies that the breach does NOT depend on whether he also lent; it is solely about the borrowing act.
The condition is irrelevant – borrowing alone suffices to break the oath.
4
Conclude the ruling
Since he borrowed, his oath is broken (حنث). He must perform kaffarah for his oath.
The consequence is that he becomes a hanith (oath-breaker).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القرض والاستقراض ففي الظهيرية : حلف لا يستقرض فاستقرض ولم يقرضه حنث .
As for loan and borrowing, in al-Zahiriyyah: If he swore not to borrow, and he borrowed (received a loan) without having lent (anything to that person), he breaks his oath.
Source pages p. 586

Commentary
This short ruling from al-Zahiriyyah addresses an oath not to borrow (istiqrad). If the person takes a loan (i.e., receives money with an obligation to repay), he breaks his oath. The phrase 'without having lent' clarifies that the oath-breaker is the borrower, not the lender. The ruling is straightforward: the act of borrowing itself, regardless of whether one also lends, constitutes a breach of an oath not to borrow.

Key Terms
القرض (al-qard): A loan of something fungible with the obligation to repay its equivalent.
الاستقراض (al-istiqrad): The act of borrowing (taking a loan).
حنث (hanitha): To break an oath; the state of being an oath-breaker.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not borrow from anyone' and then takes a loan of 100 dirhams from a friend, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because he performed 'istiqrad'.
B) No, because he did not lend to the friend.
C) Yes, if he did not intend to repay.
D) No, because an oath not to borrow only applies to borrowing from a stranger.

Key Takeaway
Borrowing (istiqrad) itself triggers the break of an oath not to borrow, regardless of whether one also lends.

Oaths and Lending: Lending via Proxy Breaks the Oath
Ruling on oaths and lending via agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when an oath not to lend is broken by lending through an agent and when an oath not to borrow is broken by accepting a ride.

Core Idea
Lending through an agent is like lending directly, but offering a ride is not a loan.

This lesson examines two specific rulings from al-Zahiriyyah regarding oaths about lending and borrowing. The first clarifies that using an agent to lend still breaks an oath not to lend, while the second shows that accepting a ride on someone's mount does not count as borrowing. Understanding these distinctions helps avoid unintended oath violations in daily interactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the first two rulings: one about swearing not to lend and then lending to a proxy, and the second about swearing not to borrow and then being given a ride.
Note the missing word in the Arabic; it is likely 'yu'ir' (lend).
2
Analyze the outcome for each
For the first, the verdict is 'yanuth' (he breaks the oath); for the second, 'la yanuth' (he does not break).
3
Understand the legal reasoning
Lending to a proxy is deemed lending because the proxy acts on behalf of the principal. A ride is not a loan of the mount itself; it is a mere accommodation that does not involve transferring possession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الإعارة والاستعارة ففي الظهيرية : لو حلف لا . فبعث فلان وكيلاً إلى الحالف واستعاره فأعاره الحالف حنث، ولو حلف لا يستعير من فأردفه فلان على دابته فردفه لا يحنث اهـ . وفي الذخيرة : حلف لا يستعير من فلان شيئاً ينصرف إلى كل موجود تصح إعارته وكان ذلك عيناً ينتفع به مع بقاء عينه، فإن دخل دار المحلوف عليه ليستقي من بئره فاستعار منه الرشا والدلو اختلف المشايخ فيه، قيل يحنث، وقيل لا لأنه لم تثبت يده عليهما لأنهما في يد صاحب الدار فلا يكون مستعيراً. وهذا إشارة إلى أن الإعارة لا تتم إلا بالتسليم وهذا هو الطريق فيما إذا أردفه على دابته فعلى قياس هذا التعليل إذا استعار منه الرشا والدلو من بئر ليس في ملك المحلوف عليه يحنث .اهـ. وقد زاد في الخانية أن من هذا القسم تسليم الشفعة والإذن فيحنث فيهما بالأمر أيضاً. وفي الظهيرية : حلف لا يسلم الشفعة فسكت ولم يخاصم حتى بطلت شفعته لا يحنث في يمينه، وإن وكل وكيلاً بالتسليم حنث . ولو حلف لا يأذن لعبده في التجارة فرآه يبيع ويشتري فسكت يصير العبد مأذونا له في التجارة ولا يحنث، وكذلك البكر إذا حلف أن لا تأذن في تزويجها فسكتت عند الاستيمار لا يحنث اهـ. وزاد الامام الاسبيجابي أن من هذا القسم النفقة فإذا حلف لا ينفق فوكل حنث . ولم يذكر المصنف الشركة .
As for lending (i'arah) and borrowing (isti'arah), in al-Zahiriyyah: If he swore not to [lend?] and then so-and-so sent a proxy to the oath-taker and borrowed, and the oath-taker lent, he breaks his oath. And if he swore not to borrow from so-and-so, and then so-and-so accommodated him on his mount, and he accommodated him, he does not break his oath. End. And in al-Dhakhirah: If he swore not to borrow anything from so-and-so, it applies to every existing thing whose lending is valid, and it is a tangible object that can be benefited from while its substance remains. If he entered the house of the one against whom the oath was made to draw water from his well, and borrowed the rope and bucket from him, the mashayikh differed: some said he breaks his oath, others said no, because his hand was not established over them, since they were in the hand of the owner of the house, so he is not a borrower. This indicates that lending is not complete except with delivery. And this is the way in the case when he accommodated him on his mount. By analogy to this reasoning, if he borrowed the rope and bucket from a well that is not in the ownership of the one against whom the oath was made, he breaks his oath. End. And in al-Khaniyah it added that from this category is the surrender of shuf'ah (preemption) and permission (idhn), and he breaks the oath by command (i.e., by ordering someone else to do it) as well. And in al-Zahiriyyah: If he swore not to surrender shuf'ah, and he remained silent and did not dispute until his right of shuf'ah lapsed, he does not break his oath. But if he appointed a proxy to surrender, he breaks his oath. And if he swore not to permit his slave to trade, and he sees him buying and selling and remains silent, the slave becomes permitted to trade, and he does not break his oath. Similarly, a virgin if she swore not to permit her marriage, and she remained silent when consulted, she does not break his oath. End. Imam al-Isbijabi added that from this category is maintenance (nafaqah): if he swore not to spend, and he appointed a proxy, he breaks his oath. And the author (al-Kanz) did not mention partnership (shirkah).
Source pages p. 586

Commentary
This longer passage covers oaths related to borrowing (i'arah) and extends to related actions. The first ruling: lending via an agent breaks an oath not to lend. The second ruling: being accommodated on someone's mount does not break an oath not to borrow, because it is not a loan of the mount but mere ride. Then al-Dhakhirah provides the general definition: the oath applies to any item that can be validly lent (i.e., a tangible thing whose benefit can be enjoyed while the substance remains). A dispute among scholars is mentioned about borrowing a rope and bucket from a well inside the oath-taker's property: some say the oath is broken, others say not because the borrower did not take possession (since the items remained in the owner's hand). This clarifies that i'arah requires delivery. Then al-Khaniyah adds that surrender of shuf'ah (pre-emption right) and permission (idhn) also break the oath if done via command. Finally, al-Zahiriyyah provides specific rulings: silence causing loss of shuf'ah does not break an oath; appointing a proxy does. Similarly, silent permission for a slave to trade or a virgin's marriage via silence does not break an oath. Al-Isbijabi adds nafaqah: appointing a proxy to spend breaks the oath. The section ends with a note that the author did not mention partnership.

Key Terms
الإعارة (al-i'arah): Lending an object for use without compensation, with the object returning to the owner.
الوكالة (al-wikalah): Agency; one person appoints another to act on his behalf in a legal capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the lesson, if a person swears not to borrow from Zaid, and Zaid gives him a ride on his horse, what is the ruling?
A) He breaks his oath because he benefited from the horse.
B) He does not break his oath because a ride is not a loan.
C) He breaks his oath because riding is a form of borrowing.
D) He does not break his oath only if the ride was short.

Key Takeaway
Lending through an agent breaks an oath not to lend, but providing a ride does not break an oath not to borrow.
